Ghazali's Ta'wil and the Metaphorical Meaning of 'Fawq'
Al-Ghazali affirms ta'wil and illustrates with the word 'fawq'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Ghazali defended God's transcendence by distinguishing literal and metaphorical meanings of the word 'above' (fawq).

Core Idea
Ta'wil is not denial but deeper understanding: 'above' can mean rank, not location.

In this passage, the author praises Al-Ghazali for boldly affirming ta'wil (figurative interpretation) of anthropomorphic verses. He then presents Ghazali's own analysis of the word 'fawq' (above) in two Quranic verses, showing that its literal sense implies corporeality, while its metaphorical sense conveys superiority of rank. This distinction is a cornerstone of defending tanzih (divine transcendence).

Guided Path
1
Understand the context of Ghazali's praise
Read why the author considers Ghazali's affirmation of ta'wil a good deed for Islam. This sets the stage for the importance of ta'wil in defending God's transcendence.
Notice the author's strong commendation; it shows the value placed on this method.
2
Analyze Ghazali's argument on 'fawq'
Examine how Ghazali distinguishes the literal meaning (spatial relation between bodies) from the metaphorical meaning (superiority of rank). Pay attention to the examples he gives.
The Quranic verses are key: 'above His servants' and 'above them'.
3
Reflect on the theological implication
Consider how this distinction protects God from being imagined as occupying physical space or having a body.
The literal meaning would imply corporeality, which is negated by the metaphorical interpretation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويعتقد أبو حامد الغزالي في التأويل ويصرح به، وهذا يدل على [فقهه] الصحيح في الله وصفاته، وتصريحه بالتأويل حسنة من حسناته (۳) على الإسلام ، بجزيه الله عليها خير الجزاء . يقول - رحمه الله - في [قوله] تعالى : ( وهو القاهر فوق عباده ) وفي قوله تعالى : ( يخافون ربهم من فوقهم ) : إن الفوق على الحقيقة نسبة جسم إلى جسم ، بأن يكون أحدهما أعلى والآخر أسفل . والفوق على المجاز يستعمل في فوقية الرتبة مثل : الخليفة فوق السلطان . والمعنى الحقيقي يدل على جسم ، والمعنى المجازي ينفي الجسمية .
And Abu Hamid al-Ghazali believes in interpretation and declares it, and this indicates his correct [understanding] regarding God and His attributes, and his declaration of interpretation is a good deed among his good deeds (3) upon Islam; may God reward him with the best reward for it. He says—may God have mercy on him—concerning [the saying] of God Almighty: 'And He is the Conqueror above His servants' and in His saying: 'They fear their Lord above them': Indeed 'above' in reality is a relation of a body to a body, such that one is higher and the other is lower. And 'above' metaphorically is used for superiority of rank, like 'the caliph is above the sultan'. The real meaning indicates a body, and the metaphorical meaning negates corporeality.
Source pages p. 275

Commentary
This passage notes that Al-Ghazali explicitly believes in and declares figurative interpretation (ta'wil) of anthropomorphic verses, and the author praises this as a good deed for Islam, asking God to reward him. Then it presents Ghazali's own explanation using the word 'above' (fawq). He distinguishes between the literal meaning of 'above' as a spatial relation between two bodies, and the metaphorical meaning of rank or authority, as when we say 'the caliph is above the sultan'. The literal sense would imply corporeality, while the metaphorical sense negates it. This is a key argument for defending God's transcendence against anthropomorphism.

Key Terms
التأويل (ta'wil)
الفوق (al-fawq)
الحقيقة (al-ḥaqīqah)
المجاز (al-majāz)
الجسمية (al-jismiyyah)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the word 'above' in the Quran always implies physical elevation of God.
Correction: Al-Ghazali shows that 'above' can be metaphorical for rank, as in 'the caliph is above the sultan', thereby negating the need for a physical location.

Checkpoint
According to Al-Ghazali, how is the word 'above' (fawq) used metaphorically in the Quran?
A) To indicate physical elevation of God.
B) To indicate superiority of rank, like a caliph above a sultan.
C) To indicate direction and location.
D) To negate God's existence.

Key Takeaway
Ta'wil allows believers to affirm God's attributes without corporeality, as demonstrated by interpreting 'fawq' metaphorically.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read Quranic verses that describe God in spatial terms (like 'above' or 'ascending'), do you feel a tension between your heart's devotion and your intellect's demand for transcendence? How does Ghazali's distinction between real and metaphorical meaning help you resolve that tension in your personal worship? Commit today to reciting one such verse and consciously applying the metaphorical understanding.
?
Have you ever avoided or felt uneasy about certain Quranic verses because they seem to describe God with human-like qualities? After this lesson, will you allow the method of ta'wil to transform your engagement with these verses? What is one practical step you will take to embrace this deeper understanding, even if it challenges your previous assumptions?

Ghazali's Ta'wil of 'Above': Metaphorical Not Literal
Ghazali presents and defends ta'wil of 'fawq' as metaphorical.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ghazali interprets the word 'fawq' (above) in Quranic verses as a metaphor for superiority in rank rather than a literal spatial relationship.

Core Idea
When Allah is 'above', it means authority, not location.

Abu Hamid al-Ghazali is a key figure who defended Islamic theology by interpreting ambiguous verses in a way that upholds Allah's transcendence. In this passage, he examines the verse 'He is the Irresistible above His servants' and distinguishes between literal and metaphorical meanings. He shows why the literal meaning implying corporeality must be rejected and what the correct metaphorical meaning entails.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic text and its literal translation to grasp Ghazali's own words.
Notice that Ghazali uses a clear distinction between 'haqiqah' and 'majaz'.
2
Identify the two meanings of 'fawq'
Find where Ghazali defines the literal and metaphorical senses. He says literal is a relation between bodies, metaphorical is superiority in rank.
3
Understand why literal meaning is impossible
Ghazali states that the literal meaning would imply Allah is a body or related to bodies, which is impossible (muhal). Reflect on why corporeality is incompatible with Allah's transcendence.
4
Connect to broader theology
Realize that this ta'wil is a method to preserve tanzih (transcendence) while affirming the Quranic text. Ghazali's approach is praised by the author as a good deed.
This lesson shows how kalam uses metaphor to avoid anthropomorphism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويعتقد أبو حامد الغزالى فى التأويل ويصرح به ، وهذا يدل على فته الصحيحة في الله وصفاته، وتصريحه بالتأويل حسنة من حسناته (۳) على الإسلام ، بجزيه الله عليها خير الجزاء . يقول - رحمه الله - في له تعالى : ( وهو القاهر فوق عباده ، وفى قوله تعالى : ( يخافون ربهم من فوقهم ، : : إن الفوق على الحقيقة نسبة جسم إلى جسم ، بأن يكون أحدهما أعلى والآخر أسفل . والفوق على المجاز يستعمل في فوقية الرتبة مثل : الخليفة فوق السلطان . والمعنى الحقيقى يدل على جسم ، والمعنى المجازى ينفي الجسمية . ويقول هذا مانصه : « فليعتقد المؤمن قطعا : أن الأول - وهو بعد المعنى الحقيقى - غير مراد ، وأنه على الله محال . فإنه من لوازم الأجسام ، أو لوازم أعراض الأجسام (١) ويقول أيضا - رحمه الله - : تأويل لفظ الفوق بالمعلو المعنوى، الذى هو المراد بقولنا : السلطان فوق الوزير . فإنا لا نشك فى ثبوت معناه الله تعالى (۲)
And Abu Hamid al-Ghazali believes in ta'wil and declares it, and this indicates his correct understanding of Allah and His attributes, and his declaration of ta'wil is a good deed among his good deeds for Islam, may Allah reward him for it generously. He says – may Allah have mercy on him – regarding His statement: 'And He is the Irresistible above His servants' (6:18), and His statement: 'They fear their Lord above them' (16:50): 'Indeed, "al-fawq" (the above) in the literal sense is a relation of a body to a body, such that one is higher and the other lower. And "al-fawq" in the metaphorical sense is used for superiority in rank, like the caliph is above the sultan. The literal meaning indicates a body, and the metaphorical meaning negates corporeality.' And he says, this is his text: 'Let the believer believe decisively that the first – which is the literal meaning – is not intended, and that it is impossible for Allah. For it is among the requirements of bodies or among the requirements of the accidents of bodies (1)'. And he also says – may Allah have mercy on him: 'The ta'wil of the word "al-fawq" with the intellectual metaphorical meaning, which is intended by our saying: the sultan is above the vizier. For we have no doubt about the establishment of its meaning for Allah Most High (2).'
Source pages p. 275

Commentary
This passage presents al-Ghazali's method of ta'wil (interpretation) for Quranic verses and his explicit commitment to it. The author praises this as a sign of Ghazali's correct understanding of Allah's transcendence. Ghazali then analyzes the word 'al-fawq' (above) as used in two verses. He distinguishes between the literal meaning (al-haqiqah) and the metaphorical meaning (al-majaz). Literally, 'above' denotes a spatial relationship between two bodies, which would imply corporeality for Allah. Metaphorically, it indicates superiority in rank, like the caliph's authority over the sultan. Ghazali insists that the literal meaning is not intended for Allah and is impossible (muhal) because it necessitates being either a body or an accident of a body. He then reinforces this with a second statement: the correct ta'wil is the metaphorical meaning of superiority, which is affirmed for Allah. The passage thus demonstrates Ghazali's commitment to tanzih (transcendence) by rejecting anthropomorphic interpretations.

Key Terms
التأويل (ta'wil)
الفوق (al-fawq)
الحقيقة (al-haqiqah)
المجاز (al-majaz)
الجسمية (al-jismiyyah)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The word 'above' when used of Allah must be taken in its literal spatial sense.
Correction: Ghazali argues that the literal sense implies corporeality, which is impossible for Allah; therefore, the correct interpretation is metaphorical, indicating superiority in rank.

Checkpoint
According to al-Ghazali, what does the literal meaning of 'fawq' imply about Allah?
A) It implies Allah is omnipotent.
B) It implies Allah is above in rank only.
C) It implies Allah is a body or related to bodies.
D) It implies Allah is not above anything.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali's ta'wil of 'fawq' as metaphorical superiority safeguards divine transcendence by rejecting any literal meaning that would imply corporeality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read that Allah is 'above' His servants, does a mental image of a direction or location form in your mind? How does Ghazali's ta'wil challenge that assumption, and what must you actively do to purge any trace of corporealism from your belief?
?
If you have ever thought of Allah as being literally up in the sky, how does that contradict the true meaning of tawhid? What specific step will you take today to replace that image with the correct understanding of Allah's transcendent authority?
?
Are you willing to submit to the discipline of ta'wil, even when it feels like you are 'interpreting away' a cherished literal understanding? What is the cost of refusing to do so for your faith in Allah's uniqueness?

Ghazali's Methodology for Apparent Verses
Ghazali presents rational proof and a hermeneutic methodology.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's methodology for dealing with apparent verses about Allah's attributes, and how he distinguishes the approach for commoners from that for scholars.

Core Idea
Ghazali protects commoners from theology and entrusts scholars with deeper interpretation.

In this passage, Imam al-Ghazali addresses a crucial question: how should Muslims understand verses and hadiths that seem to describe Allah in physical terms? He presents a clear distinction between what is required of the general public and what is appropriate for scholars, offering a practical methodology that safeguards faith while respecting intellectual depth.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the Problem
Start by understanding the dilemma: verses like 'al-Rahman 'ala al-arsh istawa' and the hadith of descent appear to suggest physical attributes. How should we handle them?
2
Study Ghazali's Rational Proof
Read the opening argument that Allah cannot be described as 'settling' (istiqrar) because that implies limitation and corporeality.
This is a kalam proof based on the nature of tamakkun.
3
See How He Handles the Objection
Notice that Ghazali does not dismiss the apparent meanings but offers a methodology (manhaj) as a response.
4
Distinguish the Two Groups
Identify the two categories: commoners ('awamm) and scholars ('ulama'). Understand what is appropriate for each.
For commoners: no ta'wil, but purification of beliefs. For scholars: deeper understanding is permitted but not obligatory.
5
Grasp the Core Obligation
Ghazali states that the only obligation (taklif) is tanzih – affirming transcendence – not understanding all meanings.
This is a key principle for dealing with ambiguous texts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن كلامه - رحمه الله - : ندعى أن الله تعالى منزه عن أن يوصف بالاستقرار على العرش . فإن كل متمكن على جسم، والمستقر عليه : مقدر لا محالة . فإنه إما أن يكون أكبر منه أو أصغر أو مساويا ... فإن قيل : فما معنى قوله تعالى : « الرحمن عنى العرش استوى ، وما معنى قوله عليه الصلاة والسلام : « ينزل الله كل ليلة إلى سماء الدنيا ؟ قلنا : الكلام على الظواهر الواردة في هذا الباب طويل . ولكن تذكر منهجا في هذين الظاهرين ، يرشد إلى ما عداه . وهو أنا نقول : الناس فى هذا فريقان : عوام وعلماء . والذي نراه هو اللائق بعو ام الخلق: هو أن لا يخاض بهم فى هذه التأويلات بل ننزع عن عقائدهم كل ما يوجب التشبيه ، ويدل على الحدوث، ونحقق عندهم أنه موجود و ليس كمثله شيء ، وهو السميع البصير ، ... و أما العلماء فاللائق بهم تعريف ذلك وتفهمه . ولست أقول: إن ذلك فرض عين - إذ لم يرد به تكليف - بل التكليف : هو التنزيه عن كل ما يشبهه بغيره . فأما معاني القرآن ، فلم يكلف الأعيان فهم جميعها أصلا (۳) ،
And among his words – may Allah have mercy on him: 'We claim that Allah Most High is transcendent above being described as settling on the Throne. For everything that is placed on a body, and that which it settles upon is necessarily measured. For it is either larger than it, smaller than it, or equal to it... So if it is said: What is the meaning of the statement of Allah Most High: "The Most Merciful has established Himself over the Throne" (20:5), and what is the meaning of his statement – peace be upon him – "Allah descends every night to the heaven of the world"? We say: The speech on the apparent meanings reported in this chapter is long. But we mention a methodology in these two apparent meanings, which guides to what else. And it is that we say: People in this matter are two groups: commoners and scholars. And what we see as befitting the commoners is that they should not be taken into these interpretations, but rather we remove from their beliefs everything that necessitates anthropomorphism and indicates temporality, and we make them certain that He exists, nothing is like Him, and He is the Hearing, the Seeing. ... As for the scholars, what befits them is defining it and understanding it. And I do not say that it is an individual obligation – for no such obligation has come – but rather the obligation is the transcendence from everything that resembles Him. As for the meanings of the Qur'an, the minds were not obligated to understand all of them at all (3).'
Source pages p. 276

Commentary
This passage presents al-Ghazali's own words from his book Al-Iqtisad, as cited by the author. It begins with a claim: Allah is transcendent, not to be described as settling (istiqrar) on the Throne. The reason given is that anything placed on a body (tamakkun) requires the place to be of a comparable size (larger, smaller, or equal), which implies limitation and corporeality. Then Ghazali anticipates an objection: what about the Quranic verse 'al-Rahman 'ala al-arsh istawa' and the hadith of Allah's descent? The answer is a long discussion, but he summarizes a methodology (manhaj) applicable to these and similar texts. He divides people into two categories: commoners ('awamm) and scholars ('ulama). For commoners, the appropriate approach is not to engage them in ta'wil (interpretation) but rather to remove from their beliefs anything that implies anthropomorphism (tashbih) or temporality (huduth), and to firmly establish in them that Allah exists, nothing is like Him, and He is the Hearing, the Seeing. For scholars, it is fitting for them to know and understand the correct interpretation. Ghazali explicitly states that understanding these meanings is not an individual obligation (fard 'ayn) because no such obligation has been revealed; the obligation (taklif) is only tanzih (transcendence). He concludes that minds were not obligated to understand all meanings of the Quran. This passage thus provides a practical methodology for dealing with ambiguous verses while safeguarding the commoners' faith.

Key Terms
الاستقرار (al-istiqrar)
العرش (al-‘arsh)
الاستواء (al-istiwā')
النزول (al-nuzūl)
العوام (al-‘awāmm)
العلماء (al-‘ulamā')
التنزيه (al-tanzīh)
التشبيه (al-tashbīh)
الحدوث (al-ḥudūth)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think Ghazali forbids ta'wil entirely or says commoners should be taught the literal meaning.
Correction: Ghazali only says commoners should not be taken into these interpretations; instead, their beliefs should be purified from tashbih and they should be taught tanzih.

Checkpoint
According to al-Ghazali, what is the obligation (taklif) for all Muslims regarding Allah's attributes?
A) To understand every apparent meaning in the Quran.
B) To perform ta'wil of all ambiguous verses.
C) To affirm tanzih (transcendence) and avoid tashbih.
D) To believe that Allah descends literally every night.

Key Takeaway
For Ghazali, the core obligation is tanzih (transcendence), and understanding the specific meanings of ambiguous verses is not required of everyone.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read a verse like 'The Most Merciful rose over the Throne' (20:5), do you feel a need to know exactly how it happens, or are you content to affirm 'nothing is like Him'? What does your reaction reveal about your reliance on scholars or your own desire for certainty?
?
Ghazali says the obligation is only transcendence. Have you ever examined your own beliefs about Allah for subtle traces of anthropomorphism (e.g., imagining a shape, location, or movement)? What concrete step will you take this week to purify your conception of Allah?
?
In which group do you truly place yourself: commoners or scholars? If you are not a scholar, are you humble enough to accept that some meanings are beyond you, or do you feel entitled to interpret every verse yourself? How will you change your approach to ambiguous texts starting today?

Divine Transcendence

The First Literal Meaning is Impossible for Allah
Razi commands the believer to reject the literal meaning of anthropomorphic expressions as impossible for Allah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the literal meaning of anthropomorphic expressions must be rejected for Allah, based on the principle of divine transcendence.

Core Idea
Literal anthropomorphic meanings imply bodily properties, which are impossible for Allah.

Imam Razi opens this lesson with a clear command: the believer must hold with certainty that the literal meaning of scriptural expressions about Allah is not intended. This is not a matter of interpretation, but a logical necessity. In this passage, he provides the core reason: such meanings entail being a body or having bodily accidents, both of which are impossible for the Creator.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the commanding tone and the logical structure: command, affirmation, reason.
The phrase 'بعد المعنى الحقيقى' may be a typo; it likely means 'after the real meaning'.
2
Understand the two affirmations
Razi states two things: (1) the first (literal) meaning is not intended, (2) it is impossible for Allah. Grasp the connection between them.
3
Grasp the reason
The reason is that the literal meaning is a necessary property of bodies or their accidents. This links anthropomorphic expressions to physicality, which is impossible for Allah.
This is the principle of tanzih (transcendence).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فليعتقد المؤمن قطعا : أن الأول - وهو بعد المعنى الحقيقى - غير مراد ، وأنه على الله محال . فإنه من لوازم الأجسام ، أو لوازم أعراض الأجسام
Let the believer believe with certainty that the first [meaning] - which is after the real meaning - is not intended, and that it is impossible for Allah. For it is among the necessary properties of bodies, or among the necessary properties of accidents of bodies.
Source pages p. 276

Commentary
This passage is a doctrinal statement from Imam Razi. He commands the believer to hold with certainty that the first (literal) meaning of scriptural expressions like 'istiwa' is not what is meant, and that attributing such a meaning to Allah is impossible. The reason is that these literal meanings entail either being a body or being an accident of a body, both of which are impossible for Allah. Thus, the passage establishes the principle of tanzih (transcendence) by negating anthropomorphic interpretations.

Key Terms
الأول
المعنى الحقيقى
محال
لوازم الأجسام
لوازم أعراض الأجسام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Rejecting the literal meaning of anthropomorphic expressions is equivalent to denying the truth of the Qur'an or Hadith.
Correction: Razi clarifies that rejecting the literal meaning is necessary because the literal meaning implies impossibility (being a body or accident). It is not denial of the text, but proper understanding of divine transcendence.

Checkpoint
Why does Razi say that the first (literal) meaning of anthropomorphic expressions is impossible for Allah?
A) Because it contradicts the consensus of scholars
B) Because it implies being a body or an accident of a body
C) Because it was only meant for the Prophet
D) Because it is unclear

Key Takeaway
The literal meanings of anthropomorphic expressions are impossible for Allah because they entail bodily properties, affirming His absolute transcendence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite or hear verses about Allah's 'hand' or 'sitting' on the Throne, does your mind ever form a mental image? How does Razi's command to believe with certainty challenge that habit? What concrete step will you take the next time such an image arises?
?
Are there any areas of your understanding of Allah where you have tolerated uncertainty or ambiguity about His transcendence? Will you commit to studying these matters until you can say 'I believe with certainty' as Razi demands?
?
If you have ever been tempted to think that rejecting the literal meaning is a form of denial or weakness, what does this lesson reveal about the true nature of faith? How will you defend the principle of tanzih when questioned by others?

Razi's Figurative Interpretation of 'Above'
Razi interprets 'fawq' figuratively as high rank with an analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi interprets the word 'above' (fawq) in reference to Allah as a figurative high rank, using the analogy of a king above a minister.

Core Idea
Razi shows that 'above' for Allah means rank, not location—like a sultan above a minister.

Imam Razi continues his discussion of ambiguous verses by providing a concrete example of ta'wil. He takes the word 'above' (fawq) which might imply physical elevation and shows that when applied to Allah, it must be understood figuratively as high rank and authority. This lesson unpacks his concise yet powerful analogy.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once and then the English translation. Notice the concise style Razi uses.
2
Understand 'ta'wil'
Recognize that Razi is performing ta'wil (figurative interpretation) to avoid attributing physicality to Allah.
Ta'wil is a key method in Islamic theology for interpreting ambiguous verses.
3
Analyze the analogy
Think about what it means to say 'the sultan is above the minister' — it does not mean physical elevation but higher rank and authority.
This analogy clarifies how 'above' applies to Allah non-locally.
4
Affirmation without doubt
Notice that Razi asserts we do not doubt the affirmation of this meaning for Allah. The figurative interpretation does not weaken the attribute; it preserves transcendence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويقول أيضا - رحمه الله - : تأويل لفظ الفوق بالمعلو المعنوى، الذى هو المراد بقولنا : السلطان فوق الوزير . فإنا لا نشك فى ثبوت معناه الله تعالى
And he also says - may Allah have mercy on him -: The interpretation of the word 'above' as the figurative highness, which is meant by our saying: 'The sultan is above the minister.' For we do not doubt the affirmation of its meaning for Allah, exalted.
Source pages p. 276

Commentary
This passage provides a concrete example of tawil (interpretation). Razi explains that when the word 'fawq' (above) is used for Allah, it should be interpreted figuratively as high rank and authority, similar to saying a king is above his minister. This is a non-locational meaning. Razi asserts that this figurative meaning is affirmed for Allah without any doubt.

Key Terms
تأويل
الفوق
المعلو المعنوى
السلطان فوق الوزير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that interpreting 'fawq' figuratively weakens Allah's attribute.
Correction: Razi shows it is a legitimate and necessary interpretation to avoid corporealism; the attribute is affirmed but in a manner befitting Allah.

Checkpoint
What example does Razi give to illustrate the figurative meaning of 'above' for Allah?
A) The sun is above the earth
B) The sultan is above the minister
C) Knowledge is above ignorance
D) The sky is above the ground

Key Takeaway
Razi's interpretation of 'fawq' as figurative high rank shows that divine attributes can be understood metaphorically to preserve Allah's transcendence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'Allah is above everything', do you ever catch yourself visualizing a physical location? What does that reveal about your heart's attachment to tangible images? How can you train your mind to affirm Allah's transcendence without resorting to imagination? Commit to one specific practice this week.
?
The sultan above minister analogy is familiar—yet do you truly live as if Allah's rank is infinitely higher than any earthly authority? When you obey a boss or ruler, do you feel the same awe for Allah's command? What concrete change in your daily life would reflect that Allah is truly 'above' all?
?
Razi's ta'wil preserves Allah's transcendence. But are there attributes of Allah you are uncomfortable interpreting figuratively? Why? Could that discomfort stem from a desire to define Allah in your own terms? Repent and surrender to what the scholars have unanimously affirmed.

Qurtubi on Majority View of Tanzih
Qurtubi states that the majority of scholars hold tanzih obligatory for istiwa.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the majority of early and later scholars, as reported by al-Qurtubi, held that tanzih (transcendence) is obligatory when interpreting the verse of istiwa.

Core Idea
Tanzih is the majority stance on istiwa.

In this passage, al-Qurtubi, the renowned Maliki mufassir, reports a critical consensus: the majority of scholars, both early and later, affirm that declaring Allah's transcendence (tanzih) is necessary when interpreting the verse 'thumma istawa 'ala al-'arsh'. This establishes that tawil (interpretation) in favor of tanzih is not a minority view but the mainstream position.

Guided Path
1

Al-Qurtubi died in 671 AH.
2
Focus on the key phrase
Al-Qurtubi says 'al-aktharun' (the majority) of both earlier and later scholars. This indicates a strong consensus.
The sentence is cut off, but the intended meaning is clear.
3
Understand the implication
The passage implies that when tanzih is obligatory, the apparent literal meaning of 'istiwa' is not taken. This supports the broader theme of the book.
This counters the misconception that tawil is a fringe view.

Why Literal Interpretation of Throne Verses is Impossible
Refutation of literal interpretation of Throne and its carriers

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why literal interpretation of verses about Allah's Throne and its carriers leads to absurd consequences, and how scholars like al-Ghazali and al-Qurtubi interpret them figuratively to preserve divine transcendence.

Core Idea
Literal reading of Throne verses makes the Creator need His creation.

This lesson examines a key argument from Imam al-Razi's refutation of anthropomorphism. He cites Quranic verses about the Throne and its carriers, showing that a literal understanding would imply God's need for created things—an impossibility. The passage then introduces the correct approach: figurative interpretation (ta'wil) as practiced by leading Sunni scholars.

Guided Path
1
Read the Quranic verses and the literal interpretation problem
The passage begins with two verses (7:54, 69:17). Notice how Imam al-Razi points out the absurd consequence if taken literally.
The verses are about God's establishing over the Throne and eight angels carrying it.
2
Understand the absurdity argument
If literal, then God would need the Throne and the angels, which are created. That contradicts God's self-sufficiency.
This is a logical reductio ad absurdum.
3
Learn the proper figurative interpretation
The author explains that God uses familiar royal imagery to help humans understand, but He is unlike anything.
This is the principle of tanzih (transcendence).
4
Examine the hadith about the carriers and its status
The hadith about eight angels like mountain goats are solitary (aahaad) narrations, which do not establish creedal doctrines.
If they do establish beliefs, they must be interpreted figuratively.
5
See how scholars like al-Ghazali and al-Qurtubi applied ta'wil
Al-Ghazali confirms figurative interpretation; al-Qurtubi compares Throne to the Kaaba—attributed to God but not for dwelling.
This shows a consistent Sunni method.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال تعالى : « إن ربكم الله الذي خلق السموات والأرض في ستة أيام، ثم استوى على العرش، (الأعراف ٥٤ ) ومعنى ويحمل عرش ربك فوقهم يؤمئد ثمانية ( الحاقه ۱۷ ) إذا فسرته على ظاهره ، يكون الله محتاجا إلى الكرسي ، والكونى محتاج إلى الثمانية ، والكرسي مخلوق ومحتاج ، والثمانية مخلوقون ومحتاجون . فهل يعقل أحد أن يكون الخالق محتاجا إلى المخلوقين ؟ إن المعنى المراد من النص الكريم : هو أن الله تعالى يقرب ذاته إلى عقول البشر ، حسبما يألفون في حياتهم من عادات الملوك ، وحسبما يفهمون باللغة التي يتخاطبون بها . فعبر عن نفسه كملك ، أما هو فليس كمثله شيء . ومعنى أن موسى - عليه السلام - يأخذ بقائمة من قوائم العرش، كا معنى من حديث الأو عال الذي فيه : «إن فوق السماء السابعة ثمانية أو عال، بين أظلا فهن وركبهن ، مثل ما بين سماء إلى سماء ، وفوق ظهورهن : العرش، وفي رواية : « إن حملة العرش ثمانية أملاك على صورة الأوعال ، ما بين أظلافها إلى ركيها مسيرة سبعين عاما ، للطائر المسرع ، وهذه الأحاديث مروية بطريق الآحاد ، وهى لا تثبت عقائد غيبية . وإذا قلنا بأنها تثبت عقائد . فإنها تؤول إلى معنى ينفي التجسيم عن الله عز وجل ، كما وضح الشيخ الغزالي أبو حامد . والقرطبي المفسر يؤولها بقوله : « إضافة العرش إلى الله تعالى ، كإضافة البيت ، وليس البيت للسكنى ، فكذلك
المرش
He said, the Most High: 'Indeed, your Lord is Allah, who created the heavens and the earth in six days, then established Himself above the Throne' (al-A'raf 54). And the meaning of 'and the Throne of your Lord will be carried above them that Day, eight [angels]' (al-Haqqah 17). If you interpret it literally, then God would be in need of the Chair, and [the word?] would be in need of the eight, and the Chair is created and in need, and the eight are created and in need. Can any reasonable person imagine that the Creator is in need of the created? The intended meaning of the noble text is that God, the Most High, brings His essence close to the minds of humans according to what they are accustomed to in their lives from the customs of kings, and according to what they understand in the language they speak. So He described Himself as a king, but He is not like anything. And the meaning of Moses - peace be upon him - taking hold of a leg of the Throne is like the meaning of the hadith of the mountain goats which says: 'Above the seventh heaven there are eight mountain goats; between their hooves and their knees is like what is between one heaven and another, and above their backs is the Throne.' And in another narration: 'The carriers of the Throne are eight angels in the form of mountain goats; between their hooves and their knees is a distance of seventy years for a swift bird.' These hadiths are narrated through solitary chains (aahaad), and they do not establish unseen beliefs. And if we say that they do establish beliefs, then they are interpreted in a way that negates anthropomorphism (tajseem) concerning Allah, the Mighty and Majestic, as clarified by Shaykh al-Ghazali Abu Hamid. And al-Qurtubi, the commentator, interprets them by saying: 'The attribution of the Throne to Allah, the Most High, is like the attribution of the House [to Him], and the House is not for dwelling, so likewise...'
Source pages p. 280

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of anthropomorphic interpretation of Quranic verses about God's Throne and its carriers. The author begins by citing two verses: (7:54) about God's istiwa' over the Throne, and (69:17) about eight angels carrying the Throne. He argues that if these are taken literally, they imply that God is in need of the Throne and its carriers, which are created and needy themselves. This leads to the absurd conclusion that the Creator is in need of His creation. Therefore, the true meaning is that God uses familiar imagery from human royal customs to bring His essence closer to human understanding, but He is nothing like any created thing. The author then references a hadith about the eight carriers resembling mountain goats, noting that these are solitary (aahaad) narrations which either do not establish creedal beliefs or, if they do, must be interpreted in a way that negates anthropomorphism (tajseem). He cites Imam al-Ghazali and al-Qurtubi as authorities who interpret the attribution of the Throne to God as a metaphorical attribution, like the attribution of the Kaaba to God, which is not meant for dwelling.

التأويل
التجسيم
حملة العرش
الآحاد

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some Muslims believe that Quranic verses about the Throne must be taken literally, implying that Allah sits on a physical throne carried by angels.
Correction: Imam al-Razi proves that literal interpretation leads to the absurdity of the Creator needing His creation; therefore, these verses must be interpreted figuratively to preserve Allah's transcendence.

Literal interpretation of Throne verses implies God's need for creation, which is impossible; hence they must be interpreted figuratively, as done by Sunni scholars.

Imam Razi's Method and Jesus' Affirmation of God's Eternity
Comparing Imam Razi's method with Jesus' affirmation of God's eternity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi's theological method regarding God's attributes parallels Jesus' own affirmation of God's transcendence in the Gospel of Barnabas.

Core Idea
Jesus and Imam Razi both used scripture to affirm that God has no beginning and no end.

Imam Razi's approach to discussing God's essence and attributes is often misunderstood. In this lesson, we see a fascinating parallel: the author of Asas al-Taqdis compares Razi's speech to that of Jesus in the Gospel of Barnabas. This comparison highlights a shared emphasis on divine transcendence (tanzih). Let's explore the passage and its significance.

Guided Path
1
Understand the comparison

The author is not equating Razi and Jesus, but showing a similarity in method.
2
Examine the quoted passage

This quote is used to show that earlier scriptures also affirm God's transcendence.
3
Reflect on the theological point
Consider how both Jesus and Imam Razi emphasize tanzih (transcendence) and reject anthropomorphism. Ask yourself: Why is this comparison powerful?
It shows that the Islamic position is consistent with earlier prophetic teachings.

Jesus' Declaration of Divine Transcendence as Proof Against Anthropomorphism
Jesus' dialogue affirming divine transcendence, with author's commentary linking to Islamic theology.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the words of Jesus Christ (peace be upon him) affirm God's absolute transcendence (tanzih) and refute anthropomorphism (tashbih), and how this aligns with the discourse of later Islamic scholars like Imam Razi and Imam al-Ghazali.

Core Idea
Jesus' testimony confirms God is beyond physical attributes—a timeless truth echoed by Islamic scholars.

This lesson presents a remarkable dialogue from the Gospel of Barnabas wherein Jesus Christ, facing a crowd that had mistakenly deified him, ascends a stone and publicly declares the absolute transcendence of God. He quotes from earlier scriptures to affirm God's eternity, invisibility, incorporeality, self-sufficiency, and omnipresence. The passage then connects this prophetic declaration to the teachings of Imam al-Ghazali and Imam Fakhr al-Din al-Razi, challenging anthropomorphists to reconcile their views with the words of a prophet.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the full translation to understand the dialogue and commentary.
Pay attention to the structure: introduction, narrative, dialogue, author's reflection.
2
Identify the six attributes Jesus affirms
In the dialogue, Jesus makes six distinct statements about God. List them and notice how each is met with the priest's agreement.
These attributes are all about God's transcendence.
3
Consider the purpose of the dialogue
The Romans thought Jesus was divine. Jesus responds by affirming God's transcendence and denying any likeness to creation.
This directly refutes anthropomorphism.
4
Reflect on the author's comparison
The author says Jesus' words resemble those of Imam al-Ghazali and Imam Razi. Ask yourself why this comparison is powerful.
It shows consistency of tanzih across prophets and scholars.
5
Understand the challenge to anthropomorphists
The passage ends with a direct challenge to those who claim God has a body or attributes like creation. How does this passage answer them?
If a prophet says otherwise, how can they disagree?

النص :
حينئذ ارتقى يسوع أحد الحجارة الإثني عشر التي أمر يشوع الإثني عشر سبطا ، أن يأخذوها من وسط الأردن ، عندما عبر إسرائيل من هناك. دون أن تبتل أحذيتهم وقال بصوت عال : ليصعد كامننا إلى محل مرتفع حيث يتمكن من تحقيق كلامى . فصعد من ثم الكاهن إلى هناك . فقال له يسوع بوضوح يتمكن كل واحد من سماعه : قد كتب في عهد الله الحى(۱)، وميثاقه أن ليس لإلهنا بداية، ولا يكون له نهاية . أجاب المكامن : لقد كتب هكذا هناك . فقال يسوع : إنه كتب هناك : أن الهنا قد برأ كل شيء بكلمته فقط (۱) . فأجاب الكامن : إنه لكذلك . فقال يسوع : إنه مكتوب هناك : أن الله لا يرى (٢) وأنه محجوب عن عقل الإنسان لأنه غير متجسد وغير مركب وغير متغير . فقال الكاهن : إنه لكذلك حقا . فقال : إنه مكتوب هناك : كيف أن سماء السموات لا تسعه (٣) . لأن الهنا غير محدود . فقال الكاهن : هكذا قال سليمان النبي يا يسوع . قال يسوع: إنه مكتوب هناك : أن ليس لله حاجة . لأنه لا يأكل ولا ينام ولا يعتريه نقص . قال الكامن : إنه لكذلك قال يسوع : إنه مكتوب هناك : إن إلهنا في كل مكان ، وأن لا إله غيره ، الذي يضرب ويشفى ، ويفعل كل ما يريد (٤) . قال الكاهن : هكذا كتب . حينئذ رفع يسوع يديه ، وقال : إيها الرب إلهنا . هذا هو إيماني الذي أتى به إلى دينونتك ، شاهدا على كل من يؤمن بخلاف ذلك ، ( برنا با ۹۰ : ۲ - ۱۷ )
هذا هو هو كلام نبي من أنبياء الله ، ورسول من رسل الله . عن الله وصفاته ، شبيه بكلام الإمام الغزالى أبي حامد ، و للإمام فخر الدين فماذا يقول فيه المجسمون ، والمجسمون بلاكيف ؟ وليس هو كلام المسيح وحده ، بل هو كلام النبيين والمرسلمين من قبله . فإنه ـ كما هو واضح من النص - يستشهد على كلامه عن الله وصفاته بآيات مكتوبة في التوراة وفي الزبور ، و فى أسفار الأنبياء - ذكرنا مواضعها في التعليقات -
The literal and metaphorical in reports of God's attributes in the books of the Torah and the speech of Imam Fakhr al-Din al-Razi - may God be pleased with him – resembles the speech of the Messiah Jesus son of Mary, peace be upon him, concerning God's essence and attributes. And the Messiah is a great prophet - for Barnabas narrated in his Gospel: that when the Romans saw the miracles of the Messiah, they thought he was God or the Son of God, and they stirred up a people in the land of Palestine. And for that reason, the chief of the scholars of the Children of Israel and the governor Herod approached him to remove the sedition that had arisen because of him, and they begged him to ascend a high place and speak to the people to calm them.
The text:
Then Jesus ascended one of the twelve stones which Joshua commanded the twelve tribes to take from the middle of the Jordan, when Israel crossed over from there, without their sandals getting wet, and said in a loud voice: Let our priest ascend to a high place where he can verify my words. So the priest then ascended there. Then Jesus said to him clearly, so that each one could hear: It is written in the covenant of the living God (1) and His pact that our God has no beginning, nor will He have an end. The priest answered: It is written thus there. Then Jesus said: It is written there that our God created everything only by His word (1). The priest answered: It is so. Then Jesus said: It is written there that God is not seen (2) and that He is veiled from the human intellect because He is not embodied, not composite, and not subject to change. The priest said: It is truly so. He said: It is written there: how the heaven of heavens does not contain Him (3). Because our God is unlimited. The priest said: Thus said Solomon the prophet, O Jesus. Jesus said: It is written there that God has no need, because He does not eat, does not sleep, and no deficiency befalls Him. The priest said: It is so. Jesus said: It is written there that our God is everywhere, and there is no god besides Him, who strikes and heals, and does whatever He wills (4). The priest said: Thus it is written. Then Jesus raised his hands and said: O Lord our God, this is my faith which I have brought to Your judgment, a witness against everyone who believes otherwise, (Barnabas 90:2-17).
This is the speech of a prophet of God's prophets and a messenger of God's messengers concerning God and His attributes, resembling the speech of Imam al-Ghazali Abu Hamid, and of Imam Fakhr al-Din. So what do the anthropomorphists say about it, and the anthropomorphists without modality? And it is not the speech of the Messiah alone, but the speech of the prophets and messengers before him. For he - as is clear from the text - cites verses written in the Torah, in the Psalms, and in the books of the prophets - we have mentioned their places in the comments.
Source pages p. 281

Commentary
This passage is a proof from the reported words of Jesus Christ (peace be upon him) to support the doctrine of divine transcendence (tanzih) against anthropomorphism (tashbih). The author begins by stating the general topic: the literal and metaphorical in reports of God's attributes in the Torah, and that the speech of Imam Razi resembles the speech of Jesus. He then provides context from the Gospel of Barnabas: when the Romans thought Jesus was divine, the Jewish leaders asked him to speak to calm the people. The core text (النص) is a dialogue where Jesus ascends a stone and addresses a priest, quoting scriptures to affirm that God has no beginning or end (eternity), created everything by His word, is invisible and veiled from intellect because He is incorporeal, not composite, and unchanging, that the heavens cannot contain Him (unlimited), that He has no need (does not eat, sleep, or suffer deficiency), and that He is omnipresent, unique, and omnipotent (strikes and heals, does all He wills). Each statement is met with the priest's agreement. Jesus then declares this as his faith, a witness against those who believe otherwise. After the quote, the author comments that this speech of Jesus resembles the speech of Imam al-Ghazali and Imam Razi, and challenges the anthropomorphists (المجسمون) and those who affirm attributes without modality (المجسمون بلاكيف) to respond. He further notes that this is not only Jesus' speech but the speech of all prophets, as Jesus himself cites scriptures from the Torah, Psalms, and prophets. Thus, the passage argues that the true belief in God's transcendence is consistent across all revelations, and any deviation is refuted by the words of the prophets themselves.

Key Terms
تنزيه
تشبيه
مجسمة
صفات الله
الإنجيل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that comparing Imam Razi's speech to Jesus' speech implies Jesus is not a distinct prophet or that the Gospel of Barnabas is canonical in Islam.
Correction: The author uses the Gospel of Barnabas illustration as a historical proof for tanzih, not as a source of Islamic creed. Jesus is a prophet, and his words support the same transcendent theology later articulated by Islamic scholars. The comparison is one of agreement in doctrine, not equal authority.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what attribute of God does Jesus NOT mention in his dialogue with the priest?
A) God has no beginning or end
B) God is merciful
C) God is unseen
D) God is self-sufficient

Key Takeaway
The prophetic affirmation of God's transcendence (tanzih) is consistent across revelations and serves as a powerful refutation of anthropomorphism.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of God, do you ever unconsciously picture Him with human-like features or limitations? How does Jesus’ declaration that God is 'not embodied, not composite, not changeable' challenge your inner imagination? What practical step can you take to purify your conception of God starting today?
?
Jesus said that God is 'veiled from the human intellect.' In a world that demands proof and visibility, does your faith sometimes waver because you cannot see or fully comprehend God? How can you embrace the mystery of His transcendence while still trusting in His closeness?
?
Jesus publicly declared his faith in God's transcendence 'as a witness against everyone who believes otherwise.' Are you willing to stand up for tawhid even when it is unpopular? In what situation this week can you bear witness to God's oneness and uniqueness?

Divine Transcendence

Biblical Proofs of Divine Transcendence
Jesus cites biblical scriptures to affirm God's transcendence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi uses Jesus's scriptural citations from the Torah and Psalms to establish God's transcendence beyond anthropomorphism.

Core Idea
Jesus affirms God's transcendence through scriptures, refuting any physical likeness.

In this passage, Imam Razi presents a dialogue where Jesus quotes directly from the Torah and Psalms to assert core attributes of God: that He is unseen, unlimited, without need, and present everywhere. This lesson examines how these scriptural proofs serve as an argument against anthropomorphism in Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the Dialogue
Read the exchange between Jesus and the priest, noting that each of Jesus's statements is a scriptural citation. Observe how the priest confirms each one.
2
Identify the Attributes
List the divine attributes affirmed: God is not seen, not incarnate, not composed, not changeable, not limited, has no need, does not eat or sleep, omnipresent, sovereign. Each attribute negates a limitation.
3
Connect to Islamic Kalam
Understand that Razi is showing that even prior prophets taught tanzih, supporting the Islamic position that God is not like creation. Razi compares this to al-Ghazali and himself.

الأنبياء . و ماذا قال المسيح عيسى بن مريم عليه السلام في يد الله ، أي في الصفحات الخبرية كاليد والرجل والعين وما شابه ذلك ؟ إنه لما قال لبني إسرائيل : كل شيء يأتي من يد الله ، قال له تلميذ من تلاميذه ، اسمه « متى » : « يا معلم . إنك لقد اعترفت أمام اليهودية كلها . بأن ليس الله من شبه كالبشر. وقلت الآن : إن الإنسان ينال من الله . فإذا كان لله يدان ، فله إذن شبه بالبشر ؟ أجاب يسوع : إنك لفي ضلال يا متى . ولقد ضل كثيرون كذا ، إذ لم يفقهوا معنى الكلام . لأنه لا يجب على الإنسان أن يلاحظ ظاهر الكلام ، بل معناه : إذ الكلام البشري بمثابة ترجمان بيننا وبين الله . ألا تعلم أنه لما أراد الله أن يكلم آباءنا على جبل سيناء ، صرخ آباؤنا : كلمنا أنت يا موسى ، ولا يكلمنا الله ، لئلا نموت ؟ وماذا قال الله على لسان أشعياء النبي : أليس كما بعدت السموات عن الأرض ، هكذا بعدت طرق الله عن طرق الناس ، وأفكار الله عن أفكار الناس ؟ إن الله لا يدركه قياس ، إلى حد أني أرتجف من وصفه . ويبين المسيح عيسى عليه السلام أن الله يعبر عن ذاته بأوصاف البشر ، على سبيل التمثيل والمشاكلة والمقابلة . أما هو عز وجل فليس مثل البشر ، ولا مثل أي شيء . فيقول : « اعمر الله الذي تقف نفسي في حضرته : إن الكون أمام الله الصغير ، كحبة رمل ، والله أعظم من ذلك بمقدار ما يلزم من حبوب الرمل ، لكل السموات والجنة ، بل أكثر . فانظروا الآن . إذا كان هنالك نسبة بين الله والإنسان ، الذي ليس سوى كتلة صغيرة من طين، واقفة على الأرض . فانتبهوا إذن .
By His word alone. The priest answered: It is so. Jesus said: It is written there that God is not seen and that He is hidden from the human mind because He is not incarnate, not composed, and not changeable. The priest said: It is truly so. He said: It is written there how the heaven of heavens does not contain Him, because our God is not limited. The priest said: Thus said Solomon the prophet, O Jesus. Jesus said: It is written there that God has no need, because He does not eat, does not sleep, and no deficiency befalls Him. The priest said: It is so. Jesus said: It is written there that our God is in every place, and that there is no god but Him, who strikes and heals, and does all that He wills. The priest said: Thus it is written. Then Jesus raised his hands and said: O Lord our God, this is my faith which I have brought to your judgment, a witness against everyone who believes otherwise. This is the speech of a prophet from the prophets of God, and a messenger from the messengers of God, about God and His attributes, similar to the speech of Imam al-Ghazali Abu Hamid and Imam Fakhr al-Din. So what do the anthropomorphists and the anthropomorphists who say 'without how' say about it? And it is not the speech of the Messiah alone, but rather the speech of the prophets and messengers before him. For it is, as is clear from the text, he cites about his speech concerning God and His attributes verses written in the Torah, the Psalms, and the books of the prophets. And what did the Messiah, Jesus son of Mary (peace be upon him), say about the hand of God, i.e., about the scriptural passages like the hand, foot, eye, and the like? When he said to the Children of Israel: Everything comes from the hand of God, a disciple of his, named Matthew, said to him: 'O teacher, you have confessed before all of Judaism that God is not like humans. And now you say that man receives from God. If God has two hands, then He has resemblance to humans?' Jesus answered: 'You are in error, O Matthew. Many have erred like this, as they did not understand the meaning of speech. For it is not proper for a person to observe the outward form of speech, but rather its meaning. Indeed, human speech is like an interpreter between us and God. Do you not know that when God wanted to speak to our fathers on Mount Sinai, our fathers cried out: Speak to us yourself, O Moses, and let not God speak to us, lest we die? And what did God say through the prophet Isaiah: Is it not that as the heavens are distant from the earth, so distant are the ways of God from the ways of people, and the thoughts of God from the thoughts of people? God cannot be grasped by analogy, to the extent that I tremble from describing Him.' And the Messiah Jesus (peace be upon him) explains that God expresses Himself through human attributes, by way of likening, approximation, and correspondence. But He, the Mighty and Majestic, is not like humans, nor like anything. He says: 'I swear by God before whom my soul stands: the universe before God the small is like a grain of sand, and God is greater than that by the amount needed of grains of sand for all the heavens and paradise, even more. So look now. If there is any proportion between God and man, who is only a small lump of clay standing on the earth. So pay attention then.'
Source pages p. 282

Commentary
This passage is a proof for divine transcendence (tanzih) and a refutation of anthropomorphism (tashbih), presented through a dialogue between Jesus (peace be upon him) and a priest/disciple. Imam Razi begins by quoting Jesus's statements from biblical scriptures affirming that God is invisible, not limited, without need, omnipresent, and unique in power. Jesus concludes that this is the faith of all prophets. Razi then compares this to the positions of al-Ghazali and Fakhr al-Din al-Razi (himself), asking what anthropomorphists can say against it. The second part addresses the 'hand of God' and similar expressions. When Jesus says everything comes from God's hand, his disciple Matthew objects, thinking this implies a physical hand and thus resemblance. Jesus rebukes him, explaining that the literal outward meaning (zahir) is not intended; rather, the meaning (ma'na) matters. Human speech is an interpreter (targuman) between God and us. He cites the fear of the Israelites at Sinai and Isaiah 55:9 to show God's incomparability. Jesus then states that God describes Himself using human attributes by way of tashbih (likening), mushakala (approximation), and muqabala (correspondence), but God is unlike anything. The passage ends with a vivid analogy: the universe is like a grain of sand before God, emphasizing the utter disproportion between Creator and creation. The argument is entirely scriptural, using the Bible as common ground. This passage guards against the error of taking anthropomorphic expressions literally, a key concern in kalam.

Key Terms
التنزيه
التجسيم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some believe that God can be seen or has a body because the Bible may suggest it.
Correction: Jesus explicitly states that God is not seen and is hidden from the human mind because He is not incarnate, not composed, and not changeable.

Checkpoint

A) God is omnipresent
B) God is not limited
C) God has no need
D) God is unseen

Key Takeaway
The scriptures affirm God's absolute transcendence, denying any limitation or physicality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you imagine God in your mind, do you ever unconsciously picture Him as having a form or being somewhere? How does this passage challenge that tendency? What will you actively do today to remind yourself that God is beyond any physical likeness?
?
The passage says God is محجوب عن عقل الإنسان (hidden from human intellect). Are you comfortable with not being able to fully comprehend God, or do you sometimes try to reduce Him to what your mind can grasp? How can you cultivate awe and submission in the face of His incomprehensibility?
?
Jesus says this is the same faith of all prophets. Do you truly believe that the God you worship is the same God of all prophets—utterly transcendent and beyond any limitation? Or do you find yourself slipping into thinking of Him as limited, perhaps by your own expectations? Write down one limitation you might have unconsciously attributed to God and repent from it.

Divine Transcendence

The Figurative Interpretation of Anthropomorphic Expressions
Jesus explains that anthropomorphic expressions in scripture are figurative.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the principle that scriptural anthropomorphic expressions like 'hand of God' are not taken literally but are figurative, as explained by Jesus in response to his disciple Matthew.

Core Idea
Outward form of speech is not intended; meaning matters, and God is unlike anything.

When Jesus said all comes from the 'hand of God,' his disciple Matthew objected, thinking this implied a physical hand. Jesus rebukes him, clarifying that divine speech uses human language figuratively. This lesson explores that hermeneutical principle and how it applies to all anthropomorphic passages in scripture.

Guided Path
1
Read the Exchange
Read Jesus's response to Matthew. Note the disciple's objection and Jesus's correction. The disciple mistakenly takes 'hand' literally. Jesus emphasizes understanding the intended meaning, not the outward form.
2
Identify the Hermeneutical Principle
Jesus says: 'do not observe the outward form (zahir) but the meaning (ma'na). Human speech is an interpreter between us and God.' This principle is key for interpreting anthropomorphic expressions.
3
Reflect on the Analogy
Jesus says the universe is like a grain of sand before God. This illustrates the vast difference between Creator and creation, so human attributes cannot apply literally.

آباءنا على جبل سيناء ، صرخ آباؤنا : كلمنا أنت يا موسى ، ولا يكلمنا الله ، لئلا نموت ؟ وماذا قال الله على لسان أشعياء النبي : أليس كما بعدت السموات عن الأرض ، هكذا بعدت طرق الله عن طرق الناس ، وأفكار الله عن أفكار الناس ؟ إن الله لا يدركه قياس ، إلى حد أني أرتجف من وصفه . ويبين المسيح عيسى عليه السلام أن الله يعبر عن ذاته بأوصاف البشر ، على سبيل التمثيل والمشاكلة والمقابلة . أما هو عز وجل فليس مثل البشر ، ولا مثل أي شيء . فيقول : « اعمر الله الذي تقف نفسي في حضرته : إن الكون أمام الله الصغير ، كحبة رمل ، والله أعظم من ذلك بمقدار ما يلزم من حبوب الرمل ، لكل السموات والجنة ، بل أكثر . فانظروا الآن . إذا كان هنالك نسبة بين الله والإنسان ، الذي ليس سوى كتلة صغيرة من طين، واقفة على الأرض . فانتبهوا إذن .
By His word alone. The priest answered: It is so. Jesus said: It is written there that God is not seen and that He is hidden from the human mind because He is not incarnate, not composed, and not changeable. The priest said: It is truly so. He said: It is written there how the heaven of heavens does not contain Him, because our God is not limited. The priest said: Thus said Solomon the prophet, O Jesus. Jesus said: It is written there that God has no need, because He does not eat, does not sleep, and no deficiency befalls Him. The priest said: It is so. Jesus said: It is written there that our God is in every place, and that there is no god but Him, who strikes and heals, and does all that He wills. The priest said: Thus it is written. Then Jesus raised his hands and said: O Lord our God, this is my faith which I have brought to your judgment, a witness against everyone who believes otherwise. This is the speech of a prophet from the prophets of God, and a messenger from the messengers of God, about God and His attributes, similar to the speech of Imam al-Ghazali Abu Hamid and Imam Fakhr al-Din. So what do the anthropomorphists and the anthropomorphists who say 'without how' say about it? And it is not the speech of the Messiah alone, but rather the speech of the prophets and messengers before him. For it is, as is clear from the text, he cites about his speech concerning God and His attributes verses written in the Torah, the Psalms, and the books of the prophets. And what did the Messiah, Jesus son of Mary (peace be upon him), say about the hand of God, i.e., about the scriptural passages like the hand, foot, eye, and the like? When he said to the Children of Israel: Everything comes from the hand of God, a disciple of his, named Matthew, said to him: 'O teacher, you have confessed before all of Judaism that God is not like humans. And now you say that man receives from God. If God has two hands, then He has resemblance to humans?' Jesus answered: 'You are in error, O Matthew. Many have erred like this, as they did not understand the meaning of speech. For it is not proper for a person to observe the outward form of speech, but rather its meaning. Indeed, human speech is like an interpreter between us and God. Do you not know that when God wanted to speak to our fathers on Mount Sinai, our fathers cried out: Speak to us yourself, O Moses, and let not God speak to us, lest we die? And what did God say through the prophet Isaiah: Is it not that as the heavens are distant from the earth, so distant are the ways of God from the ways of people, and the thoughts of God from the thoughts of people? God cannot be grasped by analogy, to the extent that I tremble from describing Him.' And the Messiah Jesus (peace be upon him) explains that God expresses Himself through human attributes, by way of likening, approximation, and correspondence. But He, the Mighty and Majestic, is not like humans, nor like anything. He says: 'I swear by God before whom my soul stands: the universe before God the small is like a grain of sand, and God is greater than that by the amount needed of grains of sand for all the heavens and paradise, even more. So look now. If there is any proportion between God and man, who is only a small lump of clay standing on the earth. So pay attention then.'
Source pages p. 282

Commentary
This passage is a proof for divine transcendence (tanzih) and a refutation of anthropomorphism (tashbih), presented through a dialogue between Jesus (peace be upon him) and a priest/disciple. Imam Razi begins by quoting Jesus's statements from biblical scriptures affirming that God is invisible, not limited, without need, omnipresent, and unique in power. Jesus concludes that this is the faith of all prophets. Razi then compares this to the positions of al-Ghazali and Fakhr al-Din al-Razi (himself), asking what anthropomorphists can say against it. The second part addresses the 'hand of God' and similar expressions. When Jesus says everything comes from God's hand, his disciple Matthew objects, thinking this implies a physical hand and thus resemblance. Jesus rebukes him, explaining that the literal outward meaning (zahir) is not intended; rather, the meaning (ma'na) matters. Human speech is an interpreter (targuman) between God and us. He cites the fear of the Israelites at Sinai and Isaiah 55:9 to show God's incomparability. Jesus then states that God describes Himself using human attributes by way of tashbih (likening), mushakala (approximation), and muqabala (correspondence), but God is unlike anything. The passage ends with a vivid analogy: the universe is like a grain of sand before God, emphasizing the utter disproportion between Creator and creation. The argument is entirely scriptural, using the Bible as common ground. This passage guards against the error of taking anthropomorphic expressions literally, a key concern in kalam.

Key Terms
يد الله
ظاهر الكلام
معنى الكلام
التمثيل
المشاكلة
المقابلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some believe that the phrase 'hand of God' in scripture proves that God has a physical hand.
Correction: Jesus explicitly says that the outward form of speech is not to be taken literally; the intended meaning is what matters, and God is not like humans.

Checkpoint
According to Jesus's answer to Matthew, why should one not take the outward form (zahir) of biblical expressions like 'hand of God' literally?
A) Because the Bible is not reliable
B) Because human speech is only an interpreter (targuman) between God and us, and the intended meaning (ma'na) is what matters
C) Because Matthew misunderstood the original Hebrew
D) Because Jesus later retracted that statement

Key Takeaway
Anthropomorphic expressions in scripture must be interpreted according to their intended meaning, not their outward form, because God is absolutely unlike creation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read descriptions of God in scripture that seem to attribute human-like actions (e.g., God's hand, God's anger), do you catch yourself imagining God in human-like terms? How can you train your mind to immediately shift to the intended meaning of God's absolute transcendence?
?
Jesus rebukes Matthew for misunderstanding the meaning of the words. Are there areas in your own understanding of God where you have 'not understood the meaning of the speech'? What specific verses challenge you, and how will you seek the intended meaning today?
?
The passage says God's ways are as distant from ours as the heavens are from the earth. Does this distance frighten you or comfort you? How should this truth reshape your prayer and your trust in God's actions in your life, especially when you do not understand His plan?

Biblical Proofs of Divine Transcendence
Razi cites 16 biblical verses affirming tanzih, then notes a Muslim division on interpreting Torah texts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam al-Razi uses biblical texts to argue for the Islamic doctrine that God is unlike anything (tanzih).

Core Idea
The Torah and Prophets consistently affirm that God has no likeness.

Imam al-Razi, in his work Asas al-Taqdis, employs an unusual method: he cites 16 verses from the Old Testament to prove that God is not like anything. This lesson presents those verses and explains their significance within Razi's broader argument for divine transcendence. You will also learn about the differing Muslim attitudes toward the Torah's descriptions of God.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening quote
The passage begins with a quotation from Christian tradition about God being hidden from human senses. Notice how Razi sets the stage for his argument.
The source of this quote is unclear; it may be from the Gospels or Church Fathers.
2
Examine the 16 verses
Razi lists 16 biblical verses from the Torah, Psalms, and Prophets. Each verse explicitly denies any likeness to God. Read through them and observe the recurring phrase 'not like' or 'none like'.
The verses are from Deuteronomy, 2 Samuel, 1 Chronicles, Job, Psalms, Isaiah, and Jeremiah.
3
Understand the Muslim division
After the list, Razi notes that Muslims who studied the Torah divided into two groups: one group held that the Torah anthropomorphizes God (tajsim), while the other group presumably interpreted those verses metaphorically. The text cuts off before completing the discussion.
Razi's own position is that the Torah, properly understood, supports tanzih, as the 16 verses show.
4
Reflect on the method
Consider why Razi uses biblical evidence. He is not endorsing the Torah as authoritative scripture, but showing that previous revelations also taught divine transcendence. This strengthens the Islamic argument.
This is a common approach in interfaith polemics: using the opponent's scripture to support one's own doctrine.

Commentary
This passage is a proof-by-scripture section where Imam Razi marshals evidence from the Torah and prophetic books (the Old Testament) to support the Islamic doctrine of divine transcendence (tanzih), namely that 'God is not like anything' (laysa kamithlihi shay'). The passage opens with a short quote from a Christian source (perhaps the Gospels or Church Fathers) about God being hidden from human senses, then moves to the Torah. Razi lists 16 biblical verses, each explicitly stating that there is no likeness to God. The verses are drawn from the Pentateuch (Deuteronomy), the historical books (2 Samuel, 1 Chronicles), the wisdom literature (Job, Psalms), and the major prophets (Isaiah, Jeremiah). After the list, Razi notes that Muslim scholars who engaged with the Torah on this topic split into two factions: the first group holds that the Torah anthropomorphizes God (tajsim) because some verses appear to describe God in human terms. The text cuts off before describing the second group, but the implication is that the second group interprets such verses metaphorically or dismisses them. Razi's own position is that the Torah, properly understood, affirms tanzih, as the verses he cites demonstrate. The passage thus serves both as a scriptural argument for tanzih and as an observation on the hermeneutical debates among Muslim scholars regarding previous scriptures.

Biblical Proofs of Divine Incomparability
Al-Razi lists eight more biblical verses affirming God's incomparability.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the Torah and prophetic books contain multiple explicit statements affirming that God is unlike anything.

Core Idea
Sixteen biblical verses declare that nothing is like God.

In this passage, Imam al-Razi presents a series of biblical quotations that directly affirm the principle of divine transcendence. These verses use rhetorical questions and declarations to assert that God has no likeness or equal. They form a scriptural foundation for the Islamic doctrine of tanzih.

Reflect on the cumulative evidence

Two Muslim Views on the Torah's Depiction of God
Describes two Muslim views on the Torah and affirms the correct one.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two Muslim factions' interpretations of the Torah regarding divine transcendence and why the author affirms the view that the Torah negates anthropomorphism.

Core Idea
The Torah's transcendent verses are decisive; anthropomorphic verses are open to interpretation.

In this lesson, we explore a nuanced discussion among Muslim scholars about how to understand the Torah's descriptions of God. Some scholars wrongly concluded that the Torah anthropomorphizes God, while others correctly argued that its transcendent texts are clear and definitive. The author sides with the latter, emphasizing that apparent anthropomorphic expressions in the Torah are similar to those in the Qur'an and should be interpreted in light of the unequivocal transcendent verses.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage with context
Read the Arabic passage once through, then the translation. Note that the author has previously cited sixteen transcendent texts from the Torah.
The passage builds on earlier discussion, so context matters.
2
Identify the two groups and their arguments
Find the two groups described. What does each group believe about the Torah? What evidence do they cite?
3
Understand why the second group's view is correct
The author gives a reason why the second group's position is correct. Identify that reason and reflect on its implications for interpreting scripture.
The key term is 'muhkamat' (unequivocal).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والذين كتبوا من المسلمين في الله وصفاته طبقا لنصوص التوراة . انقسموا إلى فريقين . فريق يرى : أن التوراة تجسم الله عز وجل . لأن فيها نصوص يدل ظاهرها على التجسيم ، وهؤلاء غفلوا عن نصوص مشابهة لها في القرآن . مثل : مكر الله وغضبه ومجيئه ، وقوله ، فليعلن ، وما شابه ذلك . وغفلوا أيضا عن نصوص التنزيه فيها ، النصوص التي أوردنا منها هنا ستة عشر نصا . وفريق يرى أن التوراة تنفى التجسيم - وقولهم هو الصحيح - لأن نصوص التنزيه فيها محكمة لا تحتمل غير ففي التشبيه والتمثيل .
And those who wrote among the Muslims about God and His attributes according to the texts of the Torah divided into two groups. A group holds that the Torah anthropomorphizes God Almighty, because in it there are texts whose apparent meaning indicates anthropomorphism, and these people neglected similar texts in the Qur'an, such as: the deception of God, His anger, His coming, and His saying 'let it be', and the like. And they also neglected the texts of transcendence in it, the texts of which we have cited sixteen here. And a group holds that the Torah negates anthropomorphism - and their saying is the correct one - because the texts of transcendence in it are unequivocal (muhkamat), not admitting other than [interpretation] of [?] comparison and likeness.
Source pages p. 285

Commentary
This passage explains how Muslim scholars have differed in their interpretation of the Torah's descriptions of God. The author identifies two groups. The first group mistakenly believes that the Torah anthropomorphizes God because of passages that apparently depict God in human terms. The author criticizes them for ignoring similar anthropomorphic expressions in the Qur'an (such as God's deception, anger, coming, and creative command 'Be') and for overlooking the clear transcendent texts, of which he has just cited sixteen. The second group correctly holds that the Torah actually negates anthropomorphism. Their view is correct because the transcendent texts in the Torah are 'muhkamat' (unequivocal and decisive) and do not admit alternative interpretations that would imply comparison or likeness to creation. Thus the author affirms the position that the Torah, like the Qur'an, ultimately upholds divine transcendence.

المحكم
المتشابه
التنزيه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Torah consistently depicts God in human form, so it anthropomorphizes God.
Correction: The author shows that the Torah contains both apparent anthropomorphic verses and clear transcendent verses; the latter are decisive and should be used to interpret the former, preserving divine transcendence.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is the second group's view (that the Torah negates anthropomorphism) considered correct?
A) Because the Torah has no anthropomorphic passages.
B) Because the transcendent texts in the Torah are unequivocal and do not admit other interpretations.
C) Because Muslim scholars unanimously agree.
D) Because the Qur'an explicitly says so.

Key Takeaway
The correct approach to scripture is to prioritize clear, unequivocal verses about God's transcendence over apparent anthropomorphic expressions, a principle applicable to both the Qur'an and previous scriptures.

?

In your own study of Islam, are there areas where you tend to emphasize certain verses or hadith while ignoring others that are clearer? Will you commit to seeking out the muhkam texts and letting them guide your understanding, even when it challenges your preconceptions?
?
If you truly believed that God is absolutely transcendent and incomparable, how would that change the way you speak about Him, think about worship, and relate to His attributes? What first step can you take today to ground your perception of God in the decisive transcendent verses?

Jewish and Muslim Scholars Affirming Divine Transcendence
The author cites Jewish scholars, especially Maimonides, who affirm divine transcendence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that both Muslim and Jewish scholars have contributed to affirming divine transcendence, and how Maimonides used rational arguments to reject anthropomorphism.

Core Idea
Affirming transcendence transcends religious boundaries.

This passage from Imam al-Razi's work presents evidence from both Muslim and Jewish sources that deny anthropomorphism. It first references the works of Rahmatullah al-Hindi and Ibn Hazm, then lists several Jewish scholars who explicitly affirm God's transcendence, culminating in a detailed quotation from Moses Maimonides. You will see how rational argumentation leads to the same conclusion across traditions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and note the two sections
The passage begins with Muslim scholars citing Torah verses, then moves to a list of Jewish scholars and Maimonides' arguments. Read it once to get the overall flow.
Pay attention to the names and the biblical references.
2
Identify the Jewish scholars listed and their stance
Find the names of Jewish scholars who negate anthropomorphism. They include Onkelos, Yonatan ben Uziel, Spinoza, Ibn Kamuna, and Maimonides. They explicitly affirm transcendence.
3
Examine Maimonides' explanation of anthropomorphic language in Torah
Maimonides says the Torah speaks in human language, using attributes for understanding. He argues that anthropomorphism stems from literalism, not reason.
This is a key hermeneutical principle.
4
Understand the rational principles Maimonides applies
He lists what must be negated from God: corporeality, passion, change, potentiality, resemblance. These are negated by clear proof.
This shows the use of rational theology.
5
Reflect on the biblical verses cited to support transcendence
Maimonides quotes Isaiah and Jeremiah that explicitly deny any likeness to God. These verses serve as scriptural proof.
Notice how he harmonizes reason and scripture.
