And Sheikh Rahmatullah al-Hindi worked in (Izhar al-Haqq) a chapter on the unequivocal (muhkam) and the ambiguous (mutashabih). And he mentioned from the texts of the Torah two verses that indicate transcendence. And Sheikh Ibn Hazm al-Andalusi mocked the Jewish scholars who did not pay attention to the verses that negate likeness and similarity, in his book 'al-Fasl fi al-Milal wa al-Ahwa' wa al-Nihal'. *** And scholars from the Children of Israel negate absolute anthropomorphism and anthropomorphism without 'how' (bila kayf) from God, and explicitly affirm transcendence. Among these are: Anhelos[?] the proselyte, and Yonatan ben Uziel, and Spinoza[?], and Ibn Kamuna, and Moses ben Maimon. Maimonides says: 'The Torah spoke about God in the language of the children of Adam - i.e., it described Him with human attributes so that the children of Adam could understand - and that the belief of anthropomorphism did not lead its adherents to anthropomorphism by rational consideration, but by following the apparent meanings of the texts of the books' (chapter 53). And Maimonides says: 'Everything that leads to corporeality, or that leads to passion and change, or that leads to non-existence - such as that He does not have something in actuality then becomes actual - or that leads to resemblance to any of His creatures, the negation of that from God is necessitated by clear proof.' And he explicitly stated in the books of the prophets the negation of comparison, saying: 'To whom will you liken Me and equal Me?' (Isaiah 40:25) and said: 'To whom will you liken God, and what likeness will you compare to Him?' (Isaiah 40:18) and said: 'There is none like You, O Lord' (Jeremiah 10:6). And this [؟] (chapter 55).
Source pages p. 286

Commentary
This passage discusses the contributions of both Muslim and Jewish scholars in affirming divine transcendence. First, it mentions Sheikh Rahmatullah al-Hindi's work 'Izhar al-Haqq', where he wrote a chapter on the unequivocal (muhkam) and ambiguous (mutashabih) verses and cited two Torah verses supporting transcendence. It also notes that Ibn Hazm al-Andalusi mocked Jewish scholars who failed to recognize the verses that deny any likeness or similarity to God. Then the author lists several Jewish scholars who, on the contrary, explicitly reject anthropomorphism—both absolute anthropomorphism (tajsim mutlaq) and anthropomorphism without modality (tajsim bila kayf)—and affirm transcendence. These include Onkelos the proselyte, Yonatan ben Uziel, Spinoza, Ibn Kamuna, and Moses Maimonides. The author then quotes Maimonides at length from his 'Guide for the Perplexed' (Dalalat al-Ha'irin). Maimonides explains that the Torah's anthropomorphic language is figurative, using human attributes for the sake of human understanding. He argues that the belief in anthropomorphism stems from following literal interpretations, not rational thought. He then asserts that anything implying corporeality, passion, change, potentiality, or resemblance to creatures must be negated from God by clear proof. Maimonides also cites biblical verses that explicitly negate comparison. The passage ends with a reference to a chapter (possibly chapter 55 of the same work).

Neglecting Tanzih Texts in the Quran
Critique of neglecting tanzih verses in Quran.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that some people neglect both anthropomorphic and transcendental verses in the Quran, and that the author has cited sixteen tanzih texts as evidence.

Core Idea
Tanzih texts are as much part of revelation as anthropomorphic ones.

This lesson examines a critique of those who selectively focus on anthropomorphic Quranic verses while ignoring the clear transcendental (tanzih) texts. The author points out that both categories are present in the Quran, and he has compiled sixteen tanzih texts to underscore the balance.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation once to get the overall meaning.
Pay attention to the list of anthropomorphic examples.
2
Identify the two categories
Notice that the author mentions two types of texts that are neglected: anthropomorphic (like makr, ghadab) and transcendental (tanzih).
3
Understand the author's reference
The author says he has cited sixteen tanzih texts earlier. This is a reminder that the Quran contains ample tanzih verses.
If you have the full book, you may look up those texts.
4
Reflect on the lesson
Consider why it is important to give equal weight to both types of verses when formulating theology.
Selective reading leads to imbalanced understanding.

Jewish Scholars and Maimonides on Divine Transcendence
List of Jewish scholars rejecting tajsim and quote from Maimonides on negating corporeality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that a tradition of Jewish scholars, including Maimonides, explicitly rejected corporealism (tajsim) and affirmed divine transcendence (tanzih) using rational arguments and scriptural interpretation.

Core Idea
Rational theology, not merely scripture, leads to negating corporeality from God.

This passage introduces a fascinating piece of interfaith theological history: Jewish scholars who, like their Muslim counterparts, insisted on God's absolute transcendence. It cites Maimonides, a towering figure in Jewish philosophy, who argues that belief in a corporeal God stems from literalist reading of scripture, not rational thought. He then provides a rational criterion and biblical proof texts for negating all forms of anthropomorphism.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Jewish scholars listed
Read through the names of the scholars mentioned. Note that they are presented as a tradition of tanzih within Judaism, countering the common assumption that all Jews believed in a corporeal God.
The OCR may have corrupted some names; focus on the overall point.
2
Understand Maimonides' critique of tajsim
Maimonides argues that belief in tajsim is not based on reason but on following the apparent meanings of scripture. This is a key rationalist move: scripture must be interpreted allegorically when it seems to imply corporeality.
3
Learn Maimonides' rational criterion
He states that anything implying corporeality, passivity, change, potentiality, or resemblance to creatures must be negated by clear proof. This is a foundational principle for any rational theology of divine transcendence.
This criterion parallels Islamic kalam arguments.
4
Reflect on the biblical verses
Maimonides cites three verses from the Prophets that explicitly negate any likeness to God. Notice how he uses scripture to support a rational, non-corporeal understanding of God.

وعلماء من بني إسرائيل ينفون التجسيم المطلق والتجسيم بلا كيف، عن الله ، ويصرحون بالتنزيه. ومن هؤلاء : أنه ولوس المتهود ، و يوناتان بن عازيائيل ، وسيبنوزا ، وابن كمونة، وموسى بن ميمون يقول ابن ميمون المتوفى سنة ٦٠٢ه فى, دلالة الحائرين، : إن التوراة تكلمت عن الله بلسان بني آدم - أى وصفته بصفات البشر ليفهم بنو آدم - وأن معتقد التجسيم لم يدعه إلى التجسيم نظر عقلى، بل تبع ظواهر نصوص الكتب (فصل ۵۳) ويقول ابن ميمون : إن كل ما يؤدى إلى الجسمانية ، أو ما يؤدى لانفعال و تغير ، أو ما يؤدى لعدم - مثل أن لا يكون له شيء بالفعل ثم يصير بالفعل - أو ما يؤدى لشبه شي. من مخلوقاته . يلزم نفيه من الله بالبرهان الواضح . و قد صرح في كتب الأنبياء بنفى التشبيه ، فقال : ؟ فيمن تشبهو ننى فأساويه ، أش ٤٠ : ٢٥ ) وقال : لا فيمن تشبهون الله، وأى شبه تعادلونه ( به ، ؟ ( أ ش ٤٠ : ١٨ ( وقال : « إنه لا نظير لك يارب ، ( إ ر ٦:١٠ ) وهذا كتبر ( فصل ٥٥ )
And scholars from the Children of Israel negate absolute corporealism (tajsim mutlaq) and corporealism without modality (tajsim bi-la kayf) from Allah, and they explicitly declare transcendence (tanzih). And among these are: Androlus al-Mutahawwid, and Yonatan ben Uziel, and Sibinza, and Ibn Kamuna, and Musa bin Maimun. Ibn Maimun, who died in the year 602 AH, says in 'Dalalat al-Ha'irin': 'Indeed, the Torah spoke about Allah in the language of the sons of Adam – i.e., it described Him with human attributes so that the sons of Adam would understand – and that the belief in corporealism (tajsim) was not called to corporealism by rational consideration, but rather followed the apparent meanings of the texts of the books (Chapter 53).' And Ibn Maimun says: 'Everything that leads to corporeality, or that leads to passivity and change, or that leads to non-existence – such as that He does not have something in actuality then becomes in actuality – or that leads to resemblance to any of His creatures, it is necessary to negate it from Allah by clear proof (al-burhan al-wadih).' And he explicitly stated in the books of the Prophets the negation of likeness (tashbih): He said: 'To whom will you liken Me that I should be equal?' (Isaiah 40:25) and said: 'To whom will you liken God, and what likeness will you compare to Him?' (Isaiah 40:18) and said: 'There is no one like You, O Lord' (Jeremiah 10:6). And this is a book (Chapter 55).
Source pages p. 286

Commentary
This passage lists Jewish scholars who explicitly negate both absolute corporealism (tajsim mutlaq) and corporealism without modality (tajsim bi-la kayf) from God, and declare tanzih. The names include Androlus al-Mutahawwid, Yonatan ben Uziel, Sibinza, Ibn Kamuna, and Musa bin Maimun (Maimonides). It then quotes Maimonides from his work 'Dalalat al-Ha'irin' (Guide for the Perplexed). Maimonides states that the Torah spoke of God in human language, describing Him with human attributes for human understanding. He argues that belief in corporealism is not based on rational inquiry but on following the apparent meanings of scriptural texts. Maimonides further asserts that anything implying corporeality, passivity, change, potentiality (like not having something in act then becoming in act), or resemblance to any creature must be negated from God by clear proof (burhan wadih). He then cites three biblical verses from Isaiah and Jeremiah that negate tashbih. The passage ends with a reference to a book or chapter (Chapter 55).

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All Jewish scholars historically believed in a corporeal God.
Correction: This passage shows that a significant tradition of Jewish scholars, including Maimonides, explicitly rejected corporealism and affirmed divine transcendence, using rational arguments and allegorical interpretation of scripture.

Checkpoint
According to Maimonides, what is the cause of the belief in tajsim?
A) Rational demonstration.
B) Following the apparent meanings of scriptural texts.
C) Philosophical speculation.
D) Cultural influence from Greeks.

Key Takeaway
Maimonides and other Jewish scholars demonstrate that rational theology and allegorical interpretation lead to the same conclusion as Islamic kalam: God is absolutely transcendent and free from any resemblance to creation.

?

?
Are there any beliefs you hold about God that you've accepted simply because 'the text says so' without considering the rational implications? What would it cost you to apply Maimonides' test of negating anything that implies imperfection, and will you commit to doing so today?
?
Maimonides argues that rational proof must override literal readings when they lead to corporealism. Is there an area in your faith where you resist rational scrutiny? What attribute of God are you most hesitant to think about rationally, and why? Will you take time this week to study it with both scripture and reason?

Agreement on Tawhid, Difference in Shari'a
Argument that Muslims and Jews differ only in law, not in tawhid and tanzih.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that according to Imam Razi, Muslims and Jews share core beliefs in tawhid and tanzih, and their only disagreement is in the shari'a (law).

Core Idea
Muslims and Jews agree on God's oneness and transcendence; their difference is only in the law.

Imam Razi presents a striking argument: despite historical conflicts, Muslims and Jews fundamentally agree on the most important theological principles—tawhid and tanzih. He challenges the assumption that their disagreement is about God Himself, redirecting it to the realm of divine law. This lesson examines his reasoning and the Quranic evidence he provides.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice how Razi poses a rhetorical question to frame his argument.
The passage uses Quranic verses as evidence; pay attention to how they support his claim.
2
Identify the shared beliefs
List the beliefs that Razi says Muslims and Jews share: belief in God, the Last Day, prophethood, and revelation.
This establishes common ground before introducing the difference.
3
Understand the difference in shari'a
Notice how Razi explains that the only difference is which shari'a to follow—Moses' or Muhammad's—and why each group holds its position.
He emphasizes that it's not about tawhid but about which law God commands.
4
Reflect on the Quranic evidence
Consider the two Quranic verses cited (3:64 and 3:70) and how they reinforce Razi's point about agreement on fundamentals and disagreement on signs.
Al-Qurtubi's tafsir clarifies that 'while you testify' refers to acknowledging similar signs from earlier prophets.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و أي فرق بيننا يجن المسلمين، وبين اليهود . إذا كنا جميعا متفقين على ا التوحيد ٢ - وعلى التنزيه ؟ لا فرق بين المسلمين و بين اليهود في توحيد الله عز وجل وفي تنزيهه عن المشابهة للحوادت . لأن اليهودي يؤمن بالله الذي يؤمر به المسلم ، ويؤمن باليوم الآخر كما يؤمن به المسلم، ويؤمن بالنبوات وبالموحي من السماء كما يؤمن به المسلم. وإنما الفرق بين المسلمين وبين اليهود هو أن اليهود قالوا سنتقرب إلى الله بشريعة موسى عليه السلام - وهى التوراة - وان نتقرب إليه بشريعة محمد في المسلمين، لأننا لسنا متأكدين من نبوته . وأن المسلمين قالوا : نحن نؤمن بنبوة موسى عليه السلام: ولكن لن نتقرب إلى الله بشريعة موسى . لأن نبوة محمد قد ثبتت عندنا ، وقد أمرنا الله - على لسانه ـ أن لا تعمل بشريعة موسى . فالخلاف بين المسلمين و بين اليهود هو في الشريعة وليس في التوحيد وفي التنزيه . فقد قال الله تعالى : « قل : يا أهل الكتاب . تعالوا إلى كلمة سواء بيننا وبينكم : الا تعبد إلا الله ، ولا نشرك به شيئا ، ولا يتخذ بعضنا بعضا أربابا من دون الله .. فإن تولوا فقولوا : اشهدوا بأنا مسلمون ، ( آل عمران ٦٤ ) وقد شهد القرآن بأن اليهود يكفرون بآيات الله ، ولا يكفرون بالله . في قوله تعالى: يا أهل الكتاب لم تكبرون بآيات الله وأنتم تشهدون؟ ( (آل عمران (۷۰) أى تكفرون بالقرآن ودلائل نبوة الرسول وأنتم تشهدون» نعته في الكتابين ، أى فى التوراة وفى الإنجيل . وفي تفسير القرطبي : ه وأنتم تشهدون ، بمثلها من آيات الأنبياء التي أنتم مقرون بها ،
And what difference is between us [؟] Muslims and the Jews, if we all agree on tawhid and on tanzih? There is no difference between Muslims and Jews in the tawhid of Allah Almighty and in declaring Him transcendent of resemblance to contingent things. Because the Jew believes in the God that the Muslim is commanded to [believe in], and believes in the Last Day as the Muslim believes in it, and believes in prophecies and in what is revealed from heaven as the Muslim believes in it. The only difference between Muslims and Jews is that the Jews said: 'We will draw near to Allah through the shari'a of Moses, peace be upon him – which is the Torah – and we will not draw near to Him through the shari'a of Muhammad, because we are not certain of his prophethood.' And the Muslims said: 'We believe in the prophethood of Moses, peace be upon him, but we will not draw near to Allah through the shari'a of Moses, because the prophethood of Muhammad has been established with us, and Allah – upon his tongue – commanded us not to act according to the shari'a of Moses.' So the disagreement between Muslims and Jews is in the shari'a, not in tawhid and tanzih. For Allah Almighty said: 'Say: O People of the Book, come to a common word between us and you: that we worship none but Allah, and that we associate nothing with Him, and that none of us take others as lords besides Allah. Then if they turn away, say: Bear witness that we are Muslims' (Quran 3:64). And the Quran has testified that the Jews disbelieve in the signs of Allah, but do not disbelieve in Allah. In His statement: 'O People of the Book, why do you disbelieve in the signs of Allah while you testify?' (Quran 3:70) meaning you disbelieve in the Quran and the proofs of the prophethood of the Messenger, while you testify to his description in the two Books, i.e., in the Torah and the Gospel. And in the tafsir of al-Qurtubi: 'while you testify' to similar signs of the prophets that you acknowledge.
Source pages p. 287

Commentary
This passage argues that there is no difference between Muslims and Jews in matters of tawhid (the oneness of God) and tanzih (transcendence). The author poses a rhetorical question: what difference exists if both agree? He answers that the Jew believes in the same God, the Last Day, prophecies, and revelation. The only difference is in the shari'a (law): Jews adhere to the Torah and are not certain of Muhammad's prophethood, while Muslims believe in Moses but follow Muhammad's shari'a because his prophethood is established and God commanded through him to abandon the Mosaic law. Thus, the disagreement is about law, not about tawhid or tanzih. The author then cites Quran 3:64 (the 'common word' verse) to emphasize shared belief in worshipping God alone. He also cites Quran 3:70, which rebukes the People of the Book for disbelieving in God's signs (the Quran) while they testify to the Prophet's description in their scriptures. He concludes with a quote from al-Qurtubi's tafsir explaining that 'while you testify' means they acknowledge similar signs from previous prophets.

Correction: Al-Razi argues they agree on tawhid and tanzih; the difference is only in the shari'a.

A) Belief in the Last Day.

Key Takeaway
Muslims and Jews share fundamental theological beliefs; their only disagreement is over which divine law to follow.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

The Divine Name in the Torah: YHWH and I Am that I Am
Imam Razi expounds on the divine name YHWH and the meaning of 'I Am that I Am' as indicating necessary existence and unity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the significance of the divine name revealed to Moses and its theological implications for divine unity and necessary existence.

Core Idea
The name 'I Am that I Am' identifies God's essence with His attributes, affirming absolute simplicity.

Imam Razi takes us into the scriptural roots of tawhid by examining the divine name given to Moses. This passage connects the Torah narrative with kalam’s central claim: that Allah is necessarily existent and utterly simple. You will see how a biblical phrase becomes a profound statement about the nature of God.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text once, noting the key terms: يهوه, أهيه الذي أهيه, الكائن, واجب الوجود. Then read the translation to grasp the full story.
The passage refers to Exodus 3:14-15.
2
Identify the two main parts
The passage first discusses the name YHWH and how Jewish scribes write it, then recounts the revelation to Moses and interprets 'I Am that I Am'. Recognize this shift from external form to inner meaning.
3
Focus on the interpretation
The author explains that the repetition 'Ehyeh asher Ehyeh' shows that the described subject and the description are one—God and His attributes are identical. This is a key theological point.
Reflect on how this differs from understanding God as having multiple distinct attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما عن اسم الله، عز وجل في كتاب التوراة، فهو يهوه، وهو الذي يقابل كلمة الله، في القرآن الكريم. ويكتب أحيانا "أهوه"، وعلماء بني إسرائيل يخافون من كتابته، ككلمة متصلة الحروف، من هيبتهم الله وخشيتهم منه. ومن أجل خوفهم يكتبونه في كتبهم بحروف مقطعة. هكذا: ياء - واو - هاء. وهذا الاسم هو الذي نطق به الله أمام موسى عليه السلام، فإن الله لما قال لموسى: اذهب فأرسلك إلى فرعون، وتخرج شعبي بني إسرائيل من مصر، قال له موسى: وها أنا آتي إلى بني إسرائيل، وأقول لهم: إله آبائكم أرسلني إليكم. فإذا قالوا لي: ما اسمه؟ فماذا أقول لهم؟ فقال الله لموسى: أهيه الذي أهيه. وقال: هكذا تقول لبني إسرائيل: أهيه أرسلني إليكم. وقال الله أيضا لموسى: هكذا تقول لبني إسرائيل: أهيه أرسلني إليكم. وقال الله أيضا لموسى: وإله إبراهيم وإله إسحاق وإله يعقوب أرسلني إليكم (خروج 3: 10-15). وأهيه الذي أهيه، معناه: أنا هو الكائن. والكائن اسم مشتق من هيه، وهيه معناها: كان. والسر في تكرير "أهيه" مرتين: أنه يريد أن يبين لهم: أن الموصوف هو الصفة بعينها، أي الله وصفاته كشيء واحد. فكأنه يريد أن يقول: أنا الموجود الذي هو الموجود، أو الموجود الذي هو واجب الوجود.
As for the name of God, Almighty, in the book of the Torah, it is YHWH, and it corresponds to the word 'God' in the Noble Qur'an. And it is sometimes written 'Ahwah', and the scholars of the Children of Israel fear to write it as a word with connected letters, out of their awe of God and their fear of Him. And because of their fear they write it in their books with separated letters, thus: Yaa - Waw - Haa. And this is the name which God uttered before Moses, peace be upon him. For when God said to Moses: 'Go, I send you to Pharaoh, and bring out my people the Children of Israel from Egypt,' Moses said to Him: 'Here I come to the Children of Israel and say to them: the God of your fathers has sent me to you. Then if they say to me: what is His name? What shall I say to them?' So God said to Moses: 'I am that I am.' And He said: 'Thus you shall say to the Children of Israel: I am has sent me to you.' And God also said to Moses: 'Thus you shall say to the Children of Israel: I am has sent me to you.' And God also said to Moses: 'And the God of Abraham, the God of Isaac, and the God of Jacob has sent me to you' (Exodus 3:10-15). And 'I am that I am' means: 'I am the Existing One.' And 'the Existing One' is a name derived from 'hayah', and 'hayah' means 'he was'. And the secret in repeating 'I am' twice is that He wants to show them that the described being is the description itself, that is, God and His attributes are one thing. As if He means to say: 'I am the Existent who is the Existent,' or 'the Existent who is the Necessarily Existent.'
Source pages p. 288

Commentary
This passage is an exposition of the divine name in the Torah. The author identifies the name as YHWH, corresponding to 'Allah' in the Quran, and notes that Jewish scholars write it with separated letters out of reverence. He then recounts the Exodus narrative where God reveals the name 'Ehyeh asher Ehyeh' ('I am that I am') to Moses. The author interprets this name: 'Ehyeh' means 'I am the Existing One', derived from the Hebrew verb 'hayah' ('to be'). The repetition indicates that the subject (God) is identical with the predicate (His existence and attributes); there is no composition in God. The author concludes that God is the Existent that is identical with existence itself, i.e., the Necessarily Existent. This is a profound statement about divine simplicity and necessity.

أهيه الذي أهيه
الكائن
واجب الوجود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the name 'I Am that I Am' merely affirms that God exists, without deeper theological significance.
Correction: The author shows that the repetition indicates the unity of God's essence and attributes, pointing to necessary existence and divine simplicity.

Checkpoint

A) It emphasizes God's power.
B) It indicates that the described being is identical with the description.
C) It shows God's eternity.
D) It is a poetic repetition.

Key Takeaway
The divine name 'I Am that I Am' reveals that God's essence, existence, and attributes are perfectly one, making Him the Necessarily Existent.

The Names YHWH and Adonai: Two Dimensions of Tawhid
The author distinguishes between YHWH as the name for divine unity and Adonai as the name for lordship.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between tawhid al-uluhiyya and tawhid al-rububiyya as illustrated by the divine names YHWH and Adonai.

Core Idea
The names YHWH and Adonai reveal two dimensions of tawhid: oneness of divinity and oneness of lordship.

In this passage, Imam al-Razi explores the relationship between divine names and tawhid. He explains that while many of God's names are shared with creation, the names YHWH and Adonai uniquely point to two fundamental aspects of Islamic monotheism. By understanding their distinction, we grasp that tawhid is not a single concept but encompasses both the oneness of God's essence and the oneness of His lordship.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the English translation. Notice how the author moves from general to specific.
The author uses the example of al-Rahim to illustrate shared names.
2
Locate the two key names
Find the names YHWH and Adonai in the passage. What does the author say each signifies?
YHWH is a proper name for the essence; Adonai means 'the Lord'.
3
Understand the two tawhids
The passage introduces two types of tawhid: tawhid al-uluhiyya and tawhid al-rububiyya. Reflect on how each name corresponds to one type.
YHWH corresponds to tawhid of divinity, Adonai to tawhid of lordship.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأسماء الله الحسنى، غير اسم الله، تدل على أسماء من صفات، فيها اشتراك بين الله الخالق وبين المخلوقين، ولكنها في "الله" أكمل منها في المخلوقين. فالرحيم وهو اسم من أسماء الله الحسنى قد يطلق على غير الله. فيقال رجل رحيم. ويطلق على الله دلالة على أنه الكامل في الرحمة. ومن أسماء الله الحسنى عند بني إسرائيل، وهو الاسم الثاني بعد "يهوه": أدوناى، ويترجم في اللغة العربية بالرب. ففي يهوه توحيد الألوهية، وفي أدوناي توحيد الربوبية. والفرق بينهما: أن يهوه علم على الذات.
And the Most Beautiful Names of God, other than the name 'Allah', indicate names derived from attributes, in which there is sharing between God the Creator and the created beings, but they are in 'Allah' more complete than in the created beings. For example, 'al-Rahim' (the Merciful) is one of the Most Beautiful Names of God and may be used for other than God; one says 'a merciful man', and it is applied to God to indicate that He is the Perfect in mercy. And among the Most Beautiful Names of God among the Children of Israel, and it is the second name after 'YHWH': 'Adonai', and it is translated in the Arabic language as 'al-Rabb' (the Lord). For in YHWH there is tawhid al-uluhiyya (oneness of divinity), and in Adonai there is tawhid al-rububiyya (oneness of lordship). And the difference between them is that YHWH is a proper name for the essence.
Source pages p. 288

Commentary
This passage is a discussion of divine names and their implications for tawhid. The author begins by noting that many of God's names (e.g., 'al-Rahim') are shared between God and creation, but in God they are perfect. He then introduces the Jewish name 'Adonai' as the second name after YHWH, meaning 'the Lord'. He explains that YHWH signifies tawhid al-uluhiyya (the oneness of divinity—God as the sole object of worship) while Adonai signifies tawhid al-rububiyya (the oneness of lordship—God as the sole creator and sustainer). The difference is that YHWH is a proper name for the essence itself, whereas Adonai refers to God's attribute of lordship. This distinction highlights two aspects of Islamic tawhid: belief in the uniqueness of God's essence and the uniqueness of His lordship.

Key Terms
أسماء الله الحسنى
أدوناى
توحيد الألوهية
توحيد الربوبية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Tawhid is a single concept without internal distinction.
Correction: Tawhid includes two dimensions: oneness of divinity (tawhid al-uluhiyya) and oneness of lordship (tawhid al-rububiyya).

Checkpoint
What is the difference between the names YHWH and Adonai according to the author?
A) YHWH indicates mercy, Adonai indicates justice.
B) YHWH indicates oneness of divinity, Adonai indicates oneness of lordship.
C) YHWH is used by Muslims, Adonai by Jews.
D) YHWH is a description, Adonai is a proper name.

Key Takeaway
The names YHWH and Adonai teach us that tawhid has two aspects: the oneness of God's essence (divinity) and the oneness of His lordship.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly worship God alone, or do you sometimes seek the approval of people more than His pleasure, forgetting the tawhid al-uluhiyya this lesson teaches?
?
When you worry about your provision or future, do you act as if God is not the sole Lord and Provider, thus undermining the tawhid al-rububiyya that YHWH and Adonai together affirm?

Divine Transcendence
intermediate
6 min
The Claim of a Hidden Greatest Name
Maimonides explains the uniqueness of YHWH and the secret transmission of names.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Jewish tradition about the hidden greatest name as presented by Maimonides and the Talmud, and how it entered Muslim circles through occult texts.

Core Idea
The hidden greatest name is a Jewish esoteric tradition, not a secret Islamic power.

This lesson introduces the claim of a hidden 'greatest name' of God, as presented by Moses Maimonides and Jewish Talmudic tradition. We will see how the Tetragrammaton (YHWH) was considered unique, and how later substitute names were circulated secretly. This tradition eventually seeped into Muslim writings through magic books, leading some to believe in a magical hidden name. The author presents this background before refuting it.

Guided Path
1
Read Maimonides' classification
Read the passage where Maimonides explains that all divine names are derived from actions except YHWH, which is a proper name indicating God's essence alone.
Notice the distinction between derived names and the proper name.
2
Understand the secrecy of YHWH
The author describes how the pronunciation of YHWH was transmitted secretly among Jewish scholars, only taught to worthy students once a week.
This secrecy is part of the claim of a hidden name.
3
Learn about substitute names
Later, 12-letter and 42-letter names were developed and reserved for the pious, as mentioned in the Talmud.
These substitute names are part of the esoteric tradition that influenced Muslim magic texts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الإلهية ولايسم به غيره، وأن أدوناى يطلق على الرب عز وجل، ويطلق على السيد العظيم أيضا. يقول موسى ابن ميمون: وجميع أسمائه تعالى الموجودة في الكتب كلها مشتقة من الأفعال. وهذا ما لا خفاء به إلا اسم واحد، وهو: الياء والهاء والواو والهاء (يهوه) فإنه اسم مرتجل له - تعالى - ولذلك سمي الاسم الأعظم. ومعناه: أنه يدل على ذاته - تعالى - دلالة بيئة، لا اشتراك فيها. أما سائر أسمائه المعظمة، فتدل باشتراك، لكونها مشتقة من أفعال يوجد مثلها لنا - كما بينا - حتى أن الاسم المكنى به من الألف والدال والنون والياء في (آدنى) هو أيضا مشتق من السيادة. مثل: وقد خاطبنا الرجل سيد الأرض، (لك ٤٢: ٣٠)
ويقول ابن ميمون: إن اسم يهوه لأنه لم يكن معلوما عند كل أحد كيف ينطق به. كان أهل العلم يتناقلون صفة النطق به، ولا يعلمونه لأحد، إلا لتلميذ مستأهل، مرة واحدة في الأسبوع، وكان لدى أهل العلم اسم معادل ليهوه، مكون من اثني عشر حرفا في أكثر من كلمة. ما كان ممنوعا ولا مضمونا به على أحد. ولما صار أقوام سفهاء، يتعلمون هذا الاسم، ذا الاثني عشر حرفا، ويجدفون به على الله، اضطر أهل العلم إلى إعطائه للحصفاء فقط، وإلى كتمانه عن العامة. وكان لدى أهل العلم بعد ذلك: اسم معادل ليهوه مكون من اثنين وأربعين حرفا في أكثر من كلمة. جاء عنه في التلمود: «اسم ذو اثنين وأربعين حرفا، قدسي ومقدس، ويعطى فقط للحصيف، الذي وصل إلى نصف عمره، وليس عرضة للغضب ولا للسكر ولا يثير طعنا في أخلاقه، ويتكلم مع الناس بهدوة. - وكل من يعلمه فهو حريص عليه ويحفظه في طهارة - ومحبوب لمن فوقه، ومحمود لمن دونه، ومهيب عند الناس، وتعلمه قائم في يده. وارث العالمين: هذا العالم وعالم الآخرة، (قد وشيم ١٧١)
وهو الاسم الأعظم، يترجم في العبرانية: «شم همفورش»، وقد دخل همفورش في كتب المسلمين عن طريق كتب السحر والطلسمات ودخل في الكتب أن لله اسما إذا دعى به أجاب، وإذا سئل به أعطى. هو الاسم الأعظم. وهذا لا أصل له: فلا اسم أعظم إلا «يهوه» عند بني إسرائيل المعادل لاسم الله، عند المسلمين. والله يجيب المضطر إذا دعاه باسمه المتعارف عليه، لا بما خفي من أسمائه في كتب السحر.
وقد ترجمت التوراة إلى اللغة العربية. وفيها أن الخالق الأرض والسموات هو الله رب العالمين. كما في القرآن الكريم، ويكتبون «الله» بدل يهوه، فأول آيات التوراة نصها: «في البدء خلق الله السموات والأرض، وكانت الأرض خربة وخالية وعلى وجه الغمر ظلمة، وريح الله يرف على وجه المياه. وقال الله: ليكن نور فكان نور، ورأى الله النور أنه حسن، وفصل الله بين النور والظلمة، ودعا الله النور نهارا، والظلمة دعاها ليلا وكان صباح يوما واحدا» (تك ١: ١-٥)
وقد ترجم الزبور إلى اللغة العربية. وفيه يتحدث داود عليه السلام عن الله عز وجل باسم الله، لا باسم يهوه، فيقول: «إن فاحص القلوب والكلى: الله البار. ترسي عند الله، مخلص مستقيمي القلوب. الله قاض عادل،» (مز ٧: ٩-١١)
وقد ترجم الإنجيل إلى اللغة العربية. وفيه يتحدث عيسى عليه السلام
Al-ilahiyyah (divinity) and it is not named by it other than Him, and that Adonai is applied to the Lord, Mighty and Majestic, and is also applied to the great lord. Moses ibn Maimon says: All His names, exalted, found in the books are all derived from actions. This is something with no concealment except one name, which is: Yod, He, Waw, He (YHWH), for it is a proper name for Him - exalted - and therefore it is called the Greatest Name. Its meaning: it indicates His essence - exalted - with a clear indication, with no association. As for the rest of His exalted names, they indicate with association, because they are derived from actions whose like exists for us - as we have shown - even the name denoted by Alif, Dal, Nun, Ya in (Adonai) is also derived from lordship. For example: we have addressed a man as 'master of the land' (Gen 42:30). And Ibn Maimon says: The name YHWH, because it was not known to everyone how to pronounce it. The people of knowledge used to transmit the manner of pronouncing it, and they would not teach it to anyone except to a worthy student once a week. And the people of knowledge had a name equivalent to YHWH, composed of twelve letters in more than one word. It was not forbidden nor guaranteed to anyone. And when foolish people came, learning this name, the one of twelve letters, and blaspheming with it against God, the people of knowledge were forced to give it only to the wise [al-husafa?] and to conceal it from the common people. And afterwards the people of knowledge had a name equivalent to YHWH composed of forty-two letters in more than one word. It is mentioned in the Talmud: 'A name of forty-two letters, holy and sanctified, and is given only to the prudent one who has reached half his age, and is not prone to anger nor to drunkenness nor does he stir up dispute in his character, and speaks with people gently. And whoever knows it is careful with it and preserves it in purity – and is loved by those above him, praised by those below him, and awe-inspiring among people, and his learning stands in his hand. He inherits the two worlds: this world and the world to come' (Kidushin 171). And it is the Greatest Name, translated in Hebrew: 'Shem HaMeforash'. And 'HaMeforash' entered the books of Muslims through the books of magic and talismans. And it entered the books that God has a name which, if called by, He answers, and if asked by, He gives. It is the Greatest Name. And this has no basis: for there is no Greatest Name except 'YHWH' among the Children of Israel, equivalent to the name 'Allah' among Muslims. And Allah answers the distressed when he calls Him by His known name, not by what is hidden of His names in books of magic. And the Torah has been translated into Arabic. In it, the Creator of the earth and heavens is Allah, Lord of the worlds. As in the Noble Quran, and they write 'Allah' instead of YHWH. The first verses of the Torah are: 'In the beginning God created the heavens and the earth; and the earth was waste and empty, and darkness was upon the face of the deep, and the Spirit of God hovered upon the face of the waters. And God said, Let there be light, and there was light. And God saw the light, that it was good; and God separated between the light and the darkness. And God called the light day, and the darkness he called night. And there was evening and there was morning, one day' (Gen 1:1-5). And the Psalms have been translated into Arabic. In them, David, peace be upon him, speaks about God, Mighty and Majestic, with the name Allah, not with the name YHWH. He says: 'For the examiner of hearts and kidneys: Allah is righteous. My shield is with Allah, the savior of the upright of heart. Allah is a just judge' (Psalms 7:9-11). And the Gospel has been translated into Arabic. In it, Jesus, peace be upon him, speaks...
Source pages p. 289

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the idea that there exists a hidden, magical 'greatest name' of God that guarantees immediate answers. The author begins by citing Moses Maimonides’ classification of divine names: all names are derived from actions except the Tetragrammaton (YHWH), which is a proper name indicating God’s essence with no sharing. The author then explains how the pronunciation of YHWH was transmitted secretly among Jewish scholars, and how they later developed a 12-letter substitute and then a 42-letter name mentioned in the Talmud as reserved for the pious. These esoteric names became associated with the 'Shem HaMeforash' (the explicit name). The author notes that this concept entered Muslim books through magic and talisman traditions, claiming a hidden name by which God can be compelled. The author then refutes this: there is no such hidden name; the only 'greatest name' for Jews is YHWH, which is equivalent to 'Allah' for Muslims—both are well-known, revealed names. God answers the caller by His known name, not by secret names. To substantiate this, the author points to the Arabic translations of the Torah, Psalms, and Gospel, where the name used for God is consistently 'Allah', not YHWH. For example, Genesis 1 uses 'Allah', Psalm 7 uses 'Allah', and the Gospel (implied) also uses 'Allah'. Thus, the author argues that the true greatest name is not hidden but is the common name 'Allah', which is accessible to all believers and is the name used in all revealed scriptures.

Key Terms
الاسم الأعظم
يهوه
شم همفورش
أدوناى
التلمود
موسى ابن ميمون

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Maimonides, what is unique about the name YHWH?
A) It is the longest name of God.
B) It is a proper name not derived from an action.
C) It is the only name that can be pronounced.
D) It is a name shared with other beings.

Key Takeaway
The Jewish tradition of a hidden greatest name is an esoteric practice, not a secret power for Muslims, and its entry into Muslim circles came through occult texts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have been taught the names of Allah, but do you ever use them carelessly? When you say 'Ya Allah' in your daily life, do you feel the weight of addressing the Lord of the worlds? How can you make every mention of His name a moment of profound reverence starting today?
?
The 42-letter name was only given to those not prone to anger. When you are angry, how does your speech about God change? Commit to one specific situation this week where you will pause before speaking about Him, so that your words are not polluted by your emotion.
?
The people of knowledge concealed the name to prevent blasphemy. Do you protect the honor of Allah's names by avoiding jest or idle talk about sacred matters? What would it cost you to delete one careless post or stop one joke that diminishes His greatness?

Divine Transcendence
intermediate

Refutation: The True Greatest Name is Known
The author refutes the existence of a secret hidden name and affirms that Allah is the name used in all scriptures.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's refutation of the hidden greatest name by showing that the name 'Allah' is used in all revealed scriptures and is the true greatest name.

Core Idea
The real greatest name is 'Allah', known to all, not hidden or magical.

In this lesson, we see the author's decisive refutation. After presenting the Jewish tradition, he argues that there is no hidden name that compels God's answer. Instead, the only 'greatest name' is YHWH for Jews, equivalent to 'Allah' for Muslims. To prove this, he cites the Arabic translations of the Torah, Psalms, and Gospel, where the name used is consistently 'Allah'. Thus, the greatest name is not hidden but revealed and accessible to all believers.

Guided Path
1
Read the author's rejection
Read the passage where the author states 'وهذا لا أصل له' (This has no basis) and explains that there is no hidden greatest name.
This is the core claim of the refutation.
2
Understand the equivalence
The author asserts that the only greatest name is YHWH for Jews, equivalent to Allah for Muslims.
Both are known names, not hidden.
3
Examine scriptural evidence
Look at the examples from the Torah, Psalms, and Gospel translations where 'Allah' is used.
This shows that the name Allah is the common name of God across scriptures.
4
Conclude with the author's point
God answers the caller by His known name, not by secret names from magic books.
This reinforces the idea that there is no magical hidden name.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Have you ever been tempted to think that knowing some 'secret name' would give you power? Today, the author shows that the greatest name is the one you already know: Allah. Will you commit to calling upon Him only by the names He has revealed, without seeking shortcuts?

Divine Transcendence
beginner
4 min
Origin of the Hidden Greatest Name Claim
Identifies the hidden name claim and traces its origin.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the claim of a hidden greatest name of Allah entered Muslim literature through Hebrew magic books and talismans.

Core Idea
The hidden greatest name is a borrowed idea from Hebrew sorcery, not from Islam.

Many Muslims have heard of a hidden 'greatest name' that guarantees answered prayers. This lesson traces the origin of this belief to Hebrew magic books and shows how it entered Islamic literature. The author begins by identifying the name in question.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage about the name's origin
Read the first part of the passage that gives the Hebrew term 'Shem HaMeforash' and states how it entered Muslim books via magic and talismans.
Notice the phrase 'دخل في الكتب' indicating entry into books.
2
Identify the claim made about the name
The passage says that it is claimed that Allah has a name which, if called upon, He answers and grants requests.
This is the claim that will be refuted in the next lesson.
3
Reflect on the source of the claim
Ask yourself: Why is it important to know that this idea came through magic books?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وه الاسم الأعظم، يترجم فى العبرانية : « شم همفورش ، وقد دخل همفورش ، فى كتب المسلمين عن طريق كتب السحر والطلسمات ودخل في الكتب أن لله اسما إذا دعى به أجاب, وإذا سئل به أعطى . هو الاسم الأعظم. . وهذا لا أصل له : فلا اسم أعظم إلا «يهوه، عند بني إسرائيل المعادل لاسم الله ، عند المسلمين والله يجيب المضطر إذا دعاه باسمه المتعارف عليه ، لا بما خفى من أسمائه في كتب السحر
This is the greatest name, translated in Hebrew as 'Shem HaMeforash', and HaMeforash has entered the books of Muslims through the books of magic and talismans, and it entered the books that Allah has a name if called by it He answers, and if asked by it He gives. It is the greatest name. This has no basis: there is no greater name except 'Yahweh' among the Children of Israel, equivalent to the name 'Allah' among Muslims. And Allah answers the distressed if he calls Him by His well-known name, not by what is hidden of His names in magic books.
Source pages p. 290

Commentary
This passage refutes the claim that there is a hidden 'greatest name' of Allah that grants wishes, a notion that entered Muslim circles through sorcery and talismans. The author states that this idea has no foundation. He explains that the only 'greatest name' for the Israelites is 'Yahweh', which is equivalent to 'Allah' for Muslims. He emphasizes that Allah answers the supplications of the distressed when they call upon Him by His known and customary name, not by secret names found in magic books. The passage guards against superstitious beliefs in hidden divine names.

Key Terms
الاسم الأعظم
شم همفورش
كتب السحر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: There is a hidden name that guarantees immediate answer to any prayer.
Correction: This belief originates from Hebrew magic books, not from Islamic teachings.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the Hebrew term for the hidden greatest name?
A) Allah
B) Shem HaMeforash
C) Yahweh
D) Ism al-A'zam

Key Takeaway
The idea of a hidden greatest name entered Muslim circles through magic and talisman books, not through authentic sources.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever held a belief about Allah or worship simply because it 'felt spiritual' or was commonly repeated, without verifying its source? Will you now commit to checking every claim against authentic sources, even if it means letting go of something you once cherished?
?
The passage shows that a seemingly pious concept—that Allah has a hidden name guaranteeing a response—actually originated from sorcery. How many of your own religious practices or beliefs might have dubious origins? Are you willing to investigate them, even if it means discomfort?
?
If this false claim entered Muslim books through magical texts, what other errors might have slipped into our understanding of Islam through popular but unverified stories? What steps will you take to protect yourself from being misled by appealing but false ideas?

Refutation and True Greatest Name
Refutes the hidden name and clarifies the true greatest name and supplication.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the refutation of the hidden name claim and the correct understanding that Allah answers by His well-known name, not by secret names.

Core Idea
Allah answers by His known name, not by secret names from magic.

After presenting the claim of a hidden greatest name, the author now delivers the refutation. He states that the notion has no foundation, identifies the true greatest name for each community, and clarifies how Allah responds to supplications.

Guided Path
1
Read the refutation
Read the part starting with 'وهذا لا أصل له' and understand that the hidden name claim is baseless.
2
Identify the true greatest names
The author states that the greatest name for Israelites is Yahweh, equivalent to Allah for Muslims.
This shows that there is no hidden name; the name is known.
3
Understand how Allah answers prayers
The passage concludes that Allah answers the distressed when they call upon Him by His well-known name, not by hidden names.
This teaches reliance on common supplication, not secret rituals.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وه الاسم الأعظم، يترجم فى العبرانية : « شم همفورش ، وقد دخل همفورش ، فى كتب المسلمين عن طريق كتب السحر والطلسمات ودخل في الكتب أن لله اسما إذا دعى به أجاب, وإذا سئل به أعطى . هو الاسم الأعظم. . وهذا لا أصل له : فلا اسم أعظم إلا «يهوه، عند بني إسرائيل المعادل لاسم الله ، عند المسلمين والله يجيب المضطر إذا دعاه باسمه المتعارف عليه ، لا بما خفى من أسمائه في كتب السحر
This is the greatest name, translated in Hebrew as 'Shem HaMeforash', and HaMeforash has entered the books of Muslims through the books of magic and talismans, and it entered the books that Allah has a name if called by it He answers, and if asked by it He gives. It is the greatest name. This has no basis: there is no greater name except 'Yahweh' among the Children of Israel, equivalent to the name 'Allah' among Muslims. And Allah answers the distressed if he calls Him by His well-known name, not by what is hidden of His names in magic books.
Source pages p. 290

Commentary
This passage refutes the claim that there is a hidden 'greatest name' of Allah that grants wishes, a notion that entered Muslim circles through sorcery and talismans. The author states that this idea has no foundation. He explains that the only 'greatest name' for the Israelites is 'Yahweh', which is equivalent to 'Allah' for Muslims. He emphasizes that Allah answers the supplications of the distressed when they call upon Him by His known and customary name, not by secret names found in magic books. The passage guards against superstitious beliefs in hidden divine names.

Key Terms
لا أصل له
يهوه
الله
اسمه المتعارف عليه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One must discover a secret name for prayers to be answered.
Correction: The passage teaches that Allah responds to those who call upon Him by His known name, not by hidden ones.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the greatest name for Muslims?
A) Yahweh
B) Allah
C) Shem HaMeforash
D) A hidden name

Key Takeaway
The true greatest name is Allah, and He answers prayers made with His known name, not through secret names.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you call upon Allah, do you truly believe that He hears you simply because you used the name 'Allah', or do you sometimes feel that you need a special formula to get His attention? How does this lesson challenge your reliance on magical thinking?
?
Is there any area of your life where you secretly wish for a 'hidden name'—a shortcut or secret knowledge—instead of trusting in the clear, accessible guidance of Allah? What would it cost you to abandon that wish and approach Allah directly with His well-known name?
?
If the greatest name is 'Allah' and He answers the distressed by this name, then why do you sometimes delay or avoid supplication, as if you lack the 'right' words? What holds you back from crying out to Him with the name you already know?

Ta'wil in Jewish Scriptures and Ghazali's Interpretation of Descent
Author explains why he brings Torah quotes: to show ta'wil exists in Jewish scriptures, with Ghazali's example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author uses Jewish scriptures and Ghazali's ta'wil to defend the principle of figurative interpretation in theological texts.

Core Idea
Ta'wil is not an innovation; it was practiced even in Jewish scriptures.

In this passage, the author builds a powerful case for ta'wil by showing that the concept of figurative interpretation existed long before Islam. Citing al-Razi's critique of Jewish anthropomorphism, he then presents a concrete example from Imam al-Ghazali on the hadith of descent. This lesson explores the logic behind this argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing, then read the translation. Let the author's own flow come through.
The passage has two main parts: al-Razi's statement and Ghazali's ta'wil.
2
Identify the two parts
Note where al-Razi's quote ends (after 'not a proof') and where the author introduces Ghazali's ta'wil of descent.
The author explicitly connects the two to show continuity of ta'wil.
3
Understand the author's purpose
Ask: why does the author quote Jewish scriptures? The passage says: to show that literal and figurative language existed in their books, proving ta'wil is not new.
4
Analyze Ghazali's argument
Ghazali interprets the hadith of descent as encouragement for night prayer, not literal descent. His reasoning: if God descended to be heard, we would hear Him; since we don't, it must be figurative.
This is a classic example of ta'wil based on rational impossibility.
5
Reflect on the methodology
Consider how this passage defends ta'wil by showing its precedence in earlier scriptures and its necessity for understanding anthropomorphic expressions.
The author argues that even Jews used figurative language, so Muslims should accept ta'wil.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولأن الإمام فخر الدين صرح في « أساس التقديس، بما نصه : إن اليهود كانوا على دين التشبيه ، وكانوا يجوزون المجيء والذهاب على الله ـ تعالى ـ وكانوا يقولون: إنه - تعالى - تجلى لموسى - عليه السلام - على «الطور فى ظلل من الغمام ، فظنوا مثل ذلك في زمان محمد - عليه السلام - و معلوم : أن مذهبهم ليس بحجة ، لأنه صرح بذلك ، رأيت أن أورد هنا من كلمات التوراة : كلمات ، استعملت على الحقيقة تارة ، واستعملت على المجاز تارة أخرى ، لأبين له . أن الحقيقة والمجاز موجودان عند بني إسرائيل ، في كتبهم المقدسة لديهم ، وأن التأويل الذي يؤمن به قد سبقه إليه غيره من قبل ولادته . وسأورد هذه الكلمات على النحو الذي صرح به الشيخ الغزالي أبو حامد في هذا النص : يقول الشيخ الغزالي في معنى : « ينزل الله - تعالى كل ليلة إلى السماء الدنيا . فيقول : هل من داع فأستجيب له ؟ هل من مستغفر ، فأغفر له؟ هذا الحديث سيق لنهاية الترغيب فى قيام الليل . وليس فيه إلا إيهام لفظ النزول عند الصي ، والعامي الجاري مجرى الصبي - ومن السهل على العالم أن يقنع العامي بأن النزول ليس على حقيقته بأن يقول له مثلا : إن كان نزول الله إلى السماء الدنيا ، ليسمعنا نداءه ، فما أسمعنا . فأي فائدة في نزوله ؟ ولو كان يريد أن يسمعنا نداءه ، فهو قادر على أن يسمعنا إياه وهو على ( م ١٩ أساس التقديس )
And because Imam Fakhr al-Din stated in 'Asas al-Taqdis' the following text: 'Indeed, the Jews were upon the religion of anthropomorphism (tashbih), and they allowed coming and going upon Allah — Exalted is He — and they used to say that He — Exalted is He — manifested Himself to Moses — peace be upon him — on the Mount in shadows of clouds, and they thought the like of that in the time of Muhammad — peace be upon him — and it is known that their doctrine is not a proof.' Because he stated that, I saw fit to present here from the words of the Torah: words used literally sometimes and figuratively other times, to show him that the literal and the figurative exist among the Children of Israel in their sacred books, and that the interpretation (ta'wil) which he believes in was preceded by others before his birth. And I will present these words in the manner stated by Shaykh al-Ghazali Abu Hamid in this text: Shaykh al-Ghazali says concerning the meaning: 'Allah — Exalted is He — descends every night to the lowest heaven and says: Is there any supplicant that I may answer him? Is there any seeker of forgiveness that I may forgive him? This hadith was conveyed for the utmost encouragement in night prayer. There is nothing in it except the illusion of the word 'descent' for the common person who is like a child — and it is easy for the scholar to convince the common person that descent is not literal by saying to him, for example: 'If the descent of Allah to the lowest heaven was to make us hear His call, then He did not make us hear. So what benefit is there in His descent? And if He wanted to make us hear His call, He is able to make us hear it while He is upon...' (M 19, Asas al-Taqdis)
Source pages p. 291

Commentary
This passage explains the author's motivation for presenting the earlier scriptural quotes. He cites Imam Fakhr al-Din al-Razi's statement from 'Asas al-Taqdis' that the Jews were anthropomorphists, believing in God's physical movement and manifestation on Mount Sinai, and they expected similar in Muhammad's time. Al-Razi deems their doctrine invalid. The author then says he will bring words from the Torah that are used both literally and figuratively to demonstrate that such dual usage exists in Jewish scriptures, proving that the concept of ta'wil (interpretation) is not an innovation. He then quotes al-Ghazali's interpretation of the hadith of God's descent: the hadith aims to encourage night prayer, and the word 'descent' is merely an illusion for the common person. The scholar can easily convince the layman that descent is not literal by arguing that if God descended to be heard, we would hear Him, so it must be figurative. This illustrates the principle of ta'wil.

Key Terms
التشبيه
التأويل
النزول
الترغيب
المجاز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think ta'wil is a later innovation in Islam.
Correction: The author shows that figurative interpretation existed in Jewish scriptures before Islam, so ta'wil is a universal principle.

Checkpoint
What was the doctrine of the Jews according to al-Razi as quoted in the passage?
A) They were monotheists
B) They were anthropomorphists
C) They were atheists
D) They were Muslims

Key Takeaway
The principle of ta'wil is universal and necessary for understanding anthropomorphic expressions in all scriptures, as demonstrated by Ghazali's example.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read the hadith of descent, does your mind automatically conjure an image of God moving? How can you actively train yourself to pause and seek the intended meaning rather than a literal one?
?
The Jews held anthropomorphic beliefs because they took scriptures literally. Are there any verses or hadith that you are tempted to understand literally about Allah? Are you willing to accept the ta'wil of scholars even if your heart initially resists?
?
If a fellow Muslim asks you about the meaning of 'Allah descends to the lowest heaven,' will you be able to explain it in a way that preserves Allah's transcendence without dismissing the hadith? What concrete steps will you take to prepare yourself to be that scholar who can guide the common person?

Refutation of Anthropomorphism and Precedent for Ta'wil
Author uses al-Razi and states his purpose to show ta'wil precedent from Torah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's argument that metaphorical interpretation (ta'wil) is not an innovation but has precedent in earlier scriptures, specifically the Torah.

Core Idea
Ta'wil of anthropomorphic expressions is a universal hermeneutical practice, not a Muslim invention.

The author begins by citing Imam al-Razi's critique of Jewish anthropomorphism and sets out to show from the Torah itself that both literal and metaphorical language exist in sacred texts. This establishes the legitimacy of ta'wil before presenting specific examples.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the author's tone and his reference to al-Razi.
The passage is a direct quote from al-Razi's 'Asas al-Taqdis'.
2
Identify the author's purpose
The author states he will quote words from the Torah to demonstrate that both literal and metaphorical language existed among the Children of Israel.
This is the thesis of the entire section.
3
Connect to the broader argument
Understand that the author is responding to those who reject ta'wil, showing that even the Torah, which some claim is literal, contains metaphorical usages.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولأن الإمام فخر الدين صرح في « أساس التقديس، بما نصه : إن اليهود كانوا على دين التشبيه ، وكانوا يجوزون المجيء والذهاب على الله ـ تعالى ـ وكانوا يقولون: إنه - تعالى - تجلى لموسى - عليه السلام - على «الطور فى ظلل من الغمام ، فظن و ا مثل ذلك في زمان محمد - عليه السلام - و معلوم : أن مذهبهم ليس بحجة ، لأنه صرح بذلك ، رأيت أن أورد هنا من كلمات التوراة : كلمات ، استعملت على الحقيقة تارة ، واستعملت على المجاز تارة أخرى ، لأبين له . أن الحقيقة والمجاز موجودان عند بني إسرائيل ، في كتبهم المقدسة لديهم ، وأن التأويل الذي يؤمن به قد سبقه إليه غيره من قبل ولادته . وسأورد هذه الكلمات على النحو الذي صرح به الشيخ الغزالي أبو حامد في هذا النص : يقول الشيخ الغزالي في معنى : « ينزل الله - تعالى كل ليلة إلى السماء الدنيا . فيقول : هل من داع فأستجيب له ؟ هل من مستغفر ، فأغفر له؟ هذا الحديث سيق لنهاية الترغيب فى قيام الليل . وليس فيه إلا إيهام لفظ النزول عند الصى ، والعامى الجارى بجرى الصبي - ومن السهل على العالم أن يقنع العامى بأن النزول ليس على حقيقته بأن يقول له مثلا : إن كان نزول الله إلى السماء الدنيا ، ليسمعنا نداءه ، فما أسمعنا . فأي فائدة في نزوله ؟ ولو كان يريد أن يسمعنا نداءه ، فهو قادر على أن يسمعنا إياه وهو على العرش ، فلماذا ينزل ؟ فيفهم العالمى : أن ظاهر النزول باطل ، ويتيقن نفي صورة النزول . وهذه الكلمات هي التي محل نظر وجدل وأخذ ورد بين شراح التوراة . وقد رفعت بعضها من ههنا ، ووضعته في التعليقات . فعلى من يريد البحث كاملا أن ينقل الكليات التي وضعتها في التعليقات إلى مهنا . ويخيل إلى أن من يقرأ ما كتبته في تأويل الكلمات ، ثم يقرأ كتاب موسى ابن ميمون ، المسمى بدلالة الحائرين ، لن يجد صعوبة في فهم كلام ابن ميمون. فى التوراة هذا النص : فنزل الرب فى السحاب . فوقف عنده هناك ونادى باسم الرب. فاجتاز الرب قدامه . ونادى : الرب الرب . إله رحيم ورؤوف ، بطىء الغضب وكثير الإحسان والوفاء ، حافظ الإحسان إلى ألوف ، غافر الإثم ... إلخ ، ( خر ٣٤: ٥ - ٧) في هذا النص كلمة «اجتاز ، وتترجم « عبر ، وعبر على الحقيقة تدل على انتقال جسم من مكان إلى مكان . وعلى المجاز تدل على المعاني التالية : ۱ - امتداد الأصوات في الهواء . مثل : « اسمع أنكم تبعثون شعب الرب على المعصية إ ( ١ هم ٢ : ٢٤) ۲ - حلول نور يراه الأنبياء فى مرأى النبوة في حلم الليل . ومثاله : أن إبراهيم عليه السلام وقع عليه سبات «وإذا رعبة مظلمة عظيمة واقعة عليه ، و فى الحلم أثناء الظلمة. إذا تتور دخان و مصباح نار ، يجوز بين تلك وفى القطع ( تك (۱۷:۱٥ ومثله : وأنا أجتاز في أرض مصر (خر ۱۲-۱۲) ١٧:١٥)
And because Imam Fakhr al-Din stated in 'Asas al-Taqdis' with his text: 'Indeed the Jews were upon the religion of anthropomorphism (tashbih), and they permitted coming and going upon Allah—Exalted—and they used to say: He—Exalted—manifested Himself to Moses—peace be upon him—on the mountain (al-Tur) in layers of cloud, and they thought similarly in the time of Muhammad—peace be upon him—and it is known that their doctrine is not an argument.' Because he stated that, I saw fit to mention here from the words of the Torah: words used literally (haqiqah) sometimes, and used metaphorically (majaz) other times, to show him that literal and metaphorical are present with the Children of Israel in their sacred books, and that the interpretation (ta'wil) he believes in was preceded by others before his birth. And I will mention these words in the manner stated by Shaykh al-Ghazali Abu Hamid in this text: Shaykh al-Ghazali says regarding the meaning: 'Allah—Exalted—descends every night to the lowest heaven and says: Is there any supplicant that I may respond to him? Is there any seeker of forgiveness that I may forgive him?' This hadith was conveyed for the utmost encouragement of night prayer. And there is nothing in it except an illusion of the word descent (nuzul) to the commoner, and the layman who runs as the child runs—and it is easy for the scholar to convince the commoner that descent is not literal by saying to him, for example: 'If Allah's descent to the lowest heaven was to make us hear His call, then He did not make us hear it. So what benefit is in His descent? And if He wanted to make us hear His call, He is able to make us hear it while He is on the Throne, so why does He descend?' So the commoner understands that the apparent meaning of descent is false, and he becomes certain of negating the form of descent. These words are the subject of consideration, debate, acceptance and rejection among the commentators of the Torah. And I have removed some of them from here and placed them in the comments. So whoever wants a complete research must transfer the totalities I placed in the comments here. And it seems to me that whoever reads what I wrote in the interpretation of the words, then reads the book of Moses ibn Maimon, called 'Guide for the Perplexed', will find no difficulty in understanding the words of ibn Maimon. In the Torah is this text: 'And the Lord descended in the cloud, and stood there with him and called upon the name of the Lord. And the Lord passed over before him, and called out: The Lord, the Lord, God merciful and gracious, slow to anger and abounding in kindness and faithfulness, keeping kindness for thousands, forgiving iniquity... etc.' (Exodus 34:5-7) In this text is the word 'ijtaza' (passed over), and it is translated as 'abara' (crossed), and 'abara' literally indicates the transfer of a body from one place to another. And metaphorically it indicates the following meanings: 1. The extension of sounds in the air, such as: 'I hear that you are sending the people of the Lord against sin' (1 Chronicles 2:24) 2. The appearance of light seen by prophets in the vision of prophecy in the dream of the night. An example of this is that Abraham—peace be upon him—was overtaken by sleep, 'and behold, a great dark terror falling upon him, and in the dream during the darkness, if there is smoke and a torch of fire that passes between those pieces' (Genesis 15:12) and similarly: 'And I will pass over the land of Egypt' (Exodus 12:12)
Source pages p. 291

Commentary
This passage is an extended argument for the legitimacy and necessity of metaphorical interpretation (ta'wil) of scriptural texts that contain anthropomorphic expressions. The author begins by citing Imam Fakhr al-Din al-Razi's statement from 'Asas al-Taqdis' that the Jews held anthropomorphic beliefs about God, such as allowing movement and manifestation in physical forms, and that their doctrine is not a valid argument. The author then states his purpose: to quote words from the Torah that are used both literally and metaphorically to demonstrate that both modes exist in Jewish scriptures, and that the ta'wil the reader believes in was already practiced before him. He will present these words as exemplified by al-Ghazali. Next, he quotes al-Ghazali's explanation of the hadith 'Allah descends every night to the lowest heaven'. Al-Ghazali argues that this hadith is meant to encourage night prayer and that the word 'descent' is only an illusion to the common person. He provides a rational argument to convince a commoner: if God's descent were to make us hear His call, we don't hear it; and if He could make us hear from the Throne, descent is unnecessary. Thus the apparent literal meaning is false, and the commoner becomes certain that the form of descent is negated. This is a method of ta'wil. The author then notes that such interpretative words are the subject of debate among Torah commentators, and he has placed some in the comments. He suggests that reading his ta'wil of words together with Maimonides' 'Guide for the Perplexed' will facilitate understanding. Finally, he presents a Torah passage (Exodus 34:5-7) containing the word 'ijtaza' (passed over). He explains that literally it denotes bodily movement, but metaphorically it can mean: 1) the extension of sounds in air (citing 1 Chronicles 2:24), and 2) the appearance of light seen by prophets in a dream (citing Genesis 15:12 and Exodus 12:12). The whole passage demonstrates that ta'wil of anthropomorphic expressions is not an invention of Muslims but has precedent in earlier scriptures.

Misconception: That ta'wil is a Muslim innovation and not found in earlier scriptures.
Correction: The author argues that the Torah itself contains words used both literally and metaphorically, showing ta'wil has ancient precedent.

The author sets out to prove that metaphorical interpretation of scripture is a longstanding practice, not a novelty.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read a verse that describes God in human-like terms, what is the first reaction of your heart? Do you immediately try to affirm God's transcendence, or do you find yourself forming a mental image? How does this passage challenge that tendency?

?
If the very words of the Torah can have both literal and metaphorical meanings, what does that say about your own certainty when you read any sacred text? Is there a danger in insisting on only one reading? What would it take for you to humbly reconsider an interpretation you hold firmly?

Al-Ghazali's Rational Ta'wil of the Descent Hadith
Ghazali explains the hadith of descent and provides a rational proof for metaphorical interpretation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Ghazali uses rational argument to demonstrate that the apparent meaning of 'descent' in the hadith cannot be literal and requires metaphorical interpretation.

Core Idea
If literal descent were true, it would be purposeless; reason compels ta'wil.

The author presents al-Ghazali's interpretation of the well-known hadith about Allah's descent to the lowest heaven. Ghazali provides a simple rational argument to show the commoner that the literal meaning is false, illustrating a key method of ta'wil.

Guided Path
1
Read the hadith and Ghazali's explanation
Read the Arabic passage, noting the structure: presentation of the hadith, explanation of its purpose, the rational argument, and the conclusion.
2
Understand the rational argument
Ghazali proposes a hypothetical scenario: if the descent were literal and meant to make us hear, why do we not hear? And if God can make us hear from the Throne, why descend? This leads to the conclusion that the literal meaning is impossible.
The argument is a simple reductio ad absurdum.
3
Appreciate the pedagogical technique
Ghazali's method is designed for a commoner: he uses a logical question that anyone can understand to show the necessity of ta'wil.
This reflects the balance between reason and scripture.
4
Connect to the author's broader project
The author then notes that this method of interpretation is also used by Torah commentators and references Maimonides, setting up the next section.

تعالى كل ليلة إلى السماء الدنيا . فيقول : هل من داع فأستجيب له ؟ هل من مستغفر ، فأغفر له؟ هذا الحديث سيق لنهاية الترغيب فى قيام الليل . وليس فيه إلا إيهام لفظ النزول عند الصى ، والعامى الجارى بجرى الصبي - ومن السهل على العالم أن يقنع العامى بأن النزول ليس على حقيقته بأن يقول له مثلا : إن كان نزول الله إلى السماء الدنيا ، ليسمعنا نداءه ، فما أسمعنا . فأي فائدة في نزوله ؟ ولو كان يريد أن يسمعنا نداءه ، فهو قادر على أن يسمعنا إياه وهو على العرش ، فلماذا ينزل ؟ فيفهم العالمى : أن ظاهر النزول باطل ، ويتيقن نفي صورة النزول . وهذه الكلمات هي التي محل نظر وجدل وأخذ ورد بين شراح التوراة . وقد رفعت بعضها من ههنا ، ووضعته في التعليقات . فعلى من يريد البحث كاملا أن ينقل الكليات التي وضعتها في التعليقات إلى مهنا . ويخيل إلى أن من يقرأ ما كتبته في تأويل الكلمات ، ثم يقرأ كتاب موسى ابن ميمون ، المسمى بدلالة الحائرين ، لن يجد صعوبة في فهم كلام ابن ميمون. فى التوراة هذا النص : فنزل الرب فى السحاب . فوقف عنده هناك ونادى باسم الرب. فاجتاز الرب قدامه . ونادى : الرب الرب . إله رحيم ورؤوف ، بطىء الغضب وكثير الإحسان والوفاء ، حافظ الإحسان إلى ألوف ، غافر الإثم ... إلخ ، ( خر ٣٤: ٥ - ٧) في هذا النص كلمة «اجتاز ، وتترجم « عبر ، وعبر على الحقيقة تدل على انتقال جسم من مكان إلى مكان . وعلى المجاز تدل على المعاني التالية : ۱ - امتداد الأصوات في الهواء . مثل : « اسمع أنكم تبعثون شعب الرب على المعصية إ ( ١ هم ٢ : ٢٤) ۲ - حلول نور يراه الأنبياء فى مرأى النبوة في حلم الليل . ومثاله : أن إبراهيم عليه السلام وقع عليه سبات «وإذا رعبة مظلمة عظيمة واقعة عليه ، و فى الحلم أثناء الظلمة. إذا تتور دخان و مصباح نار ، يجوز بين تلك وفى القطع ( تك (۱۷:۱٥ ومثله : وأنا أجتاز في أرض مصر (خر ۱۲-۱۲) ١٧:١٥)
And because Imam Fakhr al-Din stated in 'Asas al-Taqdis' with his text: 'Indeed the Jews were upon the religion of anthropomorphism (tashbih), and they permitted coming and going upon Allah—Exalted—and they used to say: He—Exalted—manifested Himself to Moses—peace be upon him—on the mountain (al-Tur) in layers of cloud, and they thought similarly in the time of Muhammad—peace be upon him—and it is known that their doctrine is not an argument.' Because he stated that, I saw fit to mention here from the words of the Torah: words used literally (haqiqah) sometimes, and used metaphorically (majaz) other times, to show him that literal and metaphorical are present with the Children of Israel in their sacred books, and that the interpretation (ta'wil) he believes in was preceded by others before his birth. And I will mention these words in the manner stated by Shaykh al-Ghazali Abu Hamid in this text: Shaykh al-Ghazali says regarding the meaning: 'Allah—Exalted—descends every night to the lowest heaven and says: Is there any supplicant that I may respond to him? Is there any seeker of forgiveness that I may forgive him?' This hadith was conveyed for the utmost encouragement of night prayer. And there is nothing in it except an illusion of the word descent (nuzul) to the commoner, and the layman who runs as the child runs—and it is easy for the scholar to convince the commoner that descent is not literal by saying to him, for example: 'If Allah's descent to the lowest heaven was to make us hear His call, then He did not make us hear it. So what benefit is in His descent? And if He wanted to make us hear His call, He is able to make us hear it while He is on the Throne, so why does He descend?' So the commoner understands that the apparent meaning of descent is false, and he becomes certain of negating the form of descent. These words are the subject of consideration, debate, acceptance and rejection among the commentators of the Torah. And I have removed some of them from here and placed them in the comments. So whoever wants a complete research must transfer the totalities I placed in the comments here. And it seems to me that whoever reads what I wrote in the interpretation of the words, then reads the book of Moses ibn Maimon, called 'Guide for the Perplexed', will find no difficulty in understanding the words of ibn Maimon. In the Torah is this text: 'And the Lord descended in the cloud, and stood there with him and called upon the name of the Lord. And the Lord passed over before him, and called out: The Lord, the Lord, God merciful and gracious, slow to anger and abounding in kindness and faithfulness, keeping kindness for thousands, forgiving iniquity... etc.' (Exodus 34:5-7) In this text is the word 'ijtaza' (passed over), and it is translated as 'abara' (crossed), and 'abara' literally indicates the transfer of a body from one place to another. And metaphorically it indicates the following meanings: 1. The extension of sounds in the air, such as: 'I hear that you are sending the people of the Lord against sin' (1 Chronicles 2:24) 2. The appearance of light seen by prophets in the vision of prophecy in the dream of the night. An example of this is that Abraham—peace be upon him—was overtaken by sleep, 'and behold, a great dark terror falling upon him, and in the dream during the darkness, if there is smoke and a torch of fire that passes between those pieces' (Genesis 15:12) and similarly: 'And I will pass over the land of Egypt' (Exodus 12:12)
Source pages p. 291

Commentary
This passage is an extended argument for the legitimacy and necessity of metaphorical interpretation (ta'wil) of scriptural texts that contain anthropomorphic expressions. The author begins by citing Imam Fakhr al-Din al-Razi's statement from 'Asas al-Taqdis' that the Jews held anthropomorphic beliefs about God, such as allowing movement and manifestation in physical forms, and that their doctrine is not a valid argument. The author then states his purpose: to quote words from the Torah that are used both literally and metaphorically to demonstrate that both modes exist in Jewish scriptures, and that the ta'wil the reader believes in was already practiced before him. He will present these words as exemplified by al-Ghazali. Next, he quotes al-Ghazali's explanation of the hadith 'Allah descends every night to the lowest heaven'. Al-Ghazali argues that this hadith is meant to encourage night prayer and that the word 'descent' is only an illusion to the common person. He provides a rational argument to convince a commoner: if God's descent were to make us hear His call, we don't hear it; and if He could make us hear from the Throne, descent is unnecessary. Thus the apparent literal meaning is false, and the commoner becomes certain that the form of descent is negated. This is a method of ta'wil. The author then notes that such interpretative words are the subject of debate among Torah commentators, and he has placed some in the comments. He suggests that reading his ta'wil of words together with Maimonides' 'Guide for the Perplexed' will facilitate understanding. Finally, he presents a Torah passage (Exodus 34:5-7) containing the word 'ijtaza' (passed over). He explains that literally it denotes bodily movement, but metaphorically it can mean: 1) the extension of sounds in air (citing 1 Chronicles 2:24), and 2) the appearance of light seen by prophets in a dream (citing Genesis 15:12 and Exodus 12:12). The whole passage demonstrates that ta'wil of anthropomorphic expressions is not an invention of Muslims but has precedent in earlier scriptures.

تأويل
حقيقة
مجاز

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the hadith 'nuzul' must be interpreted literally as a physical descent.
Correction: Al-Ghazali shows that literal interpretation leads to absurdity (purposeless descent, no audible effect), so ta'wil is necessary.

Checkpoint
What is the key rational argument al-Ghazali uses to convince the commoner that the descent is not literal?
A) The commoner cannot understand the true nature of God.
B) If God's descent were literal, He would have no need to descend because He can make Himself heard from the Throne.
C) The hadith is weak and should be rejected.
D) The descent is only a dream vision.

Key Takeaway
Al-Ghazali's rational argument demonstrates that reason necessitates ta'wil of anthropomorphic expressions when literal meaning leads to absurdity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite the hadith of descent, do you feel your heart tremble at the thought of Allah’s nearness? Or do you mentally correct the literal meaning? How does this intellectual approach affect your nightly prayer—does it draw you closer or create distance?
?
If God’s descent is only metaphorical, what does that say about your personal relationship with Him during qiyam al-layl? Do you still feel He is near, listening? If not, what must you change to recover that intimacy?
?
Al-Ghazali argues that the literal meaning is 'false'—yet the Prophet ﷺ uttered these words. How do you reconcile your love for the Prophet with the necessity of ta’wil? Is there a danger of over-rationalizing until the heart becomes cold? What step will you take tonight to ensure your worship remains both intellectually sound and spiritually alive?
