Torah Evidence for Metaphorical Language: The Word 'Ijtaza'
Author analyzes the Torah word 'ijtaza' showing its literal and metaphorical uses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Torah itself uses words both literally and metaphorically, specifically the word 'ijtaza' (passed over), providing precedent for ta'wil.

Core Idea
The Torah contains metaphorical language, proving ta'wil is an ancient practice.

The author cites a specific Torah passage (Exodus 34:5-7) containing the word 'ijtaza' and demonstrates that it has multiple metaphorical meanings beyond the literal bodily crossing, showing that the Jewish scripture itself uses figurative language.

Guided Path
1
Read the Torah passage
Read the Arabic and translation of Exodus 34:5-7, focusing on the word 'ijtaza' and its context.
This passage describes God passing before Moses.
2
Understand the literal meaning
The author explains that literally 'ijtaza' implies a transfer of a body from place to place, which is impossible for God.
3
Explore the metaphorical meanings
The author provides two metaphorical uses of the root 'abara': (1) extension of sounds in air, and (2) appearance of light in prophetic visions.
Biblical examples are given for each.
4
Connect to broader argument
This shows that even in the Torah, words have figurative meanings, justifying ta'wil of similar expressions in Islam.
The author also references Maimonides to strengthen the point.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The author proves that ta'wil is an ancient practice found in the Torah itself. Literal and Metaphorical Meanings of 'Ajtaz'
Author enumerates literal and three metaphorical meanings of 'ajtaz'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Arabic word 'ajtaz' ('passed by') can have both a literal and several metaphorical meanings in scriptural contexts.

Core Idea
A scriptural word may have a literal physical meaning and also carry metaphorical senses.

In studying scriptural texts about God's actions, one must distinguish literal from metaphorical language. The author examines the word 'ajtaz' (passed by) as used in the Bible, explaining its literal meaning and three metaphorical applications. This helps avoid misunderstanding passages that seem to describe physical movement of God.

1
Read the passage carefully

And the Lord passed by before him, and proclaimed: The Lord, the Lord God, merciful and gracious, longsuffering, and abundant in goodness and truth, keeping mercy for thousands, forgiving iniquity... etc.' (Exodus 34:5-7). In this text is the word 'ajtaz', translated as 'crossed over'. Literally, 'crossed over' indicates the movement of a body from one place to another. The passage thus clarifies that the verb is not always literal and can signify abstract concepts.

Maimonides and Targum on Anthropomorphic Interpretations
Author critiques Maimonides' interpretation and presents Targum's alternative.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the critique of Maimonides' interpretation of 'ajtaz' and the Targumic method of avoiding anthropomorphism by positing a deleted genitive.

Core Idea
The Targum avoids anthropomorphism by inserting a created intermediary (Shekhinah) between God and anthropomorphic actions.

This lesson examines a specific case of interpreting anthropomorphic language in Scripture. Moses ibn Maimon (Maimonides) offered a metaphorical interpretation of Exodus 33: 'the Lord passed by before him.' The author of our text finds this interpretation far-fetched and instead presents the approach of the Targum, which uses a method of 'deletion of the genitive' to attribute the action to a created entity like the Shekhinah. We will see how this method is also found in Islamic exegesis.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the English translation carefully. Notice the flow of the argument: first the critique of Maimonides, then the presentation of the Targum's method.
The Arabic text has some OCR errors, but the meaning is clear.
2
Identify Maimonides' interpretation and author's critique
Find where the author explains what Maimonides said about 'him' in 'before him'. Then locate the sentence where the author calls it 'far-fetched' and gives the reason.
The author says the pronoun refers to Moses, not God.
3
Understand the Targumic method of 'deletion of the genitive'
Look for the phrase 'حذف المضاف'. The author explains that the Targum avoids anthropomorphism by supplying a deleted word (like Shekhinah) that becomes the subject of the verb.
For example, 'the Lord passed by' becomes 'the Lord caused His Shekhinah to pass by'.
4
Note the comparison with Islamic exegesis and Onkelos
Read how Onkelos the proselyte uses similar deletions (Glory, Shekhinah, Word) and how Islamic interpreters do the same with Quranic verses like 'And your Lord comes'.
Maimonides himself is reported to approve of this method and suggests 'sound' as an alternative.
5
Reflect on the principle of divine transcendence
Consider why this method is important: it protects God from being described in physical terms while still honoring the scriptural text.
This is a key principle in both Jewish and Islamic theological interpretation.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمعنى المجازى الثالث هو المراد . عند موسی ابن ميمون ، في تفسير ... اجتاز الرب قدامه ، ويشرحه هكذا : إن الهاء فى قدامه تعود إلى الله تعالى . وأن موسى - عليه السلام - طلب إدراك وجه الله ، فوعده الله برؤية الوراء . أى أن الله تعالى حجب عنه إدراك الوجه وتجاوزه معنى الوراء. والدليل على أن موسى عليه السلام طلب رؤية الوجه : أن الله تعالى قال له : «وجهى لايرى خر) ٢٣ : ٢٣) والدليل على أن الله تعالى وعده برؤية الوراء: أن الله تعالى قال له : «فتنظر قفای» (خر ٣٣ : ٢٣) . هذا تفسيره. وهو تفسير بعيد . لأن الهاء فى قدامه تعود إلى موسى، لا تعود إلى الله . وما جاء في الترجوم هو أحسن من تفسير ابن ميمون . لأن الترجوم إذا وجد أمرا منسوبا الله تعالى يلحقه منه تجسيم أولوا حق تجسيم ، فإنه يقدره بحذف المضاف . أى يجعل تلك النسبة لأمر ما ، مضاف الله ، محذوف . فيقول فى «فاجتاز الرب قدامه» : إن الرب قد جعل سكينته تعبر قدامه . فالذي عبر واجتاز - على حسب الترجوم - هو السكينة و معنى « قدامه . - على رأى النرجوم - أي بمحضره . والسكينة عنده جسم ، لا بمعنى اطمئنان القلب . د و«انقولوس المتهود، يقدر المحذوف في الترجوم المجد ، السكينة، القول . بحسب كل موقع . ورأى انقولوس هذا في الكتب الإسلامية ، فإن فيها من يفسر د وجاء ربك والملك صفا صفا ، بقوله : جاء أمر ربك . و موسى ابن ميمون يقول عن تأويله : إنه جيد مستحسن ، و يقول : من الممكن أن نقدر المحذوف بكلمة الصوت بدل المجد والسكينة والقول. ويكون التقدير . وعبر صوت الرب من أمامه ونادى . أو : و عبر صوت من قبل الله
And the third metaphorical meaning is the intended one according to Moses ibn Maimon in the interpretation of 'and the Lord passed by before him.' He explains it thus: The 'him' in 'before him' refers to God Almighty. And that Moses, peace be upon him, sought to perceive the face of God, so God promised him to see the back. That is, God Almighty prevented him from perceiving the face and passed him over to the meaning of the back. The evidence that Moses sought to see the face is that God said to him: 'My face shall not be seen' (Exodus 33:23). And the evidence that God promised him to see the back is that God said to him: 'And you shall see My back' (Exodus 33:23). This is his interpretation. And it is a far-fetched interpretation. Because the 'him' in 'before him' refers to Moses, not to God. And what is brought in the Targum is better than the interpretation of ibn Maimon. Because the Targum, when it finds something attributed to God Almighty that would entail anthropomorphism which they [the Targumists] seek to avoid, it supplies it by deleting the genitive. That is, it makes that attribution to a certain thing, with 'of God' deleted. So it says regarding 'and the Lord passed by before him': 'The Lord caused His Shekhinah to pass before him.' So the one that passed and crossed over, according to the Targum, is the Shekhinah. And the meaning of 'before him' according to the Targum is 'in his presence.' And the Shekhinah in its view is a body, not in the sense of tranquility of heart. And Onkelos the proselyte supplies the deletion in the Targum as 'the Glory', 'the Shekhinah', 'the Word', according to each context. And this view of Onkelos appears in Islamic books, for among them are those who interpret 'And your Lord comes, and the angels rank upon rank' (Quran 89:22) by saying 'the command of your Lord comes.' And Moses ibn Maimon says about his interpretation: it is good and approved, and he says: it is possible to supply the deletion with the word 'sound' instead of Glory, Shekhinah, and Word. So the estimation would be: 'And the sound of the Lord passed before him and proclaimed' or 'And a sound from God passed.'
Source pages p. 293

Commentary
This passage critiques Maimonides' interpretation of 'ajtaz' in Exodus. Maimonides took the pronoun 'him' in 'before him' to refer to God, interpreting that Moses sought to see God's face and was promised to see His back. The author dismisses this as far-fetched, asserting the pronoun refers to Moses. He then praises the Targum's approach: when an anthropomorphic expression occurs, the Targum avoids it by positing a deleted genitive, translating 'the Lord passed by before him' as 'the Lord caused His Shekhinah to pass before him.' Thus the agent of passing is the Shekhinah, a created bodily entity (in the Targum's view). The author mentions Onkelos the proselyte, who similarly supplies 'Glory,' 'Shekhinah,' or 'Word' depending on context. He notes that Islamic exegetes use a similar method, interpreting 'your Lord comes' as 'the command of your Lord comes.' Finally, Maimonides himself is reported to approve of this approach and suggests supplying 'sound' as the deleted word.

Refutation of Maimonides on 'The Lord Passed Before Him'
Razi critiques Maimonides' interpretation of Exodus 33:23

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Imam Razi rejects Maimonides' metaphorical interpretation of Exodus 33:23 based on a simple grammatical point.

Core Idea
The pronoun matters: 'before him' refers to Moses, not God.

Imam Razi here engages with a Jewish philosopher's interpretation of a biblical verse. Maimonides reads the verse as implying God's movement, which contradicts divine transcendence. Razi points out a simple grammatical error that undermines the entire interpretation.

Guided Path
1
Understand Maimonides' interpretation
First, read the passage where Razi presents Maimonides' explanation. Note how Maimonides interprets 'the Lord passed before him' as God passing before Moses, and the vision of the back as seeing the aftermath.
Maimonides uses Exodus 23:23 and 33:23 as proof texts.
2
Identify the grammatical point
Razi argues that the pronoun 'him' in 'before him' refers to Moses, not God. This means the verse describes God passing before Moses, not God passing before Himself.
If the pronoun refers to Moses, then God is the subject who passes, and Moses is the object. There is no movement or change in God.
3
Reflect on the refutation
Razi calls Maimonides' interpretation 'far-fetched' because it relies on a mistaken pronoun reference. This shows how a small grammatical detail can refute a major theological claim.
This underscores the importance of precise language in theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمعنى المجازى الثالث هو المراد عند موسی ابن ميمون في تفسير ... اجتاز الرب قدامه ويشرحه هكذا : إن الهاء فى قدامه تعود إلى الله تعالى . وأن موسى - عليه السلام - طلب إدراك وجه الله فوعده الله برؤية الوراء . أي أن الله تعالى حجب عنه إدراك الوجه وتجاوزه معنى الوراء. والدليل على أن موسى عليه السلام طلب رؤية الوجه : أن الله تعالى قال له : «وجهى لايرى خر) ۲۳ : ۲۳) والدليل على أن الله تعالى وعده برؤية الوراء: أن الله تعالى قال له : «فتنظر قفای» (خر ۳۳ : ۲۳) . هذا تفسيره. وهو تفسير بعيد . لأن الهاء فى قدامه تعود إلى موسى لا تعود إلى الله .
And the third metaphorical meaning is intended according to Moses Maimonides in the interpretation of ... 'The Lord passed before him' and he explains it thus: That the pronoun 'him' in 'before him' refers to God Almighty. And that Moses - peace be upon him - sought to perceive the face of God, so God promised him the vision of the back. That is, God Almighty veiled from him the perception of the face and let him pass to the meaning of the back. The proof that Moses sought the vision of the face: that God said to him: 'My face shall not be seen' (Ex 23:23). The proof that God promised him the vision of the back: that God said to him: 'And you shall see my back' (Ex 33:23). This is his interpretation. And it is a far-fetched interpretation. Because the pronoun 'him' in 'before him' refers to Moses, not to God.
Source pages p. 293

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of Maimonides' interpretation of the biblical verse 'The Lord passed before him' (Exodus 33). The author first presents Maimonides' view: that the pronoun 'him' refers to God, so God passed before Moses, and that seeing God's back means seeing the aftermath of God's passing. Maimonides cites Exodus 23:23 ('My face shall not be seen') and Exodus 33:23 ('You shall see my back') as proof. Then the author rejects this interpretation, calling it far-fetched, because the pronoun 'him' in 'before him' actually refers to Moses, not God. Thus the verse does not imply any movement or passing on God's part.

Correction: Razi shows it is based on a grammatical error: the pronoun 'him' in 'before him' refers to Moses, so no movement is attributed to God.

Key Takeaway

The Targum's Method: Deleting the Genitive to Avoid Corporealism
Razi explains the Targum's method of deleting the genitive to avoid corporealism

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Targum, Onkelos, and Maimonides reinterpret anthropomorphic verses by grammatical deletion to preserve divine transcendence.

Core Idea
The Targum avoids corporealism by grammatical deletion, not denial.

In this passage, Imam al-Razi discusses the interpretive strategy of the Targum (Aramaic translation of the Torah) to handle verses that seem to attribute bodily actions to God. Rather than rejecting the text, the Targum subtly shifts the subject by deleting a genitive construct. This lesson unpacks that method and its variations.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the literal translation to grasp the flow.
Notice the technical term 'حذف المضاف' (deletion of the genitive).
2
Identify the grammatical mechanism
The Targum avoids attributing actions directly to God by deleting the genitive and inserting a created entity like the Shekhinah. Look at the example: 'the Lord passed before him' becomes 'the Lord made His Shekhinah pass before him'.
3
Compare different interpreters
Onkelos specifies different substitutions (glory, Shekhinah, speech) depending on context. Maimonides suggests 'sound' as an alternative. Notice how this method is also used in Islamic exegesis.
The passage says this view appears in Islamic books, citing the verse 'your Lord comes' interpreted as 'your Lord's command comes'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما جاء في الترجوم هو أحسن من تفسير ابن ميمون . لأن الترجوم إذا وجد أمرا منسوبا الله تعالى يلحقه منه تجسيم أولوا حق تجسيم فإنه يقدره بحذف المضاف . أي يجعل تلك النسبة لأمر ما مضاف الله محذوف . فيقول فى «فاجتاز الرب قدامه» : إن الرب قد جعل سكينته تعبر قدامه . فالذي عبر واجتاز - على حسب الترجوم - هو السكينة و معنى « قدامه . - على رأى النرجوم - أي بمحضره . والسكينة عنده جسم لا بمعنى اطمئنان القلب . و«انقولوس المتهود» يقدر المحذوف في الترجوم المجد السكينة القول بحسب كل موقع . ورأى انقولوس هذا في الكتب الإسلامية فإن فيها من يفسر د وجاء ربك والملك صفا صفا بقوله : جاء أمر ربك . و موسى ابن ميمون يقول عن تأويله : إنه جيد مستحسن و يقول : من الممكن أن نقدر المحذوف بكلمة الصوت بدل المجد والسكينة والقول. ويكون التقدير . وعبر صوت الرب من أمامه ونادى . أو : و عبر صوت من قبل الله
And what is found in the Targum is better than Maimonides' interpretation. Because the Targum, when it finds something attributed to God Almighty that entails corporealism, those who claim corporealism, it supposes it by deletion of the genitive construct. That is, it makes that attribution to something, the deleted genitive of God. So it says in 'And the Lord passed before him': Indeed the Lord made His Shekhinah pass before him. So that which passed and crossed over - according to the Targum - is the Shekhinah. And the meaning of 'before him' - according to the Targum - is 'in his presence'. And the Shekhinah according to it is a body, not meaning tranquility of the heart. And 'Onkelos the proselyte' supposes the deleted thing in the Targum as the glory, the Shekhinah, the speech, according to each context. And this view of Onkelos is found in Islamic books, for among them are those who interpret 'And your Lord comes and the angels rank upon rank' by saying: 'The command of your Lord comes'. And Moses Maimonides says about his interpretation: It is good and excellent, and he says: It is possible to suppose the deleted thing with the word 'sound' instead of glory, Shekhinah, and speech. So the estimation would be: 'And the sound of the Lord passed before him and called'. Or: 'And a sound from God passed before him'.
Source pages p. 293

Commentary
This passage presents the Targum's method of tawil (interpretation) as superior to Maimonides'. The Targum avoids corporealism by deleting the genitive construct (حذف المضاف): instead of attributing an action directly to God, it attributes it to a created entity like the Shekhinah. For the verse 'the Lord passed before him', the Targum says 'the Lord made His Shekhinah pass before him'. The Shekhinah is considered a body, but not a human body. Onkelos the proselyte further specifies that the deleted element could be glory, Shekhinah, or speech depending on context. The author notes that this method is also used in Islamic exegesis, e.g., interpreting 'your Lord comes' as 'the command of your Lord comes'. Maimonides approves of this approach and adds the possibility of substituting 'sound' as the subject of the action.

Key Terms
الترجوم
حذف المضاف
السكينة
المجد
انقولوس المتهود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Targum rejects the literal text entirely.
Correction: It retains the text but reinterprets by grammatical deletion, not denial.

Checkpoint
What grammatical method does the Targum use to avoid attributing corporeal actions to God?
A) It denies the literal meaning entirely.
B) It deletes the genitive construct, attributing the action to something like the Shekhinah.
C) It reinterprets the text metaphorically without any grammatical change.
D) It changes the pronouns.

Key Takeaway
The Targum's method of deleting the genitive preserves divine transcendence without denying the scriptural text.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read a verse that seems to describe God in human terms, do you feel a tension between literal acceptance and the need to guard His transcendence? How has this lesson shown you a path that does not require you to deny the text?
?
The Targumists inserted a created entity as an interpreter between the reader and the literal wording. In your own spiritual life, what 'created entities' – such as your own opinions, cultural assumptions, or theological biases – might you be inserting between you and the direct word of Allah? Are you willing to let them go to see the text as it truly intends?
?
Maimonides praised the Targum's method yet proposed an alternative substitution. Do you have the humility to learn from scholars of other traditions when they offer insights that uphold tawhid? Or do you reject good interpretation because of its source? How will you approach a difficult aya tomorrow with a heart open to grammatical wisdom?

Critique of Maimonides and the Talmudic Principle
Razi critiques Maimonides and cites the Talmudic principle

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Razi critiques Maimonides' interpretive approach and the foundational Talmudic principle that explains anthropomorphic language in scripture.

Core Idea
Anthropomorphic language is a concession to human intellect, not a description of God.

Razi begins by noting that Maimonides and Onkelos exerted themselves in interpretation. He suggests that if they had simply stated that God speaks according to human intellect while being utterly unlike creation, no interpretation would be needed. He then cites the Talmudic principle that 'the Torah speaks in the language of the sons of Adam,' which underpins the necessity of metaphorical interpretation.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening critique
Read the Arabic passage carefully. Notice Razi's criticism of Maimonides and Onkelos for excessive ta'wil.
The phrase 'laysa kamithlihi shay'' is a key Quranic principle.
2
Identify the alternative proposal
Razi says if they had simply said God speaks according to human intellect and is incomparable, it would have sufficed. Reflect on why this would eliminate the need for ta'wil.
3
Understand the Talmudic quote
The Talmud says 'The Torah spoke in the language of the sons of Adam'. This is the principle Razi uses to ground his argument. Consider its implications for interpreting scripture.
The quote is in Aramaic/Hebrew, but Razi provides its meaning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد أجهد ابن ميمون نفسه هو وأنقولوس فى التأويل . ولو أنهما قالا - على فرض صحة النص - أن الله تعالى يكام البشر على قدر عقوطم فليس كمثله شيء لكان هذا القول مغنيا عن التأويل . والتلمود قد ذكر قولنا هذا في ( بياموت (٧ أ ) وهو قولهم : د عبرت عنها التوراة بلسان بنی آدم . ومعناه : أن كل ما يمكن للناس أجمعين فهمه وتصوره بأول فكرة على نحو الكمال هو الذي يحب الله تعالى . ولذلك وصف بأوصاف تدل على الجسمانية لتدل على أنه تعالى موجود . إذ لا يدرك الجمهور بأول وهلة وجوداً إلا للجسم خاصة وما ليس بجسم أو موجود في جسم فليس هو موجوداً عندهم . ويقول ابن ميمون بعد ماذكر ما قدمنا خلاصته عن التلمود : إنه مع ارتفاع الجسمانية عن الله يرتفع ظاهر النص في نزل - صعد - سار - انتصب ـ وقف ـ دار - جلس ـ سكن - خرج - جاء ـ عبره و ما شابه ذلك . جاء : نستعمل على الحقيقة في إقبال الحيوان على موضع ما . وتستعمل مجازاً فى حلول الأمر الذى ليس بجسم أصلا. مثل : ما هو آت عليك ( أش١٥:٤٧) وعلى هذا المعنى المجازى تستعمل كلمة جاء فى حق الله تعالى . إما لحلول أمره ، ألو لحلول سكينته . مثل : ها أنا آت إليك في ظلمة الغمام ، (خر ٦:١٩)
And Maimonides exerted himself, he and Onkelos, in interpretation. And if they had said – assuming the correctness of the text – that God Almighty speaks to humans according to their intellects, and there is nothing like Him, this statement would have sufficed without interpretation. And the Talmud has mentioned this our statement in Yevamot (7a), which is their statement: 'The Torah expressed it in the language of the sons of Adam.' Its meaning: That everything that all people can understand and conceive at first thought in a perfect manner, that is what God Almighty loves. Therefore He is described with attributes that indicate corporeality to indicate that He, exalted, exists. For the common people do not perceive existence at first glance except for the body specifically, and whatever is not a body or existing in a body, it does not exist in their view. And Maimonides says, after mentioning the summary we presented from the Talmud: That with the removal of corporeality from God, the apparent meaning of the text falls away regarding: descended, ascended, walked, stood, turned, sat, dwelled, went out, came, passed, and similar things. Came: It is used literally for the approach of an animal to a place. And it is used metaphorically for the arrival of a matter that is not a body at all. Like: 'What is coming upon you' (Isaiah 15:47). And on this metaphorical meaning, the word 'came' is used regarding God Almighty, either for the arrival of His command, or the arrival of His Shekhinah. Like: 'Here I am coming to you in a thick cloud' (Ex 6:19).
Source pages p. 293

Commentary
This passage discusses the broader principle underlying tawil: the Talmudic rule 'The Torah speaks in the language of the sons of Adam.' The author argues that if Maimonides and Onkelos had simply stated that God communicates according to human intellect, they would not have needed elaborate interpretations. The Talmud explains that anthropomorphic descriptions are used because the common people can only conceive existence through bodies. Once one acknowledges God's transcendence, all apparent corporeal descriptions (descending, ascending, etc.) must be interpreted metaphorically. The specific case of 'came' is analyzed: literal for animals, metaphorical for non-bodily matters, and for God it means the arrival of His command or Shekhinah. This illustrates the principle of tawil based on the recognition of divine transcendence.

Key Terms
التأويل
التلمود
דיברה תורה כלשון בני אדם

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Talmudic principle justifies literal anthropomorphic interpretation.
Correction: The principle actually explains why anthropomorphic language is used, but requires metaphorical interpretation once God's transcendence is acknowledged.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what would have made Maimonides' and Onkelos' interpretation unnecessary?
A) Accepting that God is corporeal
B) Stating that God speaks according to human intellect and is incomparable
C) Rejecting the Talmudic principle
D) Using more allegorical language

Key Takeaway
The Talmudic principle 'Torah speaks in human language' supports metaphorical interpretation, not literalism.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read verses describing God's 'hand' or 'throne', do you find yourself struggling to reconcile them with His transcendence? This lesson suggests that the very language is a concession to your limited intellect. Are you willing to accept that your own mind is incapable of grasping God's reality, and that these descriptions are not literal? What would change in your heart if you truly internalized that?
?
Razi critiques Maimonides for excessive interpretation. Have you ever overcomplicated your understanding of a simple truth about Allah? Reflect on a time when you might have sought elaborate explanations instead of humbly accepting that 'there is nothing like Him.' How can you train yourself to trust that principle first?
?
The Talmudic principle 'Torah spoke in human language' is used to justify why God uses human terms. Does this principle make you more comfortable with the Quran's anthropomorphic verses, or does it still leave you uneasy? If you are honest, do you still harbor a secret belief that God has literal physical attributes? Commit to one specific verse you will read tomorrow with this principle in mind and see how it changes your understanding.

Why the Torah Uses Corporeal Descriptions
Razi explains the rationale for anthropomorphic language

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rationale behind anthropomorphic descriptions in scripture: to convey God's existence to the masses who can only conceive existence through bodies.

Core Idea
Scripture uses bodily language because the masses perceive existence only in bodies.

After citing the Talmudic principle, Razi explains its meaning. He says that the Torah uses corporeal descriptions because the common people can only understand existence through bodies. This necessity of accommodation explains why anthropomorphic language is employed, while still maintaining that God is beyond materiality.

Guided Path
1
Read the explanation of the principle
Read the Arabic passage from 'ومعناه' to 'ليس هو موجوداً عندهم'. Focus on Razi's interpretation of the Talmudic principle.
2
Understand the purpose of corporeal descriptions
Razi says God chose descriptions that indicate corporeality to indicate His existence. Why? Because the masses do not perceive existence in non-corporeal things.
3
Reflect on the limitation of human perception
The common people initially only conceive existence as belonging to bodies. Anything non-corporeal seems non-existent to them. Thus, scripture accommodates this limitation.
This is a key point in kalam theology.

The Metaphorical Interpretation of 'Came'
Maimonides applies metaphorical interpretation to anthropomorphic terms

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maimonides applies the principle of metaphorical interpretation to anthropomorphic terms, using 'came' as an example.

Core Idea
When applied to God, 'came' metaphorically means the arrival of His command or Shekhinah.

Razi now presents Maimonides' application of the principle. Maimonides lists several anthropomorphic verbs and then focuses on 'came' (جاء). He explains that 'came' is used literally for animals approaching a place, but metaphorically for non-physical matters. For God, it means the arrival of His command or His divine presence (Shekhinah), as in the verse 'Here I am coming to you in a thick cloud.'

Guided Path
1
Read Maimonides' list of verbs
Read the passage from 'ويقول ابن ميمون' to 'و ما شابه ذلك'. Note the list of verbs: descended, ascended, walked, etc.
These are all anthropomorphic terms that require ta'wil.
2
Focus on the analysis of 'came'
Razi then presents Maimonides' specific analysis of the word 'came'. Notice the distinction between literal use (for animals) and metaphorical use (for non-bodily matters).
3
Understand the application to God
Maimonides says when 'came' is used for God, it means the arrival of His command or His Shekhinah. The verse from Exodus is given as an example.
This illustrates the principle of ta'wil in practice.

Key Takeaway
Anthropomorphic terms like 'came' are interpreted metaphorically as the arrival of God's command or presence, not physical movement.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Literal and Metaphorical Meanings of Khuruj and Ruju'
Al-Razi distinguishes literal and metaphorical uses of khuruj and ruju' for God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why terms like 'coming out' and 'returning' must be understood metaphorically when applied to God, not literally as physical movement.

Core Idea
God's acts are not physical movements; khuruj and ruju' are metaphors for divine will.

Al-Razi addresses a key challenge in understanding divine attributes: when scripture speaks of God 'coming out' or 'returning,' it must not be taken literally. He establishes the literal meaning (physical motion) and then shows why metaphorical interpretation is necessary for God. This lesson clarifies the distinction and its theological implications.

Guided Path
1
Understand the literal meaning of khuruj
Read how al-Razi defines khuruj literally: the exit of a body from one place to another. This is the physical sense that cannot apply to God.
Notice the condition 'body' and 'place' — both incompatible with divine transcendence.
2
See how khuruj is used metaphorically
Al-Razi gives biblical examples where khuruj refers to the issuance of a command or decree. This shows it can mean 'appearance of a non-physical matter'.
The metaphor preserves the idea of manifestation without physical motion.
3
Understand the parallel for ruju'
Similarly, ruju' is metaphorically used for the withdrawal of a divine act, like God removing His sakinah (tranquil presence).
This echoes the same principle: no physical return, just change in divine manifestation.
4
Grasp the broader principle: God acts by will alone
Al-Razi emphasizes that God does not need tools or instruments; He acts solely by His will. Therefore, khuruj and ruju' are borrowed terms for the appearance and removal of acts.
This reinforces tanzih (transcendence) and guards against anthropomorphism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخروج على الحقيقة يستعمل في خروج جسم من موضع كان مستقراً فيه إلى موضع آخر. وعلى المجاز يستعمل في ظهور أمر معنوي ليس بجسم أصلاً، مثل: 'خرجت الكلمة من فم الملك' (أس 7: 8). وهذا يعني نفوذ الأمر. وعلى المجاز تفسر كلمة (هو ذا الرب يخرج من مكانه) (أش 26: 21) أعني: حدوث ما يحدث بعد أن لم يكن. إذ كل حادث من قبله تعالى ينسب لأمره. مثل: 'بكلمة الرب صنعت السموات' (مز 33: 6) تشبيهاً بالأفعال الصادرة عن الملوك التي آلتهم في تنفيذ إرادتهم: الكلام. وهو تعالى غير مفتقر لآلة يفعل بها، بل فعل بمجرد إرادته فقط. فلا كلام أيضاً بوجه، ولها استعير لظهور فعل من أفعاله: الخروج، استعير الرجوع لارتفاع ذلك الفعل بحسب الإرادة أيضاً، مثل: 'أمضى وأرجع إلى موضعي' (هوشع 5: 15) أي يرفع سكينته عن الناس، وإذا رفع سكينته بقى الناس هدفاً لكل ساعٍ أن يعرض ويتفق، فيكون الخير الذي يصيبهم بحسب الاتفاق. وهذا أمر عسير، لأن الله إذا حجب وجهه عن إنسان، فليقل هذا الإنسان: على الدنيا السلام.
Al-khuruj (coming out) in the literal sense is used for the exit of a body from a place in which it was settled to another place. In the metaphorical sense it is used for the appearance of a non-physical matter, not a body at all, such as: 'The word came out of the king's mouth' (Isaiah 7:8). And this means the penetration of the command. And metaphorically is interpreted the statement 'Behold, the Lord comes out of His place' (Isaiah 26:21), meaning: the occurrence of what happens after it had not been. For every event from Him, exalted is He, is attributed to His command. Such as: 'By the word of the Lord the heavens were made' (Psalm 33:6), by analogy to actions emanating from kings whose tool in executing their will is speech. And He, exalted, is not in need of a tool to act with, rather He acts by His will alone. So there is no speech either in any way, and metaphorically borrowed for the appearance of an act from His acts: al-khuruj, and al-ruju' (returning) is borrowed for the removal of that act according to the will as well, such as: 'I will go and return to My place' (Hosea 5:15), meaning He raises His sakinah (tranquility) from the people, and when He raises His sakinah, the people remain a target for every attacker to occur and meet, so the good that befalls them is by chance. And this is a difficult matter, because when God hides His face from a person, let that person say: Peace upon the world.
Source pages p. 295

Commentary
This passage defines the literal and metaphorical meanings of two key terms: 'coming out' (khuruj) and 'returning' (ruju'). The author first states the literal meaning of khuruj: physical exit of a body from one place to another. Then he explains that metaphorically, when applied to God, it means the manifestation of a non-physical matter, such as a command or decree. He supports this with biblical examples where 'coming out' refers to the issuance of a word or the appearance of a divine action. Similarly, 'returning' (ruju') metaphorically signifies the withdrawal of that divine act or presence, as in Hosea 5:15 where God 'returns to His place' meaning He removes His sakinah (tranquil presence) from people. The author emphasizes that God does not use tools or instruments; He acts solely by His will. Therefore, these terms do not imply physical movement or change. The passage concludes with a warning about the consequence when God withdraws His presence: people become vulnerable to random events.

Correction: Al-Razi explains that these terms are metaphorical, referring to the manifestation or withdrawal of divine will, not physical motion.

Literal and Metaphorical Meanings of Walking
Al-Razi defines walking literally and its metaphorical applications to God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that terms like walking when applied to God are metaphorical, not descriptions of physical movement.

Core Idea
Walking in scripture is metaphorical, not a description of God's physical movement.

This lesson examines how al-Razi handles the term 'walking' (al-mashy) in scripture. He first gives its literal definition as animal movement, then shows three distinct metaphorical usages. This clarifies that biblical descriptions of God walking are not to be taken literally.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Pay attention to the structure: first the literal meaning, then three metaphorical applications.
Notice how al-Razi uses biblical verses as examples.
2
Identify the literal and metaphorical definitions
Identify the literal definition of walking as 'specific movements of an animal'. Then list the three metaphorical uses: spread/appearance, removal of care, and virtuous conduct.
3
Understand the refutation of anthropomorphism
Al-Razi explicitly refutes Maimonides' interpretation of Genesis 3:8. He insists it is the sound that walks, not God Himself. This is key to maintaining God's transcendence.
4
Apply the lesson to other verses
Think of other scriptural descriptions of God that might be metaphorical. How does al-Razi's method help avoid anthropomorphism?
This step connects to the broader theme of divine simplicity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السير أو المشي. على الحقيقة هو الحركات المخصوصة الصادرة من الحيوان. وعلى المجاز يستعمل المشي لانتشار أمر ما وظهوره، وإن كان ذلك ليس بجسم أصلاً. مثل: صوتها كالحية يسرى (إر 46: 22)، ومثل: صوت الرب الإله وهو متمشى في الجنة (تك 3: 8). فالصوت هو الذي يسير ويمشى، لا الرب نفسه كما يقول ابن ميمون. ويستعمل الرفع العناية عن الشيء. مثل انصرف عن هذا الأمر وولاه ظهره. ومن المعنى 'واشتد غضب الرب عليهما ومضى' (عدد 12: 9) فمضى تعنى رفع الله عنايته عنهما، لا أنه سار ومشى على الحقيقة. ويستعمل أيضاً للسير بالسيرة الفاضلة دون تحرك أصلاً. مثل: 'هلموا لنسلك في نور الرب' (أش 2: 5) أي لنعمل بالشريعة.
Al-sayr (going) or al-mashy (walking). In the literal sense it is the specific movements emanating from an animal. In the metaphorical sense walking is used for the spread and appearance of some matter, even if that is not a body at all. Such as: 'Her voice hisses like a serpent' (Jeremiah 46:22), and such as: 'The voice of the Lord God walking in the garden' (Genesis 3:8). It is the sound that goes and walks, not the Lord Himself, as Ibn Maymun (Maimonides) says. And it is used for the removal of care from something, such as he turned away from this matter and turned his back to it. And from the meaning: 'The anger of the Lord burned against them and He went' (Numbers 12:9) – 'He went' means God removed His care from them, not that He walked literally. And it is also used for having virtuous conduct without any movement at all, such as: 'Come, let us walk in the light of the Lord' (Isaiah 2:5), i.e., let us act according to the law.
Source pages p. 295

Commentary
This passage defines the term 'walking' (al-sayr or al-mashy) by first stating its literal meaning: specific bodily movements of an animal. Then the author presents three metaphorical uses common in scripture. First, walking can mean the spread or appearance of something non-physical, as in the voice of God walking in the garden (Genesis 3:8). Al-Razi explicitly refutes the anthropomorphic interpretation of Maimonides (Ibn Maymun) by asserting that it is the sound that walks, not God Himself. Second, walking can mean the removal of care or protection, as in 'He went' (Numbers 12:9) where God withdrew His care from Miriam and Aaron. Third, walking can mean living a virtuous life without any physical movement, as in 'let us walk in the light of the Lord' (Isaiah 2:5), meaning to follow the law. The key point is that all physical descriptions of God are metaphorical for His actions or attributes.

Literal and Metaphorical Meaning of Dwelling (al-Sakan)
Al-Razi defines sakan literally and its metaphorical application to God's presence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Razi distinguishes the literal meaning of dwelling (al-sakan) from its metaphorical application to God, and why this distinction is crucial for maintaining divine transcendence.

Core Idea
Physical dwelling cannot be applied to God; divine 'dwelling' is a metaphor for His enduring care.

Al-Razi begins by establishing the literal meaning of dwelling as the permanence of an animal in a place. He then explains that when applied to God, the term is metaphorical, signifying the persistence of His care or tranquility. This lesson unpacks that distinction and the biblical examples he uses to illustrate the point.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the literal translation to grasp the flow.
2
Identify the two definitions
Find the literal definition (animal's permanent residence in a place) and the metaphorical definition (establishment of something non-physical).
Notice the shift from physical to abstract.
3
Examine the biblical examples
Look at the examples from Job 3:3-5 (cloud dwelling on a day) and Exodus 24:16 and 29:45 (God's glory dwelling). See how they illustrate the metaphor.
The cloud is not an animal, so 'dwelling' is figurative.
4
Understand the theological point
Realize that divine dwelling refers to God's enduring care and presence, not physical location. This preserves God's transcendence.
This is the key takeaway for Islamic theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السكن. على الحقيقة هو دوام المقيم في مكان ما، في ذلك المكان. فإنه بطول إقامة الحيوان في مكان ما، عاماً كان أو خاصاً، يقال فيه: إنه سكن في ذلك الموضع، وإن كان هو متحركاً فيه بلا شك. وعلى المجاز يستعمل السكن لكل أمر ثبت ولزم شيئاً آخر. مثل: 'ليته هلك اليوم الذي ولدت فيه والليل الذي قال: قد حبل برجل، ليكن ذلك اليوم ظلاماً، لا يعتن به الله من فوق، ولا يشرق عليه نهار، ليملكه الظلام وظل الموت، ليحل عليه سحاب، لترعبه كاسفات النهار' (أيوب 3: 3-5). فاليوم في النص ليس جسماً، والسحاب ليس حيواناً ليحل على اليوم. وعلى هذا المجاز إذا قيل مكن الله فإن معناه: دامت سكينته أو عنايته في أي موضع، أو معناه: دامت عنايته في أي أمر. مثل: 'وحل مجد الرب' (خر 24: 16)، وأسكن فيما بين بني إسرائيل، (خر 29: 45).
Al-sakan (dwelling). In the literal sense it is the permanence of a resident in a place, in that place. For by the length of an animal's stay in a place, general or specific, it is said: it dwells in that location, even though it undoubtedly moves within it. In the metaphorical sense dwelling is used for every matter that is established and adheres to something else. Such as: 'Let the day perish in which I was born, and the night that said: a man is conceived; let that day be darkness; let not God above regard it, nor let light shine upon it; let darkness and the shadow of death claim it; let a cloud dwell upon it; let the blackness of the day terrify it' (Job 3:3-5). The day in the text is not a body, and the cloud is not an animal to dwell upon the day. Based on this metaphor, when it is said 'God dwelt' (makan? or sakan?), its meaning is: His sakinah (tranquility) or His care persisted in any place, or His care persisted in any matter. Such as: 'The glory of the Lord settled' (Exodus 24:16), and He dwelt among the children of Israel (Exodus 29:45).
Source pages p. 296

Commentary
This passage defines 'dwelling' (sakan) by first giving the literal meaning: an animal's permanent residence in a place, even though it still moves within that place. Then the author explains the metaphorical usage when applied to God. Metaphorically, 'dwelling' denotes establishment or persistence of something non-physical. He illustrates with the example from Job 3:3-5, where a cloud is said to 'dwell' upon a day, though neither the day nor the cloud is an animal or body. Applied to God, 'dwelling' means that His sakinah (tranquil presence) or His care persists in a place or matter. The author cites Exodus 24:16 and 29:45, where God's glory or dwelling among Israel refers to His continual care and presence, not physical inhabitation. The key theological point is that divine dwelling is metaphorical for God's enduring attention and mercy.

Key Terms
السكن
الحقيقة
المجاز
السكينة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That 'dwelling' means God physically resides in a specific location.
Correction: Al-Razi explains that divine dwelling is metaphorical for God's enduring care and presence, not physical inhabitation.

Checkpoint

A) God occupies a physical space like a house
B) God's presence or care is established and persists
C) God becomes stationary and does not move

Key Takeaway

Literal and Metaphorical Meanings of Standing (al-Qiyam)
Al-Razi defines qiyam literally and metaphorically.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that 'standing' (al-qiyam) has both a literal physical meaning and a metaphorical meaning of undertaking and establishing a matter, which is important when interpreting divine attributes.

Core Idea
When Allah is described as 'standing,' it means He sustains, not that He stands upright.

Before discussing God's attribute al-Qayyum (the Self-Subsisting, the Sustainer), we need to clarify the root word qiyam. Al-Razi begins by distinguishing the literal sense — the physical posture of standing — from the metaphorical sense, which involves undertaking a task with firmness. This distinction is crucial in theology because applying a literal human posture to God would be anthropomorphism.

Guided Path

The passage is incomplete due to the end of the page, but it clearly explains the shift from physical posture to figurative commitment. In the context of divine attributes, this would relate to God's attribute al-Qayyum (the Self-Subsisting, the One who sustains all), though not explicitly mentioned here.

Metaphorical Interpretation of 'Closeness' (Qurb) for God
Ibn Maimon explains the metaphorical meaning of qurb for God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that scriptural references to God's closeness must be interpreted metaphorically as cognitive awareness, not spatial proximity.

Core Idea
God's closeness is about knowledge, not location.

This passage from the Asas al-Taqdis presents the principle of interpreting anthropomorphic expressions like 'closeness' when applied to God. Drawing on Ibn Maimon (Maimonides), the author argues that because God is not a body, any language of proximity must be understood figuratively, specifically as God's knowledge of the one who calls upon Him. This lesson unpacks that argument and its implications for reading scripture.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage carefully, then read the English translation. Notice the flow: an example from Isaiah, a quote from Maimonides, and an application to Psalm 145.
2
Identify the key claim
Maimonides states a general rule: every word of closeness or approach between God and creatures in scripture is metaphorical. Underline this claim in the translation.
3
Understand the rationale
The reason is that God is not a body. Since corporeality entails spatial relations, removing corporeality nullifies all spatial terms like near, far, contact, etc. Reflect on why this logic is necessary.
4
Apply to the example
Maimonides applies the rule to Psalm 145:18: 'The Lord is close to all who call upon Him.' He explains that this means closeness of knowledge (qurb 'ilm), i.e., God's cognitive apprehension of those who call, not spatial closeness.
Note that 'qurb 'ilm' is a technical term meaning epistemic proximity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بشفتيه : ( أش ۲۸ : ۱۳) وليس المعنى أن اليهود يتقربون إلى ذات الله تعالى بأفواههم على الحقيقة . يقول موسى بن ميمون : ( فكل لفظة من القرب والتقدم ، تجدها جاءت فى كتب النبوة بين الله تعالى وبين مخلوق من المخلوقات . فهي كلها من هذا المعنى الأخير ( المجازى ( لأن الله تعالى ليس هو جسما . فلا هو تعالى يدنو ولا يقرب من شيء . ولا شيء من الأشياء يقرب منه ، أو يدنو به - تعالى - إذ بارتفاع الجسمانية يرتفع المكان ويبطل كل قرب ودنو . أو بعد أو اتصال ، أو انفصال ، أو تماس ، أو تتال . وما أراك تشك ولا يلتبس عليك قوله - أى قول الله في التوراة - : « الرب قريب من جميع دعانه ، مز (۱٤٥ : ۱) فالمراد : قرب علم . أعنى : إدراك علمى لا قرب مكان ، -
With his lips: (Isaiah 28:13) And the meaning is not that the Jews draw near to the essence of God Almighty with their mouths in reality. Moses ben Maimon says: 'Every word of closeness and approach that you find occurring in the books of prophecy between God Almighty and any of the creatures — all of them are from this latter (metaphorical) meaning, because God Almighty is not a body. So He does not draw near nor approach anything, nor does anything approach Him or draw near to Him — exalted is He — because with the removal of corporeality, place is removed and every closeness, distance, connection, separation, contact, or sequence is nullified. And I do not think you doubt or are confused by His statement — i.e., God's statement in the Torah: "The Lord is close to all who call upon Him" (Psalm 145:18); what is intended is closeness of knowledge, i.e., cognitive apprehension, not spatial closeness.'
Source pages p. 298

Commentary
This passage is an explanation of the metaphorical interpretation of terms denoting 'closeness' (qurb) when applied to God. The author begins by citing an example from Isaiah 28:13 ('with his lips') and clarifies that it does not mean Jews literally draw near to God's essence with their mouths. Then he quotes Moses Maimonides, who makes a general rule: every instance of 'closeness' or 'approach' between God and creatures in scripture must be understood metaphorically. The reason is that God is not a body, so spatial closeness, distance, connection, etc., are inapplicable. Maimonides then applies this to Psalm 145:18, 'The Lord is close to all who call upon Him,' explaining that it means closeness of knowledge (qurb 'ilm), i.e., God's cognitive awareness, not spatial proximity. The passage thus teaches that anthropomorphic language about God's nearness must be interpreted figuratively.

Key Terms

مجاز
علم
جسم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some people think that 'closeness' to God is physical or spatial, such that God is literally near in location.
Correction: This passage clarifies that because God is not a body, spatial proximity is impossible. Instead, 'closeness' refers to God's knowledge (cognitive apprehension) of those who call upon Him.

Checkpoint

A) God is physically near those who pray
B) God has knowledge of those who call upon Him
C) God descends to be near them
D) God's essence is everywhere

Key Takeaway
When scripture says God is close, it means He knows those who call upon Him, not that He is spatially near.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Will you now actively remind yourself that He is not distant physically, but His knowledge encompasses you entirely?
?
This lesson says that every instance of closeness in scripture must be metaphorical. Does that make God feel less personal to you, or does it deepen your awe of His transcendence? Be honest: do you prefer a God you can imagine being near you in space, or one whose knowledge is so complete that spatial categories are irrelevant? Which belief demands more of your trust?
?
If God's closeness is about knowledge, then He knows every detail of your prayer before you utter it. Does that make you more careful in what you say, or does it stir a desire to know Him in return? What will you change today about how you approach Him, knowing that His 'closeness' is a call to align your heart with His omniscience?

Metaphorical Interpretation of 'Filling' for God
Ibn Maimon explains that 'filling' in scripture refers to completion or perfection, not literal spatial filling.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the term 'filling' (mala'a) when applied to God is metaphorical and refers to completion or perfection, not literal spatial occupation, and how the 'glory of the Lord' can be understood as a created light.

Core Idea
'Filling' for God means perfection, not physical occupation.

This passage from Ibn Maimon clarifies a crucial point about divine transcendence: words like 'fill' cannot be taken literally for God. We will explore the literal and metaphorical meanings of 'mala'a' and see how they apply to God, with an exception for the 'glory of the Lord'.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation to get the overall meaning.
Pay attention to the examples given.
2
Identify the three uses of 'mala'a'
Notice that the passage gives a literal meaning (body filling a body) and two metaphorical meanings (completion of time, perfection of virtue).
3
Apply to God
Ibn Maimon states that all instances of 'filling' attributed to God are metaphorical, not spatial.
This preserves divine transcendence.
4
Understand the exception
The 'glory of the Lord' can be interpreted as a created light that filled the tabernacle, allowing for a non-literal space-filling.
This does not imply God's essence occupies space.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كلمة ( ملا ، على الحقيقة : تدل على جسم يحل في جسم فيملاه : مثل : د ملات جرتها ، أى أن جسم الماء حل في جسم الجرة ، وعلى المجاز يستعمل فى معنى انقضاء زمان ما ، مقدر . مثل مكملت أيامها ، ( تك ٢٥ : (٢٤) ويستعمل أيضاً في معنى الكمال في الفضيلة ، والغاية فيها . مثل : د وكان ممتلئا حكمة وفهما ومعرفة ، (۱) مل ٧ : ١٤ ) يقول ابن ميمون : « وكل لفظة ملآن تجدها منسوبة الله فهى من هذا المعنى ، لا أن ثم جسما يملا مكانا . إلا أن تريد أن تجعل « مجد الرب : النور المخلوق الذي يسمى مجداً في كل موضع ، وهو الذي ملأ المسكن . فلا ضير في ذلك ، يريد أن يقول : لا ضير في أن تفسر مجد الرب بنور الرب . وهذا النور هو الذى ملأ مسكن الرب ، لا ذات الرب نفسه
The word 'mala'a' (filling) in reality indicates a body that enters a body and fills it, like: 'I filled her jar,' meaning that the body of water entered the body of the jar. And metaphorically it is used in the sense of the completion of a certain period of time, like 'completed her days' (Genesis 25:24). And it is also used in the sense of perfection in virtue and its utmost degree, like: 'he was full of wisdom, understanding, and knowledge' (1 Kings 7:14). Ibn Maimon says: 'Every word of "filling" that you find attributed to God is from this meaning, not that there is a body that fills a place. Unless you wish to make "the glory of the Lord" the created light that is called glory in every place, and it is what filled the tabernacle; there is no harm in that.' He means: there is no harm in interpreting the glory of the Lord as the light of the Lord, and this light is what filled the tabernacle of the Lord, not the essence of the Lord Himself.
Source pages p. 298

Commentary
This passage defines the literal and metaphorical senses of 'mala'a' (filling). Literally, it implies a body entering and occupying another body's space (e.g., water filling a jar). Metaphorically, it can mean the completion of a time period or the perfection of virtues (e.g., being full of wisdom). When applied to God, Ibn Maimon asserts that all instances of 'filling' are metaphorical. The only exception is if one interprets 'the glory of the Lord' as a created light that filled the tabernacle — then it is permissible to say that light filled the space, but not that God's essence did. Thus, the passage guards against attributing spatial occupation to God.

Key Terms
ملء
مجد الرب
نور مخلوق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The glory of the Lord filling the tabernacle means God's essence is physically present there.
Correction: The passage clarifies that it refers to a created light that filled the tabernacle, not God's essence.

Checkpoint

B) It means completion or perfection, not spatial filling
C) It means God's essence fills the universe
D) It is always literal for God

Key Takeaway
When scripture says God 'fills', it means perfection, not physical occupation; the 'glory of the Lord' is a created light.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

The lesson distinguishes between God's essence and a created light that fills the tabernacle. In your worship, do you sometimes confuse the created means (e.g., feelings, blessings) with God Himself? How would your prayer life change if you insisted on this distinction?

Metaphorical Meaning of 'Height' in Describing God
Ibn Maimon distinguishes literal and metaphorical uses of 'uluww' and interprets God's height as status.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the term 'height' (uluww) is used metaphorically for God, meaning exaltedness of status rather than physical elevation.

Core Idea
When describing God, height is about status, not space.

In this passage, we explore the linguistic distinction between literal and metaphorical meanings of 'height'. When applied to God, the word 'uluww' does not indicate a physical location above, but rather His supreme dignity and honor. This lesson unpacks that interpretation.

Guided Path
1
Understand the literal meaning of 'uluww'
Read the passage's first sentence defining the literal meaning as spatial elevation.
This is the common everyday meaning.
2
Learn the metaphorical meaning
The passage then lists metaphorical meanings: rank, prestige, majesty, etc. Recognize that for God, only the metaphorical applies.
3
See biblical examples
The passage cites verses like 'I raised you from the dust' and others, showing they carry metaphorical meaning.
These examples confirm the principle.
4
Apply to God
Understand that all descriptions of God's height are metaphorical, asserting His exalted status, not physical location.
This is key for divine transcendence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لفظ «العلو » على الحقيقة : هو لارتفاع المكان . وعلى المجاز هو لارتفاع الدرجة والمنزلة والجلالة والكرامة والعزة . مثل : « من أجل أنى رفعتك عن التراب ، (۱)صم ۱٦ : ۲) وماجاء في التوراة عن ارتفاع الله وعلوه فيه محمول على المجاز . مثل : ( اللهم ارتفع على السموات ، مز ٥٦ : ٦ ) : هكذا قال العلى الرفيع ، ( أش ٥٧ : ١٧ ) > وفى هذا الموضع يقول ابن ميمون: «إن الله تعالى عند المدركين الكاملين لا يوصف بأوصاف كثيرة ، وأن هذه الأوصاف المتعددة كلها التي تدل على التعظيم والعزة والقدرة والكمال والجود ، وغيرها . كلها ترجع المعنى واحد . وذلك المعنى هو ذاته ، لا شيء خارج عن الذات ثم يقول : ( إن العلو والرفع ليس معناه ومفهومه : علو مكان ، بل علو منزلة ،
The word 'uluww' (height/exaltation) in reality is for elevation of place. And metaphorically it is for elevation of rank, prestige, majesty, honor, and might. Like: 'Because I raised you from the dust' (1 Samuel 2:8). And what comes in the Torah about the height and exaltation of God is carried on the metaphorical sense. Like: 'O God, be exalted above the heavens' (Psalm 57:5); 'Thus says the High and Lofty One' (Isaiah 57:15). And in this place Ibn Maimon says: 'God Almighty, in the view of the perfect knowers, is not described with many attributes, and that these multiple attributes — all of which indicate majesty, might, power, perfection, generosity, and others — all return to one meaning. And that meaning is His essence, nothing outside the essence.' Then he says: 'Height and loftiness are not understood as elevation of place, but rather elevation of station.'
Source pages p. 298

Commentary
This passage begins by defining the literal and metaphorical meanings of 'uluww' (height/exaltation). Literally, it is spatial elevation; metaphorically, it is elevation of rank, honor, etc. The author cites verses about God's exaltation and asserts they are metaphorical. Then Ibn Maimon adds a profound point: in the view of those who truly know God, He is not described by many distinct attributes; rather, all attributes like majesty, power, perfection, generosity, etc., refer to a single reality — His essence. Therefore, 'height' and 'loftiness' when applied to God mean exaltedness of station, not spatial height. This passage teaches both the metaphorical interpretation of spatial language and the principle of divine simplicity (that God's attributes are not multiple in His essence).

Misconception: God is physically located above the heavens
Correction: God's height is metaphorical for His exalted status; He is not in a place.

A) Elevation of place
B) Elevation of rank
C) Both
D) None

Key Takeaway
The term 'height' for God is metaphorical, referring to His exalted status, not physical location.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?

Ibn Maimon on Unity of Divine Attributes
Ibn Maimon states that multiple divine attributes all refer to God's essence, and height means status.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ibn Maimon's view that multiple attributes of God (like might, power, perfection) all refer to a single reality—His essence.

Core Idea
God's many attributes are but one: His essence.

In this passage, Ibn Maimon addresses a deep theological point: how can we speak of God's many attributes without compromising His oneness? He explains that for those who truly know God, these attributes are not multiple in reality; they all return to one meaning—His essence. This lesson examines that statement.

Guided Path
1
Identify Ibn Maimon's starting point
Notice that he begins by saying that perfect knowers do not describe God with many attributes. This sets the stage.
2
Understand the attributes listed
He lists attributes like majesty, might, power, perfection, generosity. These are all we ascribe to God.
3
Grasp the key principle
All these attributes 'return to one meaning' which is His essence. They are not separate entities.
This is divine simplicity.
4
Apply to 'height'
He concludes that height means elevation of station, not place, consistent with the principle.
This ties back to the previous lesson.

Metaphorical Interpretation of Divine Anger
Ibn Maimon explains scripture on divine anger as metaphorical.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ibn Maimon interprets scriptural references to God's anger as metaphorical, not literal emotional change.

Core Idea
God's anger in scripture is metaphorical, not an emotional change in the Divine.

This passage from Ibn Maimon addresses a key challenge in understanding divine attributes: how to reconcile scriptural terms like 'anger' with God's transcendence. He explains that 'anger' (ghadab) literally denotes pain, but when applied to God, it takes metaphorical meanings such as grief or opposition. This lesson explores his analysis of the verse 'And He grieved in His heart' (Genesis 6:6), showing how to interpret such language without attributing change to God.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation to grasp Ibn Maimon's classification of literal and metaphorical senses of 'ghadab'.
Pay attention to the two metaphorical meanings.
2
Identify the metaphorical meanings
Note the two metaphorical senses: grief and opposition/disobedience. Understand how they apply to God.
3
Examine the interpretation of Genesis 6:6
See how Ibn Maimon applies both metaphorical meanings to the verse 'And He grieved in His heart', and understand the reasoning about God speaking according to human intellect.
This guards against literalist interpretations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
غضب : على الحقيقة يدل على الوجع والألم . مثل : « بالألم تلدين البنين ، ( تك ٣ : ١٦ ) وعلى المجاز ۱ - يأتي بمعنى الغم . مثل : ( ولم يكن أبوه يغمه فى أيامه ، (۱صم ١ : ٦) أى يغضبه ۲ - ويأتي بمعنى الخلاف والعصيان . مثل : « فى النهار كله يعرقلون أمورى ، ( مز ٦:٥٥ ) ومن . تتعرقل أموره يغضب . وعلى المجاز قال الله عن نفسه في التوراة : د وتأسف في قلبه ، ( تك ٦ : ٦) وتأويله على المعنى المجازى الأول : أن الله غضب عليهم لسو فعلهم . وقال : « في قلبه ، لأنه لم يقله لنبي في ذلك الوقت الذي نفذ فيه ذلك الفعل . ومثل ذلك - ولله المثل الأعلى - مثل الإنسان إذا ظهرت له فكرة لم يخير بها أحد ، يقول : كان في قلى كذا . ولأن الله يكلم الناس على قدر عقولهم عبر عن نفسه هكذا . أما هو فليس كمثله شيء . وتأويله على المعنى المجازى الثاني : إنهم خالن وا إرادة الله ، لأن القلب في نظر الماس محل للإرادة .
Anger (ghadab): in reality it denotes pain and suffering. Like: 'In pain you shall bring forth children' (Genesis 3:16). And metaphorically: 1) It comes in the sense of grief, like: 'And his father did not grieve him in his days' (1 Samuel 1:6), i.e., anger him. 2) And it comes in the sense of opposition and disobedience, like: 'All day long they thwart my affairs' (Psalm 55:6); one whose affairs are thwarted becomes angry. And metaphorically God said about Himself in the Torah: 'And He grieved in His heart' (Genesis 6:6). Its interpretation according to the first metaphorical meaning: that God was angry with them because of the evil of their deed. And He said 'in His heart' because He did not say it to a prophet at that time when He executed that act. And that is like — and for God is the highest example — like a man when a thought occurs to him that he does not inform anyone about, he says: 'It was in my heart' or 'in my mind.' And because God speaks to people according to the capacity of their intellects, He expressed Himself in this way. As for Him, nothing is like Him. And its interpretation according to the second metaphorical meaning: that they opposed the will of God, because the heart in people's view is the seat of will.
Source pages p. 299

Commentary
This passage explains the literal and metaphorical senses of 'ghadab' (anger). Literally, anger implies pain and suffering (e.g., childbirth pain). Metaphorically, it can mean grief or opposition/disobedience. When the Torah says 'And He grieved in His heart' (Genesis 6:6), Ibn Maimon offers two interpretations. First, it means God was angry due to their evil deeds, and 'in His heart' indicates that He did not announce it through a prophet at that time, analogous to a person having a thought he doesn't share. Second, it means that they opposed God's will, because the heart is considered the seat of will. The author notes that God speaks to people according to their intellectual capacity, so He uses human language, but He is unlike anything. This passage guards against attributing emotional changes or physical organs to God.

Key Terms
غضب
غم
خلاف
قلب
إرادة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God experiences literal anger as an emotional change.
Correction: Ibn Maimon explains that scriptural references to divine anger are metaphorical, either indicating grief or opposition, and God does not undergo emotional changes.

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Maimon, what does 'He grieved in His heart' (Genesis 6:6) mean?
A) God experienced literal sorrow
B) God was angry because of their evil deeds, and 'in His heart' indicates He did not announce it at that time
C) God's heart ached
D) God changed His mind

Key Takeaway
Scriptural references to God's anger are metaphorical, pointing to His response to sin, not an emotional change.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read about God's anger in scripture, do you ever feel that God is emotionally reactive like a human? How does this lesson challenge that view, and what does it mean for your trust in a God who is beyond changing emotions?
?
Ibn Maimon says God speaks according to the capacity of our intellects. Are you willing to accept that your understanding of God may be limited and metaphorical? How does that affect the way you approach verses that describe God in human terms?
?
If God's 'grief' is not literal but a reflection of His response to sin, then what is your response to sin? Are you grieved by your own disobedience, or do you take it lightly? How can you align your heart with what God 'metaphorically' grieves over?

Discussion of metaphorical interpretation of 'high' and Ibn Maymun's monistic attribute theory

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the term 'high' is interpreted metaphorically regarding God, and Ibn Maymun's view that all divine attributes refer to a single essence.

Core Idea
God's 'highness' is not spatial but of station and essence.

This lesson examines a passage that defends divine transcendence by interpreting anthropomorphic language figuratively. It quotes biblical examples and presents Ibn Maymun's monistic view of attributes. As you read, notice how the author distinguishes between literal and metaphorical meanings.

Ibn Maymun explicitly states that 'high' does not mean physical elevation but rather highness of station. This passage thus defends divine transcendence by interpreting anthropomorphic language figuratively.

Figurative Meanings of 'Anger' in Scripture
Definition and figurative interpretations of 'anger' in scripture

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that scriptural references to God's anger are figurative, not literal, and that they convey either grief or opposition and disobedience.

Core Idea
When scripture speaks of God's anger, it uses human language figuratively: either grief or opposition to sin.

The concept of divine anger can be confusing if taken literally. In this lesson, we explore the literal and figurative meanings of the word 'anger' (غضب) in scripture, and how scholars interpret passages that attribute anger to God. The key insight is that God's anger is always metaphorical, adapting to human understanding while preserving divine transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its full literal translation. Let the author's own definitions and examples come through before you analyze.
2
Identify the literal meaning of غضب
The passage first gives the literal meaning of 'anger': pain and suffering. Notice the biblical example from Genesis 3:16. This sets the foundation for the figurative uses.
3
Learn the two figurative senses
The passage lists two figurative meanings: grief and opposition/disobedience. Each is supported by a biblical verse. Memorize these senses as they are key to interpreting anthropomorphic language about God.
4
Apply to verses about God
Read how the passage applies these senses to Genesis 6:6, where God is said to be grieved in His heart. Two interpretations are given: either it means God was angry (first figurative sense) or that people opposed God's will (second figurative sense). The heart is explained as a metaphor for will.
This step demonstrates the method of ta'wil (figurative interpretation) to uphold divine transcendence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
غضب : على الحقيقة يدل على الوجع والألم . مثل : « بالألم تلدين البنين ، ( تك ٣ : ١٦ ) وعلى المجاز ۱ - يأتي بمعنى الغم . مثل : ( ولم يكن أبوه يغمه فى أيامه ، (۱صم ١ : ٦) أى يغضبه ۲ - ويأتي بمعنى الخلاف والعصيان . مثل : « فى النهار كله يعرقلون أمورى ، ( مز ٦:٥٥ ) ومن تتعرقل أموره يغضب . وعلى المجاز قال الله عن نفسه في التوراة : د وتأسف في قلبه ، ( تك ٦ : ٦) وتأويله على المعنى المجازى الأول : أن الله غضب عليهم لسو فعلهم . وقال : « في قلبه ، لأنه لم يقله لنبي في ذلك الوقت الذي نفذ فيه ذلك الفعل . ومثل ذلك - ولله المثل الأعلى - مثل الإنسان إذا ظهرت له فكرة لم يخير بها أحد ، يقول : كان في قلى كذا . ولأن الله يكلم الناس على قدر عقولهم عبر عن نفسه هكذا . أما هو فليس كمثله شيء . وتأويله على المعنى المجازى الثاني : إنهم خالفوا إرادة الله ، لأن القلب في نظر الناس محل للإرادة . و من ذلك : « هل قلبك مستقيم نظير قلبي ؟ ، (۲) مل ١٥:١٠ ) أى إرادتك في الاستقامة كإرادتي .
Anger: literally indicates pain and suffering. Example: 'In pain you shall bear children' (Genesis 3:16). Figuratively: 1) It comes in the sense of grief. Example: 'And his father did not grieve him in his days' (1 Samuel 1:6), i.e., anger him. 2) It comes in the sense of opposition and disobedience. Example: 'All day long they impede my affairs' (Psalm 55:6?) And whoever's affairs are impeded becomes angry. And figuratively God said of Himself in the Torah: 'And He was grieved in His heart' (Genesis 6:6). Its interpretation according to the first figurative meaning: that God was angry with them because of their evil deed. And He said 'in his heart' because He did not say it to a prophet at that time when He executed that deed. And the like of that – and for God is the highest similitude – is like a man who has a thought that he does not inform anyone about; he says 'it was in my heart such-and-such.' And because God speaks to people according to their intellects, He expressed Himself thus. As for Him, nothing is like Him. And its interpretation according to the second figurative meaning: that they opposed God's will, because the heart in people's view is the locus of will. And from that: 'Is your heart upright like my heart?' (2 Kings 15:10?) i.e., your will in uprightness is like my will.
Source pages p. 299

Commentary
This passage defines the literal and figurative meanings of 'anger' (غضب) in scripture. It first gives the literal meaning (pain/suffering), then provides two figurative senses: grief (غم) and opposition/disobedience (خلاف وعصيان). It then applies these to biblical verses about God's anger, explaining that when God is said to be grieved in His heart (Genesis 6:6), it is a figurative expression. The author offers two possible interpretations: either it means God was angry (first figurative sense), or it means people opposed God's will (second figurative sense). The passage emphasizes that anthropomorphic language is adapted to human understanding, and God is not like anything. Additional examples show 'heart' as a metaphor for will.

Figurative Meanings of 'Eating' in Scripture
Definition of 'eating' and its two figurative extensions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the literal word 'eating' (أكل) extends figuratively to both destruction and benefit, based on two distinct underlying meanings.

Core Idea
One physical act generates two opposite figurative meanings.

The word 'eating' appears in scripture and religious texts, but not always with the literal sense of consuming food. This passage from Imam Razi's 'Asas al-Taqdis' explains that language derived two core aspects from eating—destruction and growth—enabling it to be used figuratively for annihilation or for benefit. Understanding these extensions helps interpret scriptural language correctly and avoids crude anthropomorphism.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic definition of eating
Start by reading the Arabic passage carefully. Notice that it begins with the literal definition, then identifies two underlying meanings.
The key terms are 'تلاف' (destruction) and 'نمو' (growth).
2
Identify the two underlying meanings
The text states that language 'considered in eating two meanings': destruction/disappearance of the eaten thing, and growth of the animal from nourishment. Underline these.
3
See how each meaning yields a figurative use
The first meaning (destruction) leads to figurative use for anything involving annihilation, e.g., 'a land that eats its inhabitants'. The second (growth) leads to figurative use for anything beneficial, e.g., learning knowledge.
Examples are from Numbers 13:33 and a non-scriptural source.
4
Reflect on the purpose of this analysis
Ask yourself: why does Razi present this? It shows that scriptural references to 'eating' regarding God (e.g., 'eating offerings') should be understood figuratively, preserving divine transcendence.
This connects to the broader theme of tanzih.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أكل : على الحقيقة تدل على تناول الطعام . ثم إن اللغة لحظته فى الأكل معنيين : المعنى الأول : هو تلاف الشيء المأكول وذهابه . والمعنى الآخر هو نمو الحيوان لما يتناوله من الغذاء . وبحسب المعنى الأول يكون لفظ الأكل مجازاً مستعملا فى كل ما فيه تلف وإبادة مثل : أرض تأكل أهلها ، ( عد ۱۳ : ۳۳ ) وبحسب المعنى الثاني يكون لفظ الأكل مجازاً مستعملا في كل ما فيه نفع للانسان . كتعلم العلم والحكمة . مثل : « تعالى كل لحماً دسماً في بيت الربي ، ( بابا بنشرا (۱۲۲) )
Eating: literally indicates the consumption of food. Then language considered in eating two meanings: The first meaning: is the destruction and disappearance of the eaten thing. The other meaning: is the growth of the animal from what it consumes as nourishment. According to the first meaning, the word 'eating' is used figuratively in everything that involves destruction and annihilation, such as 'a land that eats its inhabitants' (Numbers 13:33). According to the second meaning, the word 'eating' is used figuratively in everything that benefits a person, like learning knowledge and wisdom. Example: 'Come, eat rich flesh in the house of the rabbi' (Papa Ben Shera? (122)).
Source pages p. 300

Commentary
This passage defines the literal and figurative uses of 'eating' (أكل). It identifies two core meanings in the root: destruction (تلاف) which leads to figurative use for annihilation, and growth/nourishment (نمو) which leads to figurative use for benefit. Examples are given from scripture (Numbers 13:33) and a non-scriptural source (Papa Ben Shera). The passage illustrates how a single physical act can generate opposite figurative extensions based on different underlying aspects.

Key Takeaway
