Figurative Meanings of 'Face' in Scripture
Definition of 'face' and its three figurative meanings in scripture

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that when scripture attributes a 'face' to God, it must be interpreted figuratively—as anger, presence, or care—to preserve divine transcendence.

Core Idea
God's 'face' in scripture never means a literal face.

This lesson examines how the word 'face' (وجه) is used in scripture, distinguishing its literal meaning from figurative ones. When applied to God, 'face' cannot be taken literally because God is not a body. Instead, theologians interpret it as referring to God's anger, His presence, or His care. This passage from al-Razi illustrates the method of ta'wil (figurative interpretation) to avoid anthropomorphism.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and its translation
First, read the Arabic text carefully. Then compare with the translation to get a sense of the arguments.
Notice the structure: literal meaning first, then three figurative senses.
2
Identify the three figurative meanings
The passage lists three figurative meanings: anger, presence, and care. See how each is supported by a biblical example.
3
Apply the principle to divine attributes
Reflect on why interpreting 'face of God' figuratively is necessary: God is not a body, so any literal reading would be anthropomorphic. This protects divine transcendence.
This is the core takeaway for tanzih.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وجه : على الحقيقة اسم لوجه الحيوان . وعلى المجاز يأتي بمعان منها : الغضب . مثل : « ولم يتغير وجهها أيضاً ، تك ٧:٤٠) أي لم تغضب . وعلى هذا المعنى المجازى يفسر غضب الله وسخطه . مثل « وجه الرب فرقهم ، إرع : (١٦) أى غضب الله عليهم ففرقهم ۲ - ويأتى الوجه مجازا عن حضرة الشخص ومقامه . مثل : ( ألا يجدف عليك في وجهك ، ؟ (أى (١١:١) أي يكذب عليك في وجودك . وعلى هذا المعنى المجازى يفسر أن الله كلم موسى وجهاً لوجه، في قوله : وكلم الرب موسى وجهاً لوجه خر ۳۳ : ۱۱ ) أي بدون واسطة ۳ - ويأتي الوجه مجازاً بمعنى العناية . مثل : « يرفع الرب وجهه نحوك ، ( عدد ٦ : ٢٦)
Face: literally is the name for the face of an animal. Figuratively, it comes with meanings including: anger. Example: 'And her face also did not change' (Genesis 40:7?) i.e., she did not get angry. And according to this figurative meaning, the anger and wrath of God are interpreted. Example: 'The Lord lift up His face toward you' (Numbers 6:26).

Commentary
This passage defines the literal meaning of 'face' (وجه) as the face of an animal, then lists three figurative senses: (1) anger, illustrated by verses where 'face' indicates displeasure; (2) presence or station, explaining that 'face to face' with God means without intermediary, not physical vision; (3) care or attention, as in God lifting His face toward someone. Each sense is applied to God to avoid anthropomorphism.

Metaphorical Interpretation of Scriptural Anthropomorphic Terms
Lexical analysis of six Hebrew Bible terms distinguishing literal and metaphorical usage.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how six key scriptural terms (eat, face, back, heart, spirit, leg) are analyzed to distinguish literal from metaphorical meanings, and how this method preserves divine transcendence.

Core Idea
Scriptural descriptions of God using body parts are always metaphorical, never literal.

This lesson examines six Hebrew Bible terms that are also foundational in Islamic scripture. The author demonstrates a lexical method: starting from the concrete physical meaning, then showing how the term expands to abstract concepts. This method is crucial for Islamic theology (kalam) to interpret anthropomorphic descriptions of Allah metaphorically, avoiding tashbih (likening God to creation). The passage provides scriptural evidence for each metaphorical usage.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic source passage once without analysis, then read the full English translation. Notice the consistent pattern: each term begins with the literal meaning, then lists metaphorical usages with biblical examples.
The passage uses Hebrew Bible references, but the method applies to Quranic verses as well.
2
Identify the six terms and their literal meanings
List the six words: akl (eat), wajh (face), akhr (back), qalb (heart), ruh (spirit), rijl (leg). For each, note the stated literal meaning. This establishes the concrete basis.
The literal meaning is the physical or tangible referent.
3
Observe the metaphorical extensions
For each term, the author provides one or more metaphorical meanings with scriptural evidence. For example, 'face' metaphorically means anger, presence, or care. Write down the metaphorical meanings for all six terms.
The pattern shows that body part terms extend to abstract concepts or divine attributes.
4
Connect to divine transcendence
Reflect on how the metaphorical interpretations prevent a literal reading that would ascribe a physical body to God. For instance, God's 'face' is not a physical face but His anger or favor; His 'heart' is His will and care.
This is the theological purpose: to affirm tanzih (transcendence).
5
Test your understanding with the checkpoint
Answer the multiple-choice question to check if you can apply the lesson. It asks about the metaphorical meaning of 'face' in a specific verse.
Only the passage can answer this correctly.

Source Text

وجه : على الحقيقة اسم لوجه الحيوان . وعلى المجاز يأتي بمعان منها : الغضب . مثل : « ولم يتغير وجهها أيضاً ، تك ٧:٤٠) أي لم تغضب . وعلى هذا المعنى المجازى يفسر غضب الله وسخطه . مثل « وجه الرب فرقهم ، إرع : (١٦) أى غضب الله عليهم ففرقهم ۲ - ويأتى الوجه مجازا عن حضرة الشخص ومقامه . مثل : ( ألا يجدف عليك في وجهك ، ؟ (أى (١١:١) أي يكذب عليك في وجودك . وعلى هذا المعنى المجازى يفسر أن الله كلم موسى وجهاً لوجه، في قوله : دوكلم الرب موسى وجهاً لوجه خر ۳۳ : ۱۱ ) أي بدون واسطة ۳ - ويأتي الوجه مجازاً بمعنى العناية . مثل : « يرفع الرب وجهه نحوك ، ( عدد ٦ : ٢٦)
أخر : على الحقيقة هو اسم للظهر. وعلى المجاز يأتي ظرف زمان بمعنى بعد . مثل : « ولا قام بعده مثله ، (۲) مل ۲۳ : ٢٥) ( بعد هذه الأمور، ( تك ١:١٥) ويأتي مجازاً بمعنى التبع واقتفاء الأثر في السير ، بسيرة شخص ما . مثل ( الرب إلهكم تتبعون ، ( تث ١٣ : ٤ ) وعلى هذا المعنى المجازى يفسر قول الله تعالى لموسى عليه السلام : « فتنظر قفای » (خو ۳۳ : ۳) أي تدرك ما تبعنى .
قلب : على الحقيقة هو اسم لعضو الحيوان الذي فيه مبدأ حياة كل ذى قلب . وعلى المجاز يأتى بمعان كثيرة منها : ١ - وسط الشيء مثل : إلى كبد السماء - ( تث ٤ : ۱۱) ۲ - الفكرة . مثل : ألم يكن قلي هناك؟ (۲) مله : (٢٦) یعنی : كنت حاضراً بفكرتى عندما جرى ٢ كذا وكذا ٣ - الرأى : مثل : كل سائر إسرائيل كانوا قلباً واحداً، ليقيموا داود ملكا ء ( ١ أ خ ۱۲ : (۳۹) أي على رأى واحــــــد ٤- الإرادة . مثل : أعطيكم رعاة على وفق قلى ، (إر ٣ : ١٥). وعلى المعنى المجازى هذا يفسر قول الله تعالى نفسه دستكون عيناى وقلبي هناك كل الأيام» (امل ۳:۹) بمعنى عنايتي وإرادتي ه - العقل . مثل : د قلب الحكيم عن يمينه ، ( جا ۱۰ : ۲ ) أي عقله كامل في كل الأمور .
روح : على الحقيقة هو موضوع للهواء . مثل : « وريح الله يرف - ( تك ١ : ٢ ) وعلى المجاز يستعمل بمعنى : ۱ - روح الحيوان . مثل : د فيه روح حياة ، تك : (1٥) ۲ - صعود روح الميت إلى الله . مثل ( ٧ ١٥ ٢ ه يعود الروح إلى الله الذى وهبه ، ( جا ۱۲ : ۷ ۳ - فيوضات الله . مثل : روح الرب تكام فى (۲) سم ٣ : (٤٢ - الغرض والإرادة . مثل : و بهراق روح مصر في داخلها وأبيد مشورتها، أش ۱۹ : ۳) أي تتشتت أغراضها ، ويخفى تدبيرها
رجل : على الحقيقة اسم عضو من الحيوان يمشى عليه . وعلى المجازر تكون الرجل .
And from that: 'Is your heart upright like my heart?' (2 Kings 15:10), meaning: your will in uprightness is like my will.
Eat: In the literal sense it indicates consumption of food. Then language observed in eating two meanings: The first meaning is the wearing away and disappearance of the eaten thing. The second meaning is the growth of the animal from what it consumes of nourishment. According to the first meaning, the word 'eat' is used metaphorically in everything that involves wearing away and destruction, like: 'The land eats its inhabitants' (Numbers 13:33). According to the second meaning, the word 'eat' is used metaphorically in everything that benefits man, like learning knowledge and wisdom, as in: 'Come, eat fat meat in the house of the rabbi' (Baba Batra 122).
Face: In the literal sense it is the name for the face of an animal. Metaphorically it comes with meanings including: anger, as in: 'And her face also did not change' (Genesis 7:4), i.e., she did not become angry. According to this metaphorical meaning, God's anger and wrath are interpreted, as in: 'The face of the Lord scattered them' (Psalm 68:16), i.e., God's anger upon them scattered them. 2 - The face comes metaphorically for the presence and station of a person, as in: 'Shall he not slander you to your face?' (Job 1:11), i.e., lie about you in your presence. According to this metaphorical meaning, it is interpreted that God spoke to Moses face to face, in His saying: 'And the Lord spoke to Moses face to face' (Exodus 33:11), i.e., without intermediary. 3 - The face comes metaphorically meaning care, as in: 'The Lord lift up His face toward you' (Numbers 6:26).
Back: In the literal sense it is the name for the back. Metaphorically it comes as a time adverb meaning after, as in: 'And none stood after him like him' (2 Kings 23:25) (after these things, Genesis 15:1). And it comes metaphorically meaning following and tracking the trace in walking, by the manner of someone, as in: 'The Lord your God you shall follow' (Deuteronomy 13:4). According to this metaphorical meaning, God's saying to Moses, peace be upon him, is interpreted: 'And you shall see my back' (Exodus 33:23), i.e., you will perceive what follows me.
Heart: In the literal sense it is the name for the organ of the animal in which is the principle of life for every possessor of a heart. Metaphorically it comes with many meanings, including: 1 - The middle of a thing, like: 'To the heart of heaven' (Deuteronomy 4:11). 2 - Thought, like: 'Was my heart not there?' (2 Kings 5:26), meaning: I was present with my thought when such-and-such happened. 3 - Opinion, like: 'All the assembly of Israel were as one heart to make David king' (1 Chronicles 12:39), i.e., of one opinion. 4 - Will, like: 'I will give you shepherds according to my heart' (Jeremiah 3:15). According to this metaphorical meaning, God's saying Himself is interpreted: 'My eyes and My heart will be there all the days' (1 Kings 9:3), meaning My care and My will. 5 - Intellect, like: 'The heart of the wise is at his right hand' (Ecclesiastes 10:2), i.e., his intellect is perfect in all matters.
Spirit: In the literal sense it is a designation for air, like: 'And the spirit of God hovered' (Genesis 1:2). Metaphorically it is used meaning: 1 - The spirit of the animal, like: 'In which is a spirit of life' (Genesis 6:15). 2 - The ascent of the spirit of the dead to God, like: 'The spirit returns to God who gave it' (Ecclesiastes 12:7). 3 - The effusions of God, like: 'The spirit of the Lord came upon' (Judges 3:42). 4 - Purpose and will, like: 'I will empty the spirit of Egypt within her and destroy its counsel' (Isaiah 19:3), i.e., its purposes are scattered and its planning is hidden.
Leg: In the literal sense it is the name for a limb of the animal on which it walks. Metaphorically, the leg [?].
Source pages p. 300

Commentary
This passage is a lexicological lesson distinguishing the literal (al-haqiqa) and metaphorical (al-majaz) meanings of six key words found in scripture (here, the Hebrew Bible). The purpose is to show that when divine attributes or actions are described using bodily or material terms, these are to be understood metaphorically, preventing anthropomorphism. The author proceeds word by word.

For each term, the author first states the literal meaning, then lists one or more metaphorical usages with scriptural examples. The first word, 'eat' (akl), has two literal senses: consumption causing disappearance, and consumption for growth. Metaphorically, it is used for destruction or benefit. Next, 'face' (wajh) literally means the animal's face. Metaphorically it denotes anger, presence, or care, as in God's face representing His anger or His favor. The third word, 'back' (akhr, likely a typo for 'zahr'), literally means the back. Metaphorically it means 'after' or 'following', used for God's back meaning His trace. Fourth, 'heart' (qalb) literally is the life-organ. Metaphorically it means center, thought, opinion, will, or intellect. Divine 'heart' thus refers to God's care and will. Fifth, 'spirit' (ruh) literally is air or wind. Metaphorically it means the animal's soul, ascending spirit, divine effusion, or purpose. Lastly, 'leg' (rijl) literally is the walking limb; metaphorical usage is truncated but implied. The consistent method is to start from the concrete physical meaning and then show how the term expands to abstract concepts, especially when applied to God, to avoid attributing literal body parts to Him.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that scriptural descriptions of God with body parts (e.g., face, hand) are literal, implying God has a physical form.
Correction: This passage demonstrates that such terms are metaphorical and are to be interpreted according to their non-physical, abstract meanings when applied to God.

Checkpoint

B) God's presence and station

Key Takeaway
Scriptural anthropomorphic language must be interpreted metaphorically to uphold God's transcendence and avoid attributing physicality to Him.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Will you now check yourself when you imagine God in human-like terms? What concrete step can you take to replace that image with a more transcendent one?

Literal and Metaphorical Meanings of 'Heart' (Qalb)
Author defines literal and five metaphorical uses of 'heart' in scripture

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the term 'heart' (qalb) has both a literal physical meaning and several metaphorical meanings, and how this distinction is used in kalam to interpret scriptural references to God's heart.

Core Idea
Scripture's mention of God's 'heart' is always metaphorical.

In kalam, words must be understood properly before theological arguments begin. This passage from Imam Razi defines the term 'heart' — a word that appears in scripture regarding both humans and God. By examining its literal and metaphorical uses, we learn a key principle: when God's attributes seem to imply physicality, they demand metaphorical interpretation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the structure: first literal definition, then five metaphorical meanings.
2
Understand the literal meaning
The author gives a straightforward definition of 'heart' as a physical organ. Why is it important to establish this first?
3
Explore the five metaphorical meanings
Each is given with a biblical example. See how the same word takes on different senses like thought, opinion, will, and intellect.
4
See the theological application
The author applies the metaphorical meaning of 'will' to interpret a verse about God's 'heart.' This is the core kalam method for handling anthropomorphic expressions.
5
Reflect on the broader principle
Why must we distinguish between literal and metaphorical when talking about God? This principle safeguards divine transcendence.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قلب : على الحقيقة هو اسم لعضو الحيوان الذي فيه مبدأ حياة كل ذى قلب . وعلى المجاز يأتى بمعان كثيرة منها : ١ - وسط الشيء مثل : إلى كبد السماء - ( تث ٤ : ۱۱) ۲ - الفكرة . مثل : ألم يكن قلبي هناك؟ (٢) يعني : كنت حاضراً بفكرتى عندما جرى [؟] كذا وكذا ٣ - الرأى : مثل : كل سائر إسرائيل كانوا قلباً واحداً، ليقيموا داود ملكا ( ١ أ خ ۱۲ : ۳۹) أي على رأى واحد ٤- الإرادة . مثل : أعطيكم رعاة على وفق قلبي ، (إر ٣ : ١٥). وعلى المعنى المجازى هذا يفسر قول الله تعالى [؟] : « وتكون عيناى وقلبي هناك كل الأيام » (امل ۳:۹) بمعنى عنايتي وإرادتي ه - العقل . مثل : « قلب الحكيم عن يمينه » ( جا ۱۰ : ۲ ) أي عقله كامل في كل الأمور .
The Heart (qalb): In reality it is the name of the organ of the animal in which is the principle of life for every possessor of heart. And metaphorically it comes with many meanings, among them: 1 - The middle of a thing, like: 'to the navel of the sky' (Deut. 4:11). 2 - Thought, like: 'Was my heart not there?' (meaning: I was present in my thought when such and such happened). 3 - Opinion, like: 'All the rest of Israel were one heart to make David king' (1 Chron. 12:39), i.e., of one opinion. 4 - Will, like: 'I will give you shepherds according to my heart' (Jer. 3:15). And by this metaphorical meaning is interpreted the saying of God Most High: 'And my eyes and my heart will be there all the days' (1 Kings 9:3) meaning my care and my will. 5 - Intellect, like: 'The heart of the wise is at his right hand' (Eccl. 10:2), i.e., his intellect is perfect in all matters.
Source pages p. 301

Commentary
This passage defines the term 'heart' (qalb) in two senses: a literal, physical sense as the organ of life, and then five metaphorical senses derived from scriptural usage. The author lists each metaphorical meaning with a biblical proof-text. This method underlies the broader theological principle that when scripture attributes a 'heart' to God (e.g., 'my eyes and my heart'), it must be interpreted metaphorically to mean God's attention, will, or knowledge—not a physical organ. The procedure is typical of kalam's approach to anthropomorphic expressions (mutashabihat).

Literal and Metaphorical Meanings of 'Spirit' (Ruh)
Author defines literal and four metaphorical uses of 'spirit' in scripture

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the literal meaning of 'spirit' and its four metaphorical uses in scripture, and how this supports the method of figurative interpretation to maintain divine transcendence.

Core Idea
The term 'spirit' in scripture is primarily wind, but it also has four important metaphorical uses.

In this passage, the author continues his discussion of scriptural terms that require careful interpretation. The word 'spirit' (ruh) appears in various contexts, some of which might suggest that God has a created wind as part of His essence. The author defines the literal meaning and then lists four metaphorical usages, demonstrating that when the term is applied to God, it must be understood figuratively. This lesson will guide you through these meanings and their theological significance.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then the English translation. Notice the distinct parts: the literal meaning and the four metaphorical meanings.
The passage includes biblical verses as examples.
2
Identify the literal meaning
Find what the author says is the literal (haqiqa) meaning of 'spirit' and the verse he cites.
3
List the four metaphorical meanings
Go through each of the four metaphorical (majaz) meanings and their corresponding scriptural proofs. Write them down.
4
Understand the theological point
Ask yourself why the author goes through this enumeration. Consider how this supports the principle of ta'wil (figurative interpretation) to avoid attributing literal wind to God.
This connects to the lesson's learning objective.

روح : على الحقيقة هو موضوع للهواء . مثل : « وريح الله يرف - ( تك ١ : ٢ ) وعلى المجاز يستعمل بمعنى : ۱ - روح الحيوان . مثل : د فيه روح حياة ، تك : (۱٥) ۲ - صعود روح الميت إلى الله . مثل ( ٧ ١٥ ٢ ه يعود الروح إلى الله الذى وهبه ، ( جا ۱۲ : ۷ ۳ - فيوضات الله . مثل : روح الرب تكام فى (۲) سم ٣ : (٤٢ - الغرض والإرادة . مثل : و بهراق روح مصر في داخلها وأبيد مشورتها، أش ۱۹ : ۳) أي تتشتت أغراضها ، ويخفى تدبيرها
The Spirit (ruh): In reality it is assigned to air, like: 'And the Spirit of God was hovering' (Gen. 1:2). And metaphorically it is used with the meaning: 1 - The animal spirit, like: 'in which is the spirit of life' (Gen. ?). 2 - The ascent of the spirit of the dead to God, like: 'the spirit returns to God who gave it' (Eccl. 12:7). 3 - The outpourings of God, like: 'The Spirit of the Lord clothed [؟]' (Judges ?). 4 - Purpose and will, like: 'I will pour out the spirit of Egypt within her and destroy its counsel' (Isa. 19:3), i.e., its purposes are scattered and its planning is hidden.

Commentary
This passage follows the same pattern as the previous lesson, now for the word 'ruh' (spirit/wind). The literal meaning is wind or air, exemplified by the verse 'the Spirit of God was hovering' (Gen. 1:2). Then four metaphorical meanings are listed: the animal life-spirit, the returning spirit of the dead to God, divine outpourings, and purpose/will. The author's intent is to show that when 'ruh' is predicated of God (e.g., 'Spirit of the Lord'), it does not denote a created wind or a part of God, but rather a divine outpouring or attribute. This again supports the method of figurative interpretation (ta'wil) to maintain divine transcendence.

Literal and Metaphorical Meanings of 'Foot' (Rijl)
Author defines literal and two metaphorical uses of 'foot' in scripture

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the literal meaning of 'foot' and its two key metaphorical senses (following and causation) as applied to problematic scriptural passages about God.

Core Idea
Scriptural references to God's 'feet' refer to His causes, not a physical organ.

Before diving into the source passage, consider why scriptural anthropomorphic expressions must be interpreted carefully. The author, al-Razi, shows that the word 'foot' (rijl) has a clear literal meaning but also two metaphorical senses that resolve apparent descriptions of God having physical limbs. This lesson unpacks those senses, focusing on how the second meaning (causation) is used to explain a verse from Zechariah.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Pay attention to the structure: literal meaning then the two metaphorical uses.
The passage is concise; note the scriptural examples.
2
Identify the two metaphorical meanings
Find the definitions of 'following' and 'causation' and see how they are supported by scriptural verses. Notice the transition from human examples to divine attribution.
The second meaning is applied to God.
3
Understand the application to God
Focus on the last sentence: 'And by this meaning the Torah says of God...' Realize that 'His feet' means the establishment of His causes (miracles). This is the key to avoiding corporealism.
This is the lesson's main takeaway.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
رجل : على الحقيقة اسم عضو من الحيوان يمشى عليه . وعلى المجازر تكون الرجل . ۱ - بمعنى التبع ، مثل : : اخرج أنت وجميع الشعب الذين في عقبك ، ( خر ٨:١١) أي الشعب التابع لك . ۲ - بمعنى : السببية . مثل قول يعقوب لخاله « ما كان لك قبلى قليل . فقد اتسع إلى كثير . وباركك الرب في أثرى ، ( تك ٣٠ : ٣٠ ) وفى أثرى تترجم لرجلى ، أي باركك بسبي . لأن الأمر الذي يكون « أجل شيء . يكون هذا الشيء سببا فى حصول الأمر : وعلى هذا المعنى تقول التوراة عن الله : « وتقف رجلاه في ذلك اليوم على جبل الزيتون ، ( زك ٤:١٤) تريد به : ثبات أسبابه . اعنى : العجائب التي تظهر حينئذ في ذلك الموضع ، العجائب التي سببها - أى فاعلها - الله
The Foot (rijl): In reality it is the name of an organ of the animal on which it walks. And metaphorically it is used: 1 - In the sense of following, like: 'Go out, you and all the people who are at your feet' (Exod. 8:11), i.e., the people following you. 2 - In the sense of causation, like Jacob's words to his uncle: 'You had little before me, and it has increased abundantly, and the Lord has blessed you at my foot' (Gen. 30:30). 'At my foot' is translated as 'because of me', i.e., He blessed you because of me. For a matter that is 'the most important' becomes the cause of the matter's occurrence. And by this meaning the Torah says of God: 'And his feet will stand on that day on the Mount of Olives' (Zech. 14:4), meaning the establishment of His causes, that is, the wonders that appear then in that place, the wonders whose cause—i.e., whose agent—is God.
Source pages p. 301

Commentary
This passage first gives the literal meaning of 'foot' (rijl) as a walking organ. Then it explains two metaphorical uses: (1) following, as 'those at your feet' means followers; (2) causation, as shown in the story of Jacob and Laban where 'at my foot' means 'because of me'. The second meaning is then applied to the problematic verse in Zechariah about God's feet standing on the Mount of Olives; the author interprets it as referring to the establishment of God's causes (i.e., the miracles) rather than a physical foot. Again, the principle of metaphorical interpretation avoids corporeal attribution to God.

تبع
سببية
مجاز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Assuming that scriptural mentions of God's feet refer to a physical body part for God.
Correction: The author shows that such references are metaphorical, meaning the causes or wonders of God, not a literal organ.

Checkpoint

C) Standing

Key Takeaway
Scriptural descriptions of God's feet are understood metaphorically to refer to His causes or the effects of His power, not to a physical limb.

Interpreting 'Under His Feet' in Exodus 24: Anqalus' Ta'wil
Author presents and endorses Anqalus' interpretation of a verse about seeing God

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Anqalus reinterpreted the anthropomorphic language in Exodus 24:9-10 to uphold divine transcendence.

Core Idea
A pronoun shift can save God's transcendence from a literal reading of Scripture.

The Torah passage in Exodus 24:9-10 has been a challenge for those who uphold God's absolute transcendence, because it says the elders saw God and under His feet was something like sapphire. How can a transcendent God have feet? This lesson examines a clever interpretive move by Anqalus that shifts the reference of the pronoun 'His' from God to the throne, thereby preserving tanzih.

Guided Path
1
Read the problematic verse
Read the Arabic translation of Exodus 24:9-10 provided in the passage. Notice the literal wording: 'under His feet'. This is the anthropomorphic expression that needs interpretation.
2
Understand Anqalus' solution
Anqalus proposes that the pronoun 'His' in 'His feet' refers not to God but to the throne. This yields 'under the feet of the throne'. Why does this avoid corporealism?
The throne is a created entity, so attributing feet to it does not attribute feet to God.
3
Notice the significance of 'glory'
Anqalus notes the verse says 'under the throne of His glory' rather than just 'under His throne'. The addition of 'glory' is deliberate: it points to the Shekhinah, a created light, further distancing God from physical description.
This shows careful attention to the exact wording of Scripture.
4
Reflect on the method of ta'wil
Consider how this example illustrates the broader method of ta'wil (figurative interpretation) used by scholars to reconcile scriptural texts with rational principles of tanzih.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد جاء عن الله في التوراة : « ثم صعد موسى وهرون وناداب وأبيهو، وسبعون من شيوخ إسرائيل ، ورأوا إله إسرائيل وتحت رجليه شبه صنعة من العقيق الأزرق الشفاف ، وكذات السماء في النقاوة ، خر) ٩:٢٤-١٠) وقد تأوله أنفلوس بأن جعل الهاء فى رجليه عائدة إلى الكرسي . أى وتحت رجلى الكرسى اقوله : : وتحت کرسی مجده ، ويقول انقلوس: إنه لم يقل : وتحت كرسيه فقط ، وإنما قال : . وتحت كرسى مجده ، لأنه لو قال و تحت كرسيه فقط ، لزم كونه ـ تعالى ـ جسما ، ومستويا على جسم الكرسى . أما نسبته الكرمى إلى ( مجده، فلا يلزم منه التجسيم ، لأن المجد يؤول حينئذ بالسكينة ، وهى فور مخلوق.
And it came in the Torah from God: 'Then Moses, Aaron, Nadab, Abihu, and seventy of the elders of Israel went up, and they saw the God of Israel, and under His feet was something like a work of clear sapphire, and like the sky in purity' (Exod. 24:9-10). And Anqalus interpreted it by making the pronoun in 'His feet' refer to the throne. That is, 'under the feet of the throne', he says: 'under the throne of His glory'. And Anqalus says: He did not say only 'under His throne', but rather He said 'under the throne of His glory', because if He said only 'under His throne', it would necessitate that He—Exalted is He—is a body, sitting on the body of the throne. But as for attributing it to 'His glory', it does not necessitate corporealism, because 'glory' then refers to the Shekhinah, which is a created light.
Source pages p. 302

Commentary
This passage presents a specific problematic verse: Exodus 24:9-10 says the elders saw God and under His feet was a sapphire pavement. The author reports the interpretation of Anqalus (possibly Aquila the translator) who solves the anthropomorphism by making the pronoun 'his' in 'his feet' refer to the throne, not to God. Thus the phrase becomes 'under the feet of the throne', which is then paraphrased as 'under the throne of His glory'. Anqalus argues that the addition of 'glory' is deliberate: if only 'throne' were mentioned, it would imply that God sits on a physical throne, implying a body. But 'glory' points to the Shekhinah (the divine presence), which is a created light, thereby avoiding any attribution of corporeality to God. This is a classic example of ta'wil (figurative interpretation) to uphold tanzih (transcendence).

Correction: Anqalus shows that the pronoun can be shifted to refer to the throne, and the addition of 'glory' points to a created light, avoiding any attribution of body parts to God.

Key Takeaway
By reinterpreting the pronoun and emphasizing the word 'glory', Anqalus successfully defends divine transcendence against anthropomorphism.

Two Meanings of 'Achar' and Interpreting Anthropomorphic Verses
Two meanings of 'achar' applied to refute anthropomorphism

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the term 'achar' has two meanings—following and causality—and how this linguistic insight is used to reinterpret anthropomorphic scriptural passages about God.

Core Idea
When God's 'foot' is mentioned, it speaks of His cause, not His body.

This passage from Imam Razi explores a key method for understanding apparently anthropomorphic language in scripture: identifying the multiple meanings of a term. By examining the Hebrew word 'achar' (translated as foot/heel), we see how it can mean either 'following' or 'causality'. This insight allows us to interpret verses that ascribe feet or location to Allah as metaphorical references to His actions and creation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the use of biblical examples from Exodus, Genesis, and Zechariah.
2
Identify the two meanings of 'achar'
The passage presents two meanings: following (al-taba') and causality (al-sababiyya). Find the examples for each and note how they refute literal interpretations.
3
Examine the Exodus 24 interpretation
The passage quotes 'under His feet' and then gives two interpretations: Aquila redirects the pronoun to the throne, and Ibn Maymun interprets it as 'from His cause'. Understand how each avoids anthropomorphism.
4
Reflect on the first matter analogy
The passage ends with an analogy comparing the transparent stone to prime matter. Consider why this supports the idea that God alone is the Creator.
This part connects Aristotelian physics with scriptural exegesis.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بمعنى التبع، مثل: اخرج أنت وجميع الشعب الذين في عقبك (خر ٨:١١) أي الشعب التابع لك. بمعنى السببية، مثل قول يعقوب لخاله: ما كان لك قبلي قليل، فقد اتسع إلى كثير، وباركك الرب في أثرى (تك ٣٠:٣٠). وفي أثرى تترجم لرجلي، أي باركك بسببي. لأن الأمر الذي يكون أجل شيء، يكون هذا الشيء سببا في حصول الأمر. وعلى هذا المعنى تقول التوراة عن الله: وتقف رجلاه في ذلك اليوم على جبل الزيتون (زك ٤:١٤) تريد به ثبات أسبابه. اعني: العجائب التي تظهر حينئذ في ذلك الموضع، العجائب التي سببها - أي فاعلها - الله. وقد جاء عن الله في التوراة: ثم صعد موسى وهرون وناداب وأبيهو، وسبعون من شيوخ إسرائيل، ورأوا إله إسرائيل وتحت رجليه شبه صنعة من العقيق الأزرق الشفاف، وكذات السماء في النقاوة (خر ٢٤:٩-١٠). وقد تأوله أنفلوس بأن جعل الهاء في رجليه عائدة إلى الكرسي، أي وتحت رجلي الكرسي. أقول: وتحت كرسي مجده. ويقول أنفلوس: إنه لم يقل: وتحت كرسيه فقط، وإنما قال: وتحت كرسي مجده، لأنه لو قال وتحت كرسيه فقط، لزم كونه تعالى جسما، ومستويا على جسم الكرسي. أما نسبته الكرمى إلى مجده، فلا يلزم منه التجسيم، لأن المجد يؤول حينئذ بالسكينة، وهي فور مخلوق. هو وقد ناوله ابن ميمون بأن تحت رجليه يريد به: من سببه ومن أجله. والذي أدركوه: هو حقيقة المادة الأولى للأرض، التي هي من خلقه تعالى، وهو سبب وجودها. وما هي المادة الأولى؟ إن حجر العقيق ويترجم بحجر الياقوت عبارة عن الشفاف، لا عن اللون الأزرق، ويترجم باللون الأبيض. والشفاف عادم الألوان كلها، ولذلك يقبل الألوان كلها بالتعاقب. وقد شبه الله المادة الأولى التي خلق منها الأرض بالشفاف، لأن المادة الأولى عديمة الصور وهي تقبل الصور بالتعاقب، وتقبل أن تتشكل كما يريد الله. وقد أراهم الله الشفاف الذي هو شبيه بمادة الأرض الأولى القابلة للتشكل والبقاء والفساد. ليعبدوا أن المبدع والخالق هو الله لا غير.
In the sense of following, like: 'Go out, you and all the people who are in your heel' (Ex 8:11), meaning the people following you. In the sense of causality, like Jacob's statement to his uncle: 'What I had before you was little, but it has increased greatly, and the LORD has blessed you in my heel' (Gen 30:30). And 'in my heel' is translated as 'in my foot', meaning He blessed you because of me. Because when a matter is the ultimate thing, that thing is the cause of the occurrence of the matter. And in this sense the Torah says about God: 'And His feet will stand on that day on the Mount of Olives' (Zech 4:14), intending by it the steadiness of His causes. That is: the wonders that appear then in that place, the wonders whose cause – i.e., their doer – is God. And it has come in the Torah about God: 'Then Moses went up, and Aaron, Nadab, Abihu, and seventy of the elders of Israel, and they saw the God of Israel, and under His feet was like a work of transparent blue sapphire, and like the heavens in clarity' (Ex 24:9-10). Aquila interpreted it by making the pronoun in 'His feet' refer to the throne, i.e., 'under the feet of the throne'. I say: under the throne of His glory. And Aquila says: He did not say 'under His throne' only, but he said 'under the throne of His glory', because if He had said 'under His throne' only, it would necessitate Him – exalted – being a body, and sitting on a body of the throne. As for relating the throne to His glory, it does not necessitate anthropomorphism, because 'glory' then refers to the Shekhinah, and it is a created something. It – and Ibn Maymun transmitted that 'under His feet' intends by it: from His cause and for His sake. And what they perceived is the reality of the first matter of the earth, which is from His creation – exalted – and He is the cause of its existence. And what is the first matter? The sapphire stone, translated as ruby, is an expression of the transparent, not the blue color, and it is translated as white color. The transparent is devoid of all colors, and therefore accepts all colors successively. And God likened the first matter from which He created the earth to the transparent, because the first matter is devoid of forms and it accepts forms successively, and it accepts to be shaped as God wills. And God showed them the transparent which is similar to the first matter of the earth, capable of shape, permanence, and decay, so that they might worship that the Originator and Creator is God alone.
Source pages p. 302

Commentary
This passage is an interpretation of anthropomorphic expressions in the Torah, particularly those that ascribe feet or location to God. The author begins by explaining two meanings of the term 'achar' (translated as 'heel' or 'foot'): the sense of following (taba') and the sense of causality (sababiyya). Examples from Exodus, Genesis, and Zechariah demonstrate that when a term like 'foot' is used of God, it intends not a physical foot but the idea of causality or steadfastness of His causes. The passage then addresses the Exodus verse where the elders saw God and under His feet was a pavement of sapphire. Aquila interprets this by redirecting the pronoun 'His' to the throne, avoiding God's direct contact with anything. The author adds his own clarification: 'under the throne of His glory' – the glory refers to the Shekhinah, a created entity, so no anthropomorphism follows. Ibn Maymun (Maimonides) interprets 'under His feet' as 'from His cause and for His sake', and what the elders perceived was the first matter (al-madda al-ula) of the earth, a creation of God. The author then explains the nature of first matter by analogy with a transparent stone (sapphire or ruby) that is colorless and receives all colors. God showed the elders this transparent substance, similar to prime matter, to prove that God alone is the Creator and Originator, since first matter is passive and receives forms from God. The overall method is to employ linguistic and philosophical reasoning to reinterpret anthropomorphic texts as metaphorical references to God's actions and causation.

Correction: The passage shows that 'foot' (achar) can mean causality or following, so verses about God's feet are metaphorical references to His actions or effects, not His body.

Key Takeaway
Scriptural references to God's 'feet' are not literal but metaphorical, pointing to His causation or the effects of His power, thus preserving divine transcendence.

The elders saw a transparent stone that receives all shapes. Are you like that stone before God—passive, receptive, and willing to be shaped by His will—or do you resist, insisting on your own form? What shape is God trying to give you right now that you are fighting?
?
Jacob said to Laban, 'The LORD has blessed you in my heel'—meaning God blessed Laban because of Jacob. In your life, do you see the blessings you receive as coming 'because of' God's grace through others, or do you claim them as your own doing? How would you live differently if you acknowledged God as the cause of every good thing?

Ibn Maymun's Critique of Pre-Existent Matter
Ibn Maymun quotes a rabbinic tradition and critiques its assumption of pre‑existing matter.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the belief in creation from pre-existing matter leads to an infinite regress, and why Islamic theology insists on creation ex nihilo (خلق من لا شيء).

Core Idea
Creation from nothing is the only way to avoid infinite regress.

Ibn Maymun (Maimonides) engages with a Jewish tradition about creation from light and snow. His logical critique exposes a flaw: if everything comes from something, then God's raw materials themselves would need a source. This reinforces the Islamic doctrine of creation ex nihilo.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once, then its literal translation. Let the original voices of Rabbi Eliezer and Ibn Maymun come through before analysing.
Pay attention to the biblical citations in the rabbinic account.
2
Identify the two positions
First, identify the rabbinic view: heaven from light, earth from snow. Then identify Ibn Maymun's question: 'Should I believe that it is impossible for something to come from nothing, or is matter necessary?'
Ibn Maymun is questioning the need for pre-existing matter.
3
Understand the infinite regress
Notice Ibn Maymun's next point: if you ask 'from where' for heaven and earth, the answer refers to further materials. Then you must ask 'from where' for those materials, leading to a chain with no end. This shows the absurdity of rejecting creation ex nihilo.
The argument is a classic proof for creation from nothing.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويقول ابن ميمون: إن التأويل الذي ذهبت إليه، قد نبهني عليه الربي اليعازار بن هو رقانوس، في قوله: من أين خلقت السماء؟ من نور ردائه أخذه وبسطه كالثوب. وكما قيل: أنت الملتحف بالنور كرداء، الباسط السماء كسجف، منبسطين (مز ١٠٤:٢). من أين خلقت الأرض؟ من الثلج الذي تحت كرسى مجده. وكما قيل: وأخذ قسها منه ورماه، يقول الثلج: اسقط على الأرض (أي ٣٦:٦). هذا قول الربي أليعازار بن هورقانوس بنصه. وقد عقب عليه ابن ميمون بما نصه: يا ليت شعري. هذا الحكم. أي شيء أعتقد؟ هل اعتقد أنه من المحال أن يوجد شيء من لا شيء؟ أو لا بد من مادة يتكون منها ما يتكون؟ ولذلك طلب للسماء والأرض: من أين خلقا؟ وأي شيم حصل من هذا الجواب؟ يلز أن يقال له: من أين خلق نور ردائه؟
And Ibn Maymun says: Indeed, the interpretation that I have adopted was prompted to me by Rabbi Eliezer ben Hyrcanus, in his statement: 'From where was heaven created? From the light of His garment; He took it and spread it like a garment, as it is said: You wrap Yourself with light as with a cloak, stretching out the heavens like a curtain, spreading them out (Ps 104:2). From where was earth created? From the snow that is under the throne of His glory, as it is said: He takes its portion from it and throws it; the snow says: Fall on the earth (Job 36:6). This is the statement of Rabbi Eliezer ben Hyrcanus verbatim. And Ibn Maymun commented on it as follows: Would that I knew. This judgment: what should I believe? Should I believe that it is impossible for something to come from nothing? Or is matter necessary from which that which is formed is formed? Therefore he asked for heaven and earth: 'From where were they created?' And what thing is obtained from this answer? It must be said to him: From where was the light of His garment created?
Source pages p. 303

Commentary
This passage is a quotation and critique of a rabbinic tradition about creation. Ibn Maymun (Maimonides) presents the teaching of Rabbi Eliezer ben Hyrcanus, who claimed that heaven was created from the light of God's garment and earth from snow under the throne of His glory. This view assumes that creation requires pre-existing matter. Ibn Maymun then questions this assumption: he wonders whether it is impossible for something to come from nothing (ex nihilo) or whether matter is necessary. He points out that if one insists on a material origin, then one must also ask where the light of God's garment came from, leading to an infinite regress. The critique is a standard argument for creation ex nihilo in Islamic theology: if everything must come from something, then God's act of creation would require a pre-existing substrate, which itself would need a creator, ad infinitum. Thus, the only consistent position is that God creates directly from nothing. The passage demonstrates the theological methodology of using logical reasoning to dismantle flawed interpretations and uphold divine transcendence and omnipotence.

Key Terms
الوجود من لا شيء
المادة
الخلق
الرداء
الثلج
الكرسي
المجد
السؤال عن المادة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Creation must always involve pre-existing material.
Correction: This leads to infinite regress; the only sound view is creation ex nihilo, as argued by Ibn Maymun.

Checkpoint
What is Ibn Maymun's main objection to Rabbi Eliezer's account of creation?
A) It does not mention the Shekhinah
B) It implies that God's garment is pre-existing matter
C) It necessitates an infinite regress of materials
D) It contradicts the Torah

Key Takeaway
Creation from pre-existing matter is logically untenable because it leads to an infinite regress; therefore, the only rational belief is that God creates from nothing.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you believe that God creates from nothing, do you truly live as if everything you have is a direct gift from Him, or do you still feel entitled because you 'made' something from pre‑existing materials?
?
The rabbinic tradition tries to find a 'from where' for creation. In your own life, do you ever try to explain away God's direct involvement by pointing to secondary causes, avoiding the full weight of His absolute power?
?
Ibn Maymun's critique forces a choice: either accept infinite regress or submit to creation ex nihilo. Is there an area of your faith where you prefer a comfortable but logically flawed explanation over the demanding truth of God's unfettered sovereignty?

Critique of Rabbi Eliezer on the Throne and Creation
Razi critiques Rabbi Eliezer's view on the throne as eternal and its implications.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam Razi's critique of Rabbi Eliezer's views on the throne of glory and the theological problems with equating divine attributes with created objects.

Core Idea
Literal interpretations of divine attributes can lead to theological absurdities.

Imam Razi engages with Jewish theological sources to critique a literalist interpretation of the throne of glory. He reveals the inconsistencies in Rabbi Eliezer's view and warns against mixing divine attributes with creation. This lesson traces his argument and its implications for tawhid.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the full translation to grasp Razi's argument flow.
The passage contains references to Talmud and Bible—notice how Razi uses them critically.
2
Identify Rabbi Eliezer's claims
From the passage, what does Rabbi Eliezer say about the throne of glory and the light of God's robe? How does Maimonides interpret him?
The key claim is that the throne and 'light of the robe' may be uncreated.
3
Trace Razi's logical critique
Follow Razi's questioning: If the throne is uncreated, it becomes a divine attribute—can an attribute form a material body? Why is this absurd?
4
Consider the scriptural evidence
Razi examines Bible verses (Psalm 102:19, Lamentations 5:19) and notes their ambiguity. How do these verses relate to the arguments?
5
Reflect on the overall lesson
What does this critique teach about interpreting divine attributes? How does it preserve God's transcendence?
This connects to the core idea of avoiding anthropomorphism.

و من أبن خلق الثلج الذي تحت كرسى المجد ؟ ومن أي شيء خلق كرسى المجد نفسه ؟ فإن كان يريد بنور ردائه شيئاً غير مخلوق ، وكذلك كرسى المجد غير مخلوق . فهذا شفيع جداً . فيكون قد أقر بقدم العالم . غير أنه على رأى أفلاطون . أما كون كرسى المجد من المخلوقات . فالحكماء ينصون بذلك ، لسكن على وجه عجيب . قالوا : إنه خلق قبل خلق العالم ) ندريم ٣٩ ب ) أما نصوص الكتب فلم تذكر فيه خلقاً بوجه ، سوى قول داود: « الرب أقر عرشه فى السماء . ( مز ۱۰۲ : ۱۹ ) وهو قول يحتمل التأويل جداً . أما التأبيد فيه : فمنصوص : أنت الرب تجلس عرشك أبدأ من جيل إلى جيل ، ( مى ٥: ١٩ ) فإن كان الربي اليعازار يعتقد قدم العرش . فيكون إذا صفة الله لا جسما مخلوقا . فكيف يمكن أن يتكون شيء من صفة ؟ وأعجب شيء قوله : « نور ردائه ، ا . ه . ثم يقول ابن ميمون : إنه يستفاد من كلام الربى اليعازار : أن مادة السماء غير مادة الأرض . فادة السماء من نور الرداء ، ومادة الأرض من الثلج الذي تحت كرسى المجد . ولذلك أراهم « الشفاف ، ليدل به على مادة الأرض .
And from where was the snow that is under the throne of glory created? And from what thing was the throne of glory itself created? If he intends by 'the light of his robe' something uncreated, and likewise the throne of glory is uncreated, then this is very intercessory? Thus he would have affirmed the eternity of the world, except that it is according to Plato's opinion. As for the throne of glory being among the created things, the sages affirm that, because it is in a wondrous manner. They said: It was created before the creation of the world (Nedarim 39b). As for the texts of the scriptures, they do not mention its creation at all, except for the saying of David: 'The Lord established His throne in the heavens' (Psalm 102:19). This saying is very open to interpretation. As for its perpetuity, it is stated: 'You, Lord, sit on Your throne forever, from generation to generation' (Lamentations 5:19). If Rabbi Eliezer believes in the eternity of the throne, then it would be an attribute of God, not a created body. So how can something be formed from an attribute? And the most astonishing thing is his saying: 'the light of his robe'. Then Maimonides says: It is derived from the words of Rabbi Eliezer that the substance of heaven is different from the substance of earth. The substance of heaven is from the light of the robe, and the substance of earth is from the snow that is under the throne of glory. Therefore he showed them 'the transparent' to indicate the substance of earth.
Source pages p. 304

Commentary
This passage is a critique of Rabbi Eliezer's views on the throne of glory, as presented by Imam Razi. The author begins by questioning the origin of the snow under the throne and the throne itself. He argues that if Rabbi Eliezer considers the light of God's robe and the throne as uncreated, then he is affirming the eternity of the world, which aligns with Plato's doctrine. However, the author notes that the sages consider the throne as created, before the world, citing Talmud Nedarim 39b. He then examines scriptural evidence: David's verse about God establishing His throne is ambiguous, while the verse on perpetuity (Lamentations 5:19) seems to support eternity. If Rabbi Eliezer holds the throne as eternal, it becomes an attribute of God, not a created body, leading to an absurdity: how can a material thing be formed from an attribute? The author finds Rabbi Eliezer's statement about 'the light of His robe' particularly astonishing. Then, referring to Maimonides, he explains that Rabbi Eliezer's view implies two different substances: heaven from the light of the robe, earth from the snow under the throne. Maimonides claims that the 'transparent' substance shown was meant to indicate the substance of earth. The passage highlights the theological confusion arising from literal interpretations of anthropomorphic descriptions.

Key Terms

نور الرداء
قدم العالم
صفة الله

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The throne of glory is a physical object that can be created or uncreated like other things.
Correction: The passage shows that treating the throne as a literal entity leads to absurdities; divine attributes are not material and cannot generate bodies.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what problem arises if Rabbi Eliezer believes the throne is eternal?
A) It would mean the throne is a created body.
B) It would make the throne an attribute of God, leading to absurdity of forming a body from an attribute.
C) It would contradict the Bible.
D) It would affirm the eternity of the world in a correct way.

Key Takeaway
Imam Razi's critique demonstrates that literalism about divine attributes leads to logical contradictions, reinforcing the necessity of transcendent interpretation in tawhid.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

The Language of Humans and Scripture
Critiques literal and metaphorical interpretations of 'heart' in Torah, proposing 'language of humans' principle.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the principle that scripture speaks in human language and how it mediates between literal and metaphorical interpretations of anthropomorphic expressions.

Core Idea
Scripture speaks in human language — the key to avoiding both literalism and forced metaphor.

When scripture attributes a 'heart' to God, should we take it literally or metaphorically? Two great scholars, Jonathan ben Uzziel and Maimonides, chose opposite sides. But the author of this passage argues that both missed the mark. The real principle is simpler and more profound: the Torah speaks in the language of human beings. This lesson unpacks that principle and shows why it is the highest correctness.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then the translation. Notice how the author first gives the metaphorical meaning, then contrasts the two interpreters.
The Arabic may have OCR errors but the meaning is clear.
2
Identify the two positions
Identify what Jonathan ben Uzziel and Maimonides each said about 'heart' in Judges 16:10. What was their mistake according to the author?
One took it literally, the other metaphorically.
3
Understand the principle
The author says if they had simply said 'the Torah speaks in the language of the sons of Adam' and refrained from explanation, they would be correct. What does this principle mean?
It means scripture uses ordinary human language, not technical philosophical terms.
4
Apply to other verses
Think of other verses where God is described with human organs. How would this principle guide interpretation?
5
Reflect on the lesson
Why is this principle considered the highest correctness? How does it protect divine transcendence while honoring scripture?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مجازاً عن إرادته . مثل «فرف قلبه المشقة إسرائيل، (قض ١٦:١٠) أي كفت إرادته عن إشقاء بني إسرائيل . و د يوناثان بن عزيائيل » . ، أخذ « فرف قلبه ، على المعنى الحقيقى ، وامتنع من شرحه ، وابن ميمون أخذه على المعنى المجازى كناية عن الإرادة . ولو أنهما قالا : إن التوراة عبرت عن بلسان بني آدم ، وكفا عن الشرح بالحقيقة أو بالمجاز ، لكانا على درجة قصوى من الصواب
Metaphorically for His will. For example: 'His heart was distressed for Israel' (Judges 16:10), meaning His will ceased from afflicting the Children of Israel. And Jonathan ben Uzziel took 'his heart was distressed' in the literal sense and refrained from explaining it, while Maimonides took it metaphorically as a metonymy for the will. If they both had said: The Torah speaks in the language of the sons of Adam, and refrained from explaining it as literal or metaphorical, they would have been at the highest degree of correctness.
Source pages p. 305

Commentary
This passage critiques two interpretive approaches to anthropomorphic expressions in scripture, using the example of 'heart' (قلب) in Judges 16:10. The author first provides the metaphorical meaning: 'his heart was distressed' means 'his will ceased.' He then contrasts Jonathan ben Uzziel, who insisted on a literal interpretation and refused to explain, with Maimonides, who interpreted it metaphorically as a metonymy for will. The author's key point is that both are mistaken because they fail to recognize that scripture speaks in human language (بلسان بني آدم). The correct approach is to acknowledge this linguistic principle without forcing either a literal or metaphorical reading—this middle path is the highest correctness.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The debate is about whether to interpret anthropomorphic phrases literally or metaphorically.
Correction: The author argues that both literal and metaphorical interpretations miss the real principle: scripture speaks in human language. The correct approach is to acknowledge this without forcing either literal or metaphorical meaning.

D) They both used philosophical categories.

Key Takeaway
The highest correctness is to say scripture speaks in human language, without insisting on literal or metaphorical interpretation of anthropomorphic expressions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Is there a verse you've been trying to 'solve' with a specific interpretation? Could you lay that insistence aside and trust the text's own claim about its language?
?
How often have you felt pressure to take a definitive stance on ambiguous scriptural passages—either literal or metaphorical—out of fear of being seen as weak or unorthodox? What would change in your heart if you adopted the principle of 'language of humans' as your default posture toward difficult verses?

Metaphorical Meanings of 'Life' in Scripture
Lists literal meaning of 'life' and its metaphorical uses for recovery, wisdom, and good.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the literal and metaphorical meanings of 'life' in scripture and why applying the literal meaning to God is problematic.

Core Idea
Life cannot refer to a physical body when describing God; it is a metaphor for wisdom and goodness.

Imam Razi begins by clarifying the literal sense of 'life' as applied to physical beings. He then shows how scripture often uses 'life' and 'death' metaphorically to convey spiritual and moral states. By understanding these metaphors, we see that God's attribute of being 'the Living' points to His perfect knowledge and goodness, not to a bodily existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the translation. Notice the author's classification style.
The passage uses numbered items (1 and 2) to organize the metaphorical uses.
2
Identify the literal definition
Find the literal meaning of 'life' (حى) as a 'growing, sensitive being'. This applies to physical organisms.
This definition is crucial to see why it cannot apply to God.
3
Examine the first metaphor
Look at the first metaphorical use: severe illness is called 'death', recovery is called 'life'. Note the scriptural examples (Psalms, Isaiah).
The author uses these examples to show how common this metaphor is.
4
Examine the second metaphor
Look at the second metaphorical use: wisdom is called 'life' (Proverbs 8). The author extends this to equate correct opinions with life and corrupt opinions with death (Deuteronomy 30:15).
This shows that 'life' can refer to intellectual and moral states.
5
Reflect on the purpose
Ask yourself: Why does the author emphasize these metaphors? Connect to the overall goal of showing that God's attribute 'the Living' must be understood metaphorically.
This lesson is part of a larger discussion on divine transcendence.

الله حى : على الحقيقة اسم للنامى الحساس. وعلى المجاز يأتي بمعان منها : ۱ - لو مرض إنسان مرضاً شديداً. يعبر عن المرض الشديد بالموت مجازاً. مثل : د فمات قلبه في جوفه وصار كحجر، اصم ٢٥ : ٢٧) أي مرض مرضاً شديداً . ولو أن إنساناً برء من مرضه ، يعبر عن البرء بالحياة مجازاً مثل : « وأفاق من مرضه ، ( أش ۳۸ : ۹) ۲ - اقتناء العلم والحكمة مثل : قول الحكمة في سفر الأمثال : فالآن أيها البنون اسمعوا لي . فطوبى الذين يحفظون طرقى ، اسمعوا التعليم وكونوا حكماء ولا ترفضوه . طوبى الانسان الذي لى ساهراً كل يوم عند مصاريعي ، حافظاً قوائم أبوانى . لأنه من يجدنى بجد الحياة. وينال رضى من الرب ، ومن يخطى. عنى يضر نفسه . كل مبغضى يحبون الموت ، ( أم ۸ : ٣٣-٢٦) أي أن الحكمة حياة للذين يصادفونها . ولأن الحكمة حياة مجازاً ، يعبر عن الآراء الصحيحة بالحياة وعن الآراء الفاسدة بالموت ، مثل : « انظر : إني جعلت اليوم بين يديك الحياة والخير ، والموت والشر ، ( تث ٣٠ : ١٥) فقد صرح بأن الخير هو الحياة ، وبأن الشر هو الموت .
God is living: literally, it is a name for the growing, sensitive being. And metaphorically, it comes with meanings including: 1) If a person is severely ill, they express severe illness metaphorically as death. For example: 'His heart died within him and became like a stone' (Psalm 25:27?) meaning he fell severely ill. And if a person recovers from his illness, they express recovery metaphorically as life, like: 'He recovered from his illness' (Isaiah 38:9). 2) Acquisition of knowledge and wisdom, like the saying of wisdom in Proverbs: 'Now therefore, O sons, listen to me. Blessed are those who keep my ways. Hear instruction and be wise, and do not refuse it. Blessed is the man who watches daily at my gates, guarding the posts of my doors. For whoever finds me finds life and obtains favor from the Lord; and whoever sins against me injures himself; all who hate me love death' (Proverbs 8:32-36). That is, wisdom is life for those who meet it. And because wisdom is life metaphorically, correct opinions are expressed as life and corrupt opinions as death, like: 'See, I have set before you today life and good, death and evil' (Deuteronomy 30:15). He clearly states that good is life and evil is death.
Source pages p. 305

Commentary
This passage defines the literal meaning of 'life' (حى) as a growing, sensitive entity (the standard definition for living beings). Then it lists two metaphorical uses: first, describing severe illness as 'death' and recovery as 'life', citing Psalm 25:27? and Isaiah 38:9. Second, acquiring wisdom is metaphorically called 'life', with a lengthy quotation from Proverbs 8:32-36 emphasizing that finding wisdom finds life. From this, the author extrapolates that correct opinions are called 'life' and corrupt opinions 'death', citing Deuteronomy 30:15 where good is equated with life and evil with death. The purpose is to show that when God is called 'the Living', it cannot be taken literally (which would imply a physical body), but must be understood metaphorically as enduring, wise, or bestowing life.

Literal vs. Metaphorical Meaning of 'Wing'
Defines 'wing' literally and metaphorically as covering/shelter.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between the literal and metaphorical meanings of the term 'wing' (جناح) in scripture and why this distinction is essential for preserving divine transcendence.

Core Idea
God's 'wings' are a metaphor for His sheltering care, not a physical description.

Imam Razi clarifies a key principle for interpreting metaphorical language in scripture. Before we can understand verses that speak of God's 'wings,' we need to know what the word means literally and how it is used figuratively. This short passage establishes that foundation, showing that 'wing' metaphorically means covering or shelter.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the translation. Let the structure of the definition come through.
The passage has three clear parts: literal meaning, metaphorical meaning, and an example.
2
Identify the two definitions
Notice that the passage first gives the literal meaning (the wing of a flying animal) and then the metaphorical meaning (covering and shelter). Distinguish between them.
3
Examine the biblical example
The passage quotes Ruth 2:12. Understand how the metaphor works: taking refuge under God's wings means seeking shelter under His protection.
This example is used to show that scriptural language about God's wings is metaphorical.
4
Reflect on the theological implication
Ask yourself why this distinction matters. How does it protect the belief that God is not like created things? This connects to the principle of divine transcendence.
This lesson guards against anthropomorphism.

Wing: literally, it is the name of the wing of a flying animal. And metaphorically, it is used in the sense of covering and sheltering. And 'whom you have come to take refuge under His wings' (Ruth 2:12) means: to seek shelter under His covering.

The example is from Ruth 2:12, where taking refuge under God's wings means seeking shelter under His protection. This illustrates that when scripture attributes 'wings' to God, it is metaphorical, not literal, and points to His protective care.

Metaphorical Meanings of 'Eye' in Scripture
Lists literal 'eye' and metaphorical uses: spring, care, and mental perception.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that when scripture attributes eyes to God, it must be interpreted metaphorically as care, will, or intellectual perception, not as a physical organ.

Core Idea
Divine 'eyes' mean care and omniscience, not literal sight.

One of the most common anthropomorphic expressions in scripture is God's 'eyes.' Does God have physical eyes? This passage from Imam Razi shows that the word 'eye' has several metaphorical meanings — water, care, and intellectual perception — and that when applied to God, it never means a physical organ. Let's examine the three categories.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice how the author defines the literal meaning first, then lists the metaphorical uses.
The literal meaning is the animal eye, but that never applies to God.
2
Identify the three metaphorical uses
The passage lists three metaphorical uses of 'eye': spring (عين ماء), care (عناية), and intellectual perception (إدراك عقلي). Find each one and note the biblical examples given.
The third use requires the presence of 'seeing' (رؤية) with 'eyes'.
3
Apply the metaphors to God
Understand that when the Bible says God's eyes are upon someone, it means His care. When it says 'open your eyes and see,' it means intellectual perception (omniscience).
This guards against literalism.
4
Reflect on the principle
The key principle: divine attributes are metaphorical. Physical descriptions of God are interpreted in a way befitting His majesty.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
عين : على الحقيقة اسم لعين الحيوان . وعلى المجاز يستعمل فى ١ - عين الماء . مثل : « على عين ماء فى البرية » ( تك ٧:١٦ ) ۲ - العناية . مثل : و خذه واجعل عينيك عليه . ( إر ۳۹ : ۱۲ ) أي اجعل عنايتك به . وعلى هذا المعنى المجازى يفسر ماجاء عن الله في التوراة ، وهو د وستكون عيناي وقلى هناك كل الأيام ، (۱)مل ۹ : ٣ يفسر بعنايته وإرادته ٣ - وإذا اقترن بالعينين لفظ رؤية مثل : افتح عينيك وانظر ( ٣مل ۱۹ : ١٦ ) فمعناه : الإدراك العقلى ، وليس الإدراك الحسى
Eye: literally, it is the name of the eye of an animal. And metaphorically, it is used in: 1) An eye of water (spring). For example: 'by an eye of water in the wilderness' (Genesis 7:16?). 2) Care. For example: 'Take him and set your eyes upon him' (Jeremiah 39:12), meaning make your care upon him. And according to this metaphorical meaning is interpreted what is said about God in the Torah: 'My eyes and my heart will be there all the days' (1 Kings 9:3) – it is interpreted as His care and will. 3) If the word 'seeing' is associated with the eyes, like 'Open your eyes and see' (2 Kings 19:16), its meaning is intellectual perception, not sensory perception.
Source pages p. 306

Commentary
This passage lists the literal meaning of 'eye' (عين) as the animal eye. Then it gives three metaphorical uses: first, a spring of water (عين ماء) as in Genesis 7:16 (though the verse likely refers to a wellspring). Second, care or attention (عناية) as in Jeremiah 39:12, where 'set your eyes upon him' means 'give him your care.' The author applies this to divine attributes: when the Torah says 'My eyes and my heart will be there' (1 Kings 9:3), it is interpreted metaphorically as God's care and will. Third, if 'seeing' (رؤية) is paired with 'eyes', as in 'Open your eyes and see' (2 Kings 19:16), it means intellectual perception (الإدراك العقلي), not sensory sight. This shows that divine 'eyes' are never literal but refer to care or omniscience.

Key Terms
عين
عناية
إدراك عقلى
مجاز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That 'eye of God' refers to a literal physical eye.
Correction: The passage shows that when Scripture attributes eyes to God, it always means His care or intellectual perception, never a physical organ.

Checkpoint

A) Literally as physical eyes
B) As a spring of water
C) As care and will
D) As intellectual perception

Key Takeaway
When scripture attributes eyes to God, it always means His care or omniscience, never a physical organ.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read that God's eyes are upon you, do you truly feel His care and omniscience, or do you still imagine a physical watcher? How will you train your heart to feel His presence without physical imagery?
?

?

Metaphorical Meanings of 'Hearing': Acceptance and Knowledge
Explains literal and metaphorical meanings of 'hearing' with divine application.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that when applied to God, the term 'hearing' is never literal but denotes either omniscience (knowledge) or response (answering).

Core Idea
God's 'hearing' means acceptance or knowledge, not soundwaves.

This passage from Imam Razi's Asas al-Taqdis explores the linguistic range of the word 'hearing' (samā‘). In Arabic, it can mean auditory perception, but also acceptance and knowledge. When the Torah ascribes hearing to God, it must be understood metaphorically—either as His knowledge of people's intentions or as His answering their prayers. This protects divine transcendence from anthropomorphism.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without overanalyzing. Then read the literal translation to grasp the literal and metaphorical senses.
Notice the two metaphorical meanings listed: acceptance (qabūl) and knowledge (ʿilm).
2
Identify the two metaphorical senses
The passage first defines the literal meaning, then lists two metaphorical uses: acceptance and knowledge. Find the examples for each.
The first example is from Exodus: 'They did not listen to Moses' – here 'listen' means accept.
3
Understand divine hearing
The passage then explains two types of divine 'hearing' from the Torah: one where God knows people's intentions, and another where He answers prayer. Identify which phrase corresponds to which meaning.
The author distinguishes between 'the hearing of the Lord' (knowledge) and 'I will hear her cry' (answering).
4
Reflect on the theological rationale
Ask yourself: why does the author bother to explain this? What wrong belief does this protect against?
This guards against anthropomorphism (tashbīh) by affirming that God does not have physical ears.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Hearing: literally, it means hearing with the ear. And metaphorically, it comes in the sense of acceptance. For example: 'They did not listen to Moses' (Exodus 6:9). And it comes in the sense of knowledge and cognition. For example: 'A nation whose language you do not understand' (Deuteronomy 28:49). Commentary
This passage explains the literal meaning of 'hearing' (سماع) as auditory perception. It then lists two metaphorical meanings: first, acceptance (قبول), as in 'they did not listen to Moses' meaning they did not accept his message. Second, knowledge and cognition (العلم والمعرفة), as in 'a nation whose language you do not understand' which implies not knowing. When applied to God, the author distinguishes two types of mentions: if the phrase is like 'the hearing of the Lord' (Numbers 11:1), it means God knows their intention intellectually; if it is like 'I will hear her cry' (Exodus 22:23), it means He answers their prayer. Thus, divine 'hearing' is never literal but denotes either omniscience or response.

Learning Objective

Core Idea
God's 'riding' means absolute control, not literal transportation.

This lesson explores a key example of metaphorical interpretation in divine attributes. The author shows that when scripture speaks of God 'riding', it describes His absolute sovereignty over creation, not a physical act. This passage also introduces Hebrew terms that reinforce the same meaning.

Guided Path
1

The passage is incomplete but still forms a coherent unit.

This refutes anthropomorphism.
4
Understand the Hebrew terms
The terms 'al-bazari' and 'al-barari' are explained as referring to the highest sphere. The parallelism between 'Rider of al-barari' and 'Rider of the heavens' shows they are synonymous.

The overall purpose is to show that attributing 'riding' to God is metaphorical for His absolute dominion over the universe.

Literal and Metaphorical Meanings of 'Ayn (Eye)
Defines 'ayn and enumerates three metaphorical usages.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the literal meaning of the term 'ayn and its three metaphorical usages in scripture, especially as applied to God.

Core Idea
Bodily terms for God are metaphorical, not literal.

When scripture attributes an 'eye' to God, it does not mean a physical organ. This lesson unpacks the Arabic term 'ayn and shows how scholars interpret such passages metaphorically to protect divine transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its literal translation to grasp the structure.
2
Identify the three metaphorical meanings
The passage lists three metaphorical uses: water source, care, and intellectual perception. Note each example.
3
Apply to divine attributes
Understand how this applies to God: 'My eyes' means His care and will, not physical sight.
This is crucial for tawhid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
عين : على الحقيقة اسم لعين الحيوان . وعلى المجاز يستعمل فى ١ - عين الماء . مثل : « على عين ماء فى البرية » ( تك ٧:١٦ ) ۲ - العناية . مثل : و خذه واجعل عينيك عليه . ( إر ۳۹ : ۱۲ ) أي اجعل عنايتك به . وعلى هذا المعنى المجازى يفسر ماجاء عن الله في التوراة ، وهو وستكون عيناي وقلى هناك كل الأيام ، (۱)مل ۹ : ٣ يفسر بعنايته وإرادته ٣ - وإذا اقترن بالعينين لفظ رؤية مثل : افتح عينيك وانظر ( ٣مل ۱۹ : ١٦ ) فمعناه : الإدراك العقلى ، وليس الإدراك الحسى
'Ayn (eye): In the literal sense, it is a name for the eye of the animal. In the metaphorical sense, it is used for: 1. The source of water, like 'upon a spring of water in the wilderness' (Genesis 7:16). 2. Care, like 'Take him and set your eyes upon him' (Jeremiah 39:12), meaning 'make your care for him.' On this metaphorical meaning is interpreted what came from God in the Torah, namely 'My eyes and my heart will be there all the days' (1 Kings 9:3), interpreted as His care and will. 3. If the word 'seeing' accompanies the two eyes, like 'Open your eyes and see' (2 Kings 19:16), then its meaning is intellectual perception, not sensory perception.
Source pages p. 306

Commentary
This passage defines the word 'ayn (eye) and its usages. It is a linguistic and exegetical analysis. The author begins by stating the literal meaning: the eye of an animal. Then he lists three metaphorical usages, each with biblical examples. First, it can mean the source of water, as in 'a spring of water.' Second, it can mean care or attention, as in setting one's eyes on someone. He explains that when the Torah attributes eyes to God (e.g., 'My eyes will be there'), it is metaphorical, referring to His care and will. Third, when the phrase 'opening the eyes' is used with a verb of seeing, it metaphorically means intellectual perception, not physical sight. The author thus establishes that bodily terms in scripture applied to God must be interpreted metaphorically to uphold divine transcendence.

الحقيقة (ḥaqīqah)
المجاز (majāz)
العناية (al-ʿināyah)
الإدراك العقلى (al-idrāk al-ʿaqlī)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Attributing an eye to God means He has a physical organ.
Correction: The passage clarifies that such language is metaphorical, signifying care or intellectual perception.

Checkpoint

B) God's care and will
C) God's intellectual perception

Key Takeaway
When scripture attributes an 'eye' to God, it must be understood metaphorically as His care or intellectual perception, never as a physical organ.

Literal and Metaphorical Meanings of Sama' (Hearing)
Defines sama' and explains its metaphorical senses in the Torah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the term 'sama'' (hearing) is used both literally and metaphorically, and how this applies to verses about God's hearing to preserve divine transcendence.

Core Idea
God's hearing is not auditory; it is metaphorical – either knowledge or response.

Sanusi clarifies a key term in discussions of divine attributes: sama' (hearing). He first defines its literal meaning, then shows how it is used metaphorically in scripture. By examining verses from the Torah, he demonstrates that when God is described as 'hearing', it means either that He knows our intent or that He answers our prayer – never that He has physical ears.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the literal definition
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Note that the first sentence gives the literal meaning of sama' as hearing with the ear.
2
Note the two metaphorical meanings
The passage lists two metaphorical uses: acceptance (qabūl) and knowledge/recognition (ʿilm/maʿrifah). See the examples from the Torah.
3
Apply to divine hearing
The author applies these meanings to verses about God's hearing. Distinguish between 'hearing of the Lord' (knowledge) and 'I hear her cry' (response).
4
Reflect on divine transcendence
Consider why this metaphorical interpretation is necessary: to avoid attributing physical senses to Allah, preserving His transcendence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
سماع : على الحقيقة بمعنى السمع من الأذن . وعلى المجاز يأتي بمعنى القبول . مثل : فلم يسمعوا لموسى ، (خر ٦ :٩) ويأتي بمعنى العلم والمعرفة . مثل : « أمة لا تفهم لغتها ، ( تث ٢٨ : ٤٩ ) وما جاء في التوراة عن سماع الله الكلام الناس إن كان مثل سمع الرب، عد ۱:۱۱) فمعناه : إدرك بالعلم مرادهم ، وإن كان مثل « فإني أسمع ص أخها ، (خر ۲۳ : ۲۲) فمعناه : أجاب دعاء هما
Samaʿ (hearing): In the literal sense, it means hearing with the ear. In the metaphorical sense, it comes with the meaning of acceptance, like 'they did not listen to Moses' (Exodus 6:9). And it comes with the meaning of knowledge and cognition, like 'a nation whose language you do not understand' (Deuteronomy 28:49). And what came in the Torah concerning God's hearing of people's speech: if it is like 'the hearing of the Lord' (Numbers 11:1), then its meaning is He understood their intent through knowledge; if it is like 'I hear her cry' (Exodus 22:23), then its meaning is He answered their prayer.
Source pages p. 306

Commentary
This passage defines the word samaʿ (hearing) in both literal and metaphorical contexts. It begins with the literal meaning: hearing with the ear. Then it explains that metaphorically it can mean acceptance, as in refusing to listen to Moses (i.e., not accepting his message). It can also mean knowledge, as in not understanding a foreign language (i.e., not knowing it). The author then applies this logic to verses about God's hearing in the Torah. He distinguishes two types: if the verse speaks of 'the hearing of the Lord', it means God knew their intent through His knowledge; if the verse speaks of God hearing a cry, it means He answered their prayer. In both cases, God's hearing is metaphorical—either knowledge or response—not physical hearing with ears. This reinforces the doctrine of divine transcendence.

Correction: The passage shows that God's hearing is metaphorical: it either means He knows our intent or He answers our prayer, never literal hearing.

Literal and Metaphorical Meaning of Rukb (Riding)

Core Idea
Riding metaphorically means dominating, not mounting.

Imam Razi begins by distinguishing the literal and metaphorical uses of the word 'rukb' (riding). While the literal sense involves a human riding an animal, the metaphorical sense conveys domination and control. This distinction is crucial for interpreting biblical and Qur'anic descriptions of God, such as 'Rider of the heavens,' which must be understood as God's dominion over the heavens, not a physical riding.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully

This is a central point for divine transcendence—avoiding anthropomorphism.
