Rukb (riding): In the literal sense, it is used for a human riding an animal. In the metaphorical sense, it is used for domination over a thing, because the rider dominates and rules his mount. For example, 'He made him ride on the high places of the earth' (Deuteronomy 32:13). On this metaphorical meaning it is said concerning God Almighty: 'Rider of the heavens for your help' (Deuteronomy 33:26), meaning He dominates the heavens. And the heaven is expressed in the Torah as 'al-barārī.' And 'al-barārī' for them is the highest sphere that encompasses everything. That is because the heavens are seven, and 'al-barārī' is the highest that encompasses everything. The proof that 'al-barārī' are the heavens is that in one place it is written 'Rider of the barārī' (Psalm 68:5), and in another place it is written 'Rider of the heavens' (Haggai 2:12b? [questionable]). And it has reached me: that the first heaven in the speech of the Jews is called 'Fīlūn,' the second 'Raqīʿ,' the third 'Sheḥāqīm,' the fourth 'Zebūl,' the fifth 'Maʿōn,' the sixth 'Mākōn,' and the seventh 'ʿArāfāt.' – And in Tafsīr al-Qurṭubī, the first is named 'Raqīʿ.' – And it reached me that Ibn Maymūn (Maimonides) expressed the seventh heaven as 'ʿArāfāt.' And the Arabic copy I have of Dalālat al-Ḥāʾirīn (Guide for the Perplexed) states that he meant by 'al-barārī' the highest sphere encompassing everything, not that he meant Mount ʿArafāt in the land of Mecca the Honored. And someone else understood – as told to me – that he meant Mount ʿArafāt, because in some manuscripts of Dalālat al-Ḥāʾirīn: 'al-rawkab baʿar būt' instead of 'al-rākib fī al-barārī' (Psalm 68:5) or 'lilrākib fī al-qifār' or 'al-marākib ʿalā al-ghamām.'
Source pages p. 306

Commentary
This passage defines the word rukb (riding) and its metaphorical application to God. It begins with the literal meaning: riding an animal. Then it explains the metaphorical sense of domination, since a rider controls his mount. The author cites Deuteronomy 32:13 as a metaphorical example. He then applies this to the verse 'Rider of the heavens for your help' (Deuteronomy 33:26), interpreting it to mean that God dominates the heavens. Next, he explains that 'al-barārī' (wilderness) in Hebrew refers to the highest heaven. He asserts that there are seven heavens, and al-barārī is the highest. To prove that al-barārī denotes the heavens, he compares Psalm 68:5 ('Rider of the barārī') with Haggai 2:12 (or similar, 'Rider of the heavens') as synonymous. The author then details the Jewish names for the seven heavens, referencing Qurṭubī's tafsīr and Maimonides. He clarifies that Maimonides, in the Guide for the Perplexed, meant the highest sphere, not Mount ʿArafāt. He mentions a variant manuscript reading that led to a misunderstanding. This entire discussion reinforces the necessity of interpreting anthropomorphic expressions metaphorically.

Key Terms
ركب (rukb)
الاستيلاء (al-istīlāʾ)
راكب السماء (rākib al-samāʾ)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Believing that 'Rider of the heavens' describes God physically riding on the heavens.
Correction: The passage explains that this is metaphorical, meaning God dominates the heavens (mustawlī ʿalā al-samāʾ).

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the metaphorical meaning of 'rukb' (riding)?
A) Riding an animal
B) Domination over something
C) Sitting on a throne
D) Moving quickly

Key Takeaway
The metaphorical meaning of 'riding' as domination allows us to interpret divine descriptions like 'Rider of the heavens' without anthropomorphism.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read descriptions of God in Scripture that sound physical, like 'Rider of the heavens', what is your immediate impulse: to take it literally or to seek a deeper meaning? How does this lesson challenge your default response?
?
The author reasons that 'riding' means domination because a rider controls his mount. Is there an area of your life where you claim God's authority but resist His control? What would it mean for you to let God truly 'ride'—to dominate—that area?
?
If you were to explain to someone that God does not literally ride the heavens, what evidence from this passage would you use? Are you prepared to defend this interpretation with the same logical reasoning the author provides?

intermediate
6 min
Barari as the Highest Heaven and Clarification of Maimonides
Identifies 'barari' as highest heaven, lists seven heavens, corrects misinterpretation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that 'al-barārī' in the Torah refers to the highest heaven, and you will learn the Jewish names of the seven heavens and the correct interpretation of Maimonides.

Core Idea
Barari is the highest sphere, not Mount Arafat.

In this passage, Imam Razi explains that the term 'al-barārī' (wilderness) used in the Torah actually refers to the highest heaven that encompasses all. He provides evidence by comparing verses, lists the Jewish names for the seven heavens, and addresses a common misinterpretation of Maimonides' words. This lesson clarifies an important textual point for understanding biblical references to God's 'riding' and underscores the metaphorical interpretation of such expressions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on barari
Pay attention to how the author identifies 'barari' with the highest heaven and the proof he gives from scripture.
2
Learn the seven heavens names
The passage lists the Hebrew names for the seven heavens. Write them down to remember.
Compare with the note from Tafsir al-Qurtubi about the first heaven.
3
Understand the Maimonides clarification
Notice that the author clarifies that Maimonides meant the celestial sphere, not Mount Arafat, and that a variant manuscript led to confusion.
This shows the importance of textual accuracy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ركب : على الحقيقة يستعمل في ركوب الإنسان على البهائم . وعلى المجاز يستعمل للاستيلاء على الشيء ، لأن الراكب مستولى وحاكم على مركوبه ، مثل : اركبه على هضاب الأرض ، ( تث ۳۲ : ۱۳ ) وعلى هذا المعنى المجازى قيل فى الله تعالى : ( راكب السماء لنصرتك ، ( تث ٣٣ : (٢٦) ومعناه : أنه مستولى على السماء . ويعبر عن السماء في التوراة بالبراري. والبراري عندهم هو الفلك الأعلى المحيط بالكل . ذلك لأن السموات سبعة ، والبراري هو الأعلى المحيط بالكل . والدليل على أن البراري هي السموات : أنه فى موضع مكتوب راكب البراري ، مز ٦٨ : ٥) وفى موضع مكتوب : « راكب السماوات ، ( حجيجه ۱۲ ب ) وقد بلغني: أن السماء الأولى فى كلام اليهود اسمها فيلون ، والثانية رقيع ، والثالثة شحاقيم ، والرابعة زبول ، والخامسة ماعون ، والسادسة ماكون ، والسابعة عرفات - وفى تفسير القرطبي أن الأولى اسمها رقيع - وبلغنى أن ابن ميمون عبر عن السماء السابعة بعرفات . والنسخة العربية التي معى من دلاله الحائرين فيها أنه يقصد بالبرارى الفلك الأعلى المحيط بالكل، لا أنه يقصد جبل عرفات فى أرض مكة المكرمة ، وقد فهم غيرى - كما قال من بلغنى - أنه يقصد جبل عرفات، لأن في بعض مخطوطات دلالة الحائرين : « الروكب بعر بوت ، بدل « الراكب في البراري ، ( مز ۶۸ از ۸ : (٥) أو للراكب فى القفار ، أو « المراكب على الغمام .
Rukb (riding): In the literal sense, it is used for a human riding an animal. In the metaphorical sense, it is used for domination over a thing, because the rider dominates and rules his mount. For example, 'He made him ride on the high places of the earth' (Deuteronomy 32:13). On this metaphorical meaning it is said concerning God Almighty: 'Rider of the heavens for your help' (Deuteronomy 33:26), meaning He dominates the heavens. And the heaven is expressed in the Torah as 'al-barārī.' And 'al-barārī' for them is the highest sphere that encompasses everything. That is because the heavens are seven, and 'al-barārī' is the highest that encompasses everything. The proof that 'al-barārī' are the heavens is that in one place it is written 'Rider of the barārī' (Psalm 68:5), and in another place it is written 'Rider of the heavens' (Haggai 2:12b? [questionable]). And it has reached me: that the first heaven in the speech of the Jews is called 'Fīlūn,' the second 'Raqīʿ,' the third 'Sheḥāqīm,' the fourth 'Zebūl,' the fifth 'Maʿōn,' the sixth 'Mākōn,' and the seventh 'ʿArāfāt.' – And in Tafsīr al-Qurṭubī, the first is named 'Raqīʿ.' – And it reached me that Ibn Maymūn (Maimonides) expressed the seventh heaven as 'ʿArāfāt.' And the Arabic copy I have of Dalālat al-Ḥāʾirīn (Guide for the Perplexed) states that he meant by 'al-barārī' the highest sphere encompassing everything, not that he meant Mount ʿArafāt in the land of Mecca the Honored. And someone else understood – as told to me – that he meant Mount ʿArafāt, because in some manuscripts of Dalālat al-Ḥāʾirīn: 'al-rawkab baʿar būt' instead of 'al-rākib fī al-barārī' (Psalm 68:5) or 'lilrākib fī al-qifār' or 'al-marākib ʿalā al-ghamām.'

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does the term 'al-barārī' refer to in the Torah?
A) A desert wilderness
B) Mount Arafat

Key Takeaway
The term 'barārī' in the Torah refers to the highest heaven, and Maimonides' usage was astronomical, not geographical.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever assumed you understood a term in scripture based on a casual reading, only to discover a deeper meaning later? How does this lesson challenge you to be more careful in your study of divine texts?
?
When a misunderstanding of Maimonides' words could lead to a serious error about the heavens, what steps can you take to verify the authenticity and interpretation of religious sources before accepting a claim?
?
This lesson shows that even a single word ('barārī') can be misunderstood if taken out of context. What assumptions about your own understanding of religious terms are you willing to reconsider today, and how will you actively seek clarification?
